Refutation of Mu'tazila on Obligation's Beneficence
Razi refutes Mu'tazila on obligation's goodness requiring recompense

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's three-part refutation of the Mu'tazila's claim that obligation's goodness requires recompense.

Core Idea
God's obligation is a gratuitous favor, not a transaction requiring recompense.

This passage examines the Mu'tazila's argument that obligation is only good because it leads to recompense that makes the servant deserve glorification from God. Imam Razi dismantles this by first rejecting the foundation of rational good and evil, then showing internal inconsistencies even within their own framework.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Mu'tazila claim
Read the first sentence of the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the Mu'tazila's argument: the goodness of obligation is due to recompense for deserving glorification.
Notice that they assert gratuitous favor without prior deserving is evil.
2
Identify Razi's first refutation
Razi says the argument is false because it is based on rational good/evil and obligation upon God, which Ash'aris reject. This attacks the foundation.
Even if we accept good/evil, Razi continues.
3
Second refutation: gratuitous favor is not evil
Even within their framework, gratuitous favor cannot be evil for God because He is beyond harm and benefit.
This refutes the core premise.
4
Third refutation: counterexample with shahada
Razi provides a case where easy acts (shahada) earn greater reward than difficult ones, proving that deserving glorification does not require difficulty. Thus the Mu'tazila's reasoning is inconsistent.
If difficulty were necessary, God would increase strength and obligate easy acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : قالت المعتزلة علة حسن التكليف التعويض لاستحقاق التعظيم فإن التفضل بالتعظيم قبيح وهذا عندنا باطل لأنه بناء على الحسن والقبح والوجوب على الله تعالى وبعد تسليمه فلا نسلم أن التفضل بالتعظيم قبيح محال يستحيل عليه النفع والضر، وبتقدير تسليمه فاستحقاق التعظيم لا يتوقف على التكليف بالأفعال الشاقة بدليل أن التلفظ بكلمة الشهادة أسهل من الجهاد والصوم مع أن المستحق به أعظم فلو كان المقصود استحقاق التعظيم لمكان من الواجب أن يزيد الله تعالى في قوتنا ثم يكلفنا بما لا يشق عليها ليحصل الاستحقاق من غير المشقة
Issue: The Mu'tazila said: The cause of the goodness of obligation is recompense for deserving glorification, for gratuitous favor through glorification is evil. This is false in our view because it is based on good and evil and obligation upon God. And even if we grant that, we do not accept that gratuitous favor through glorification is evil, as harm and benefit are impossible for Him. And if we grant that, then deserving glorification does not depend on obligation with difficult acts, as evidenced by the fact that uttering the word of testimony is easier than jihad and fasting, yet what is deserved by it is greater. So if the goal were to deserve glorification, it would be necessary for God to increase our strength and then obligate us with what does not burden us, so that deserving occurs without hardship.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila's argument regarding the reason for the goodness of obligation (taklif). The Mu'tazila claim that the only reason obligation is good is because it leads to recompense (ta'wid) that makes the servant deserve glorification (ta'zim) from God, and that God doing a favor without prior deserving is evil. Imam Razi responds by noting that this argument is built on the Mu'tazila's own principles of rational good and evil and that God is under obligation, which the Ash'aris reject. Even if we accept those principles, Razi argues that gratuitous favor is not evil for God because He is beyond harm and benefit. Furthermore, even if we accept that gratuitous favor is evil, deserving glorification does not depend on difficult acts of obligation. For example, uttering the shahada is easier than jihad and fasting, yet it earns greater reward. If the sole purpose were to deserve glorification, God should increase our strength and then obligate us with easy acts to achieve that deserving without hardship. Thus the Mu'tazila's argument is internally inconsistent.

Key Terms
التعويض (recompense)
التعظيم (glorification)
التفضل (gratuitous favor)
الحسن والقبح (good and evil)
التكليف (obligation)
الاستحقاق (deservingness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God must recompense the servant for obligation because otherwise it would be unjust or evil.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that God is not subject to human moral categories like harm and benefit, and that obligation is a gratuitous favor, not a transaction.

Checkpoint
What example does Imam Razi give to show that deserving glorification does not depend on difficulty of acts?
A) Jihad and fasting
B) Uttering the shahada compared to jihad and fasting
C) Prayer and charity
D) Patience and gratitude

Key Takeaway
God's obligation is a gratuitous favor, not a transaction requiring recompense; difficulty of acts is not the basis for reward.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself thinking that your acts of worship are a burden that God owes you for? How does Razi’s argument that obligation is a gratuitous favor challenge that transactional mindset? What will you change today in how you approach your prayers?
?
When you find a religious duty easy, do you assume it earns less reward than something harder? The shahada is easier than jihad yet greater in reward. What does that reveal about your assumptions? Will you start valuing the quality of your heart in worship over the quantity of your effort?
?
If God does not need our worship and is not obligated to reward us, why do you still pray and fast? Is your motivation fear of punishment, hope for reward, or gratitude for His gratuitous favor? Which one truly drives you, and how will you cultivate the purest motive starting from your next act of worship?

First Two Objections to Prophethood: Dahriyya and Deniers of Taklif
Imam Razi lists two initial objections to prophethood from the Dahriyya and deniers of taklif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two shubah (objections) that Imam Razi lists against prophethood, coming from the Dahriyya (materialists) and those who deny taklif (religious obligation).

Core Idea
The first two objections to prophethood attack the foundations of a personal God and religious duty.

Imam Razi begins his discussion of objections to prophethood by presenting the arguments of two groups: the Dahriyya, who reject the idea of a voluntary Creator, and the deniers of taklif, who question the validity of religious obligations. These two shubah form the initial challenges that must be addressed in defending prophethood. As you read, note that Imam Razi is not endorsing these views but setting up the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how Imam Razi presents two distinct shubah in a concise manner.
The passage is introductory; the full arguments are not elaborated here.
2
Identify each shubha and its target
The first shubha attacks the concept of a free agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) and denies that the Creator is knowing, powerful, and willing. The second shubha challenges the validity of taklif itself.
Write down the key elements of each objection.
3
Consider the author's purpose
Imam Razi is listing these shubah to later refute them. Reflect on why he starts with these two objections.
Understanding the opponent's position is crucial for a robust defense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أن ما ذكرتم يدل على النبوة لكن هنا ما يدل على القدح فيها وهو من وجوه. الأول: شبهة الدهرية وهى بالقدح في الفاعل المختار وإنكار كون الصانع عالما قادرا بالحركات مريدا. وثانيها: شبهة منكرى التكليف فإنهم يقولون الأنبياء إنما جاءوا من عند الله تعالى بالتكاليف لكن القول بالتكليف مجال.
We concede that what you have mentioned indicates prophethood, but here there is something that indicates objection to it, and it is in aspects. The first: the shubha of the Dahriyya, which is the objection to the free agent and the denial that the Creator is knowing, powerful, and willing through movements. The second: the shubha of the deniers of taklif (obligation), for they say that the prophets came from God Almighty with obligations, but the assertion of taklif is subject to discussion.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage introduces the first two of four shubah (objections) against the validity of prophethood. The first shubha comes from the Dahriyya (materialists), who reject the idea of a voluntary agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhtār) and deny that the Creator is knowing, powerful, and willing through actions (ḥarakāt). This undermines the possibility of a prophet being sent by a personal God. The second shubha comes from those who deny taklif (religious obligation), arguing that prophets bring obligations from God, but the very notion of taklif is questionable or objectionable (majāl—lit. 'scope for discussion'). Imam Razi simply lists these two objections here without elaboration, as they are introductory.

Key Terms
شبهة
الدهرية
الفاعل المختار
التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Imam Razi is endorsing these objections as valid.
Correction: Imam Razi is merely presenting them as objections to be refuted; he does not agree with them.

Checkpoint
What is the first shubha mentioned by Imam Razi?
A) The shubha of the Jews
B) The shubha of the Dahriyya
C) The shubha of the Barahima
D) The shubha of the deniers of taklif

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi introduces the first two shubah against prophethood as challenges to be answered, not as accepted truths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Dahriyya deny that God is a free agent who acts with knowledge and will. If you truly believe in al-fāʿil al-mukhtār, how should that affect your trust in His guidance and your response to life's trials? Write down one specific situation where you need to rely on God's wisdom rather than your own.
?
The deniers of taklif question the very idea of religious obligations. When you feel the weight of Islamic duties (prayer, fasting, etc.), do you see them as burdens or as expressions of your relationship with the Creator? Be honest: what would change in your practice if you saw each obligation as a gift from the All-Wise?
?
Imam Razi presents these objections without refuting them here. Are you prepared to answer someone who raises these doubts? What would you say to a friend who claims God is not involved in the world, or that religious rules are unnecessary? Commit to finding one answer from Islamic sources this week.

The Philosophers' Ten Arguments for Angelic Superiority
Razi enumerates ten philosophical arguments for angels being superior to humans.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and explain the ten philosophical arguments for the superiority of angels over humans as presented by the philosophers and cited by Imam al-Razi.

Core Idea
The philosophers argue angels surpass humans in simplicity, purity, actuality, luminosity, knowledge, power, choice, and cosmic role.

Imam al-Razi presents ten arguments from the philosophers (falasifa) for why angels are superior to humans. These arguments contrast spiritual beings (ruhaniyyat) with corporeal beings (jismaniyyat) across multiple dimensions. As you read, notice how each argument builds a cumulative case for ontological superiority.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recognize that these arguments are presented by the philosophers, not necessarily endorsed by Razi. He is documenting their view for subsequent refutation.
Razi often presents opposing views before critiquing them.
2
Read the first five arguments
Focus on arguments 1-5: simplicity vs. composition, freedom from lust/anger, actuality vs. potentiality, pure existence without passivity, and luminosity vs. density. These address intrinsic qualities.
3
Read the last five arguments
Arguments 6-10 cover superiority in knowledge, power over bodies, decisive choice towards good, association with noble structures, and cosmic agency as beginning and end.
Argument 6 is the longest, detailing cognitive and practical superiority.
4
Reflect on the cumulative case
Consider how the arguments together portray angels as ontologically superior in essence, knowledge, and action. Ask yourself what counterarguments Razi might offer.
These arguments are philosophical, not strictly scriptural.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الفلاسفة فقد احتجوا على أن الملك أفضل بوجه أوجه:

أحدها: أن الروحانيات بسائط والجسمانيات مركبات والبسائط أشرف من المركبات.

وثانيها: الروحانيات مطهرة عن الشهوة والغضب الذي هو منشأ الأخلاق الذميمة والجسمانيات غير خالية عنها.

وثالثها: الروحانيات صورة مجردة كمالاتها ظاهرة بالفعل والنفوس البشرية مادية إما بجواهرها عند من يجعل النفس مزاجاً أو في أفعالها عند من يجعلها مجردة، وعلى التقديرين فهي بالقوة وما بالفعل أشرف مما بالقوة.

ورابعها: الروحانيات صورة مجردة ليس فيها طبيعة الانفعال فتكون وجودات محضة وخيرات محمدة.

وخامسها: الروحانيات نورانية علوية لطيفة والجسمانيات كثيفة سفلية.

وسادسها: الروحانيات فائقة على الجسمانيات لقوى العلم والعمل؛ أما العلم فلإحاطتها بالأمور الغائبة عنا واطلاعهم على مستقبل الأحوال الجارية علينا، وعلومهم كلية وعلوم الجسمانيات جزئية وعلومهم فعلية وعلوم الجسمانيات انفعالية وعلومهم نظرية أمينة عن الغلط وعلوم الجسمانيات كسبية متعرضة للغلط، وأما العمل فلكونهم عاكفين على العبادة ويسبحون الليل والنهار ولا يفترون والجسمانيات ليست كذلك.

وسابعها: الروحانيات لها قوة قوية على تصريف الأجسام كالسحاب والزلازل القوية من غير أن يعرضها فتور وكلال بخلاف الجسمانيات.

وثامنها: الروحانيات اختياراتها متوجهة إلى الخيرات ونظام العالم والجسمانيات اختياراتها غير جازمة بل مترددة بين جهتي السفالة والعلو.

وتاسعها: الروحانيات مختصة بالهياكل العلوية النورانية والجسمانيات مختصة بهذه الهياكل الفاسدة. ولشبه الأرواح بشبه الهياكل، فلما كانت الهياكل السماوية أشرف كانت الأرواح السماوية أشرف.

وعاشرها: الأرواح الفلكية متصرفة في هذا العالم، فإنها هي المدبرات أمراً وهي المبدأ والمعاد وهما أشرف من ذي المبدأ وذى المعاد، فالروحانيات أشرف.
As for the philosophers, they have argued that the angel is superior in several aspects:

First: Spiritual beings are simple, corporeal beings are composites, and simple things are nobler than composites.

Second: Spiritual beings are purified from lust and anger, which are the source of blameworthy traits, while corporeal beings are not free from them.

Third: Spiritual beings are abstract forms whose perfections are actual, while human souls are material—either in their substance, according to those who make the soul a temperament, or in their acts, according to those who make it abstract—and in both cases they are potential, and what is actual is nobler than what is potential.

Fourth: Spiritual beings are abstract forms without the nature of passivity, so they are pure existences and praiseworthy goods.

Fifth: Spiritual beings are luminous, high, and subtle, while corporeal beings are dense and low.

Sixth: Spiritual beings surpass corporeal beings in the powers of knowledge and action. As for knowledge: because they encompass matters absent from us and are aware of future states of affairs; their knowledge is universal, while corporeal knowledge is particular; their knowledge is active, while corporeal knowledge is passive; their knowledge is theoretical and safe from error, while corporeal knowledge is acquired and subject to error. As for action: because they are constantly devoted to worship, glorifying night and day without tiring, while corporeal beings are not like that.

Seventh: Spiritual beings have strong power to dispose of bodies, like clouds and strong earthquakes, without experiencing fatigue or exhaustion, unlike corporeal beings.

Eighth: Spiritual beings' choices are directed toward good and the order of the world, while corporeal beings' choices are not decisive but vacillate between lowliness and highness.

Ninth: Spiritual beings are associated with the high luminous structures, while corporeal beings are associated with these corrupt structures. And since souls resemble structures, when the celestial structures are nobler, the celestial souls are nobler.

Tenth: The celestial souls are the agents in this world, for they are the administrators of affairs, and they are the beginning and the return, and these two are nobler than that which has a beginning and a return, so spiritual beings are nobler.
Source pages p. 223

Commentary
This passage lists ten arguments from the philosophers (falasifa) for the superiority of angels over humans. The passage is an enumeration, so it follows a numbered structure. Each argument contrasts spiritual beings (ruhaniyyat) with corporeal beings (jismaniyyat). The first argument: simple things (like spiritual beings) are nobler than composites. Second: spiritual beings are free from lust and anger. Third: actuality is better than potentiality. Fourth: pure existence without passivity. Fifth: luminous vs dense. Sixth: superiority in knowledge (universal, active, error-free) and action (constant worship). Seventh: power over bodies without fatigue. Eighth: decisive choice towards good. Ninth: association with noble celestial structures. Tenth: they are the beginning and end of the world. The arguments build a cumulative case for the ontological superiority of angels.

Key Terms
روحانيات (ruhaniyyat)
جسمانيات (jismaniyyat)
بسائط (basait)
مركبات (murakkabat)
قوة (quwwa)
فعل (fi'l)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These arguments are accepted by the author (Imam al-Razi) as definitive proof.
Correction: Imam al-Razi is presenting the philosophers' arguments to refute them. He does not necessarily endorse them; they are part of a dialectical presentation.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why are spiritual beings superior in knowledge?
A) They have more teachers
B) Their knowledge is universal, active, and free from error
C) They read more books
D) They have better memory

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' arguments for angelic superiority rely on contrasts in simplicity, purity, actuality, luminosity, knowledge, power, choice, and cosmic role, but these are presented for critical examination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that angels are constantly devoted to worship without tiring (argument 6), does it make you complacent about your own prayer? Or does it challenge you to increase your devotion? Tonight, make a specific intention to pray an extra voluntary prayer or prolong your sujood, remembering that your potential is not an excuse but a call to action.
?
The philosophers say angels' choices are decisively toward good, while human choices vacillate (argument 8). Where in your life today are you indecisive about obeying Allah? Think of one concrete situation where you are hesitating between good and evil. Commit right now to one small step toward the good, even if it feels uncertain.
?
If humans are 'corrupt structures' associated with decay (argument 9), how does that challenge your self-esteem? Do you feel you are utterly inferior to angels? Or does the Islamic teaching of Adam's vicegerency give you a higher purpose? Write down one way you can live up to that honor today, despite your weaknesses.

The Muslim Rebuttal with Quranic Verses
Razi cites three Quranic verses and states the basis of the Muslim answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Muslim theologians used specific Quranic verses to counter the philosophers' argument for angelic superiority and why the answer relies on invalidating their principles.

Core Idea
The Quran denies angelic superiority by equating them with servants.

Here, Razi presents the core Muslim response to the philosophers' claim that angels are superior to humans. The Muslims cite three Quranic verses that challenge angelic superiority directly. The answer, however, is not merely scriptural—it is also based on invalidating the philosophers' metaphysical principles, which has been discussed earlier.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the translation. Note the three Quranic verses quoted.
The verses are from Surah Al-A'raf (7:20), Surah An-Nisa (4:172), and Surah Yusuf (12:31).
2
Identify the three verses
Find each verse in the passage and reflect on its meaning: one about becoming angels, one about Messiah and angels as servants, and one calling Yusuf a noble angel.
3
Understand the basis of the answer
Note the phrase 'إبطال أصولهم' (invalidating their principles). This shows the Muslim response is not just quoting verses but also engaging in philosophical refutation.
This connects to the broader method of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المسلمون فقد احتجوا على التفضيل بقوله تعالى: «ما نهاكما ربكما عن هذه الشجرة إلا أن تكونا ملكين»، وقوله: «ولن يستنكف المسيح أن يكون عبداً لله ولا الملائكة المقربون»، وقوله: «ما هذا بشراً إن هذا إلا ملك كريم». والجواب عن شبهة الفلاسفة مبني على إبطال أصولهم وقد تقدم ذلك من التمسك بالآيات المذكورة في [؟].

(أقول: في هذا الكلام خبط كثير. أما قوله البسيط أشرف من المركب فيقتضي أن تكون العناصر أشرف من الناس حتى من الأنبياء فإن أجسادهم مركبة من العناصر. وقوله الروحانيات مطهرة عن الشهوة والغضب والجسمانيات غير خالية عنها فيقال له إن أردت بالروحانيات المعارف فالنفيس البشرية مفارقة وهي ملابسة بالشهوة والغضب والأجسام الفلكية والمنصرية ونفوسها خالية عنها. وقوله: الروحانيات وجودات محضة ليس فيها طبيعة الانفعال والجسمانيات مركبة من مادة وصورة ففي هذه القسمة سقطت النفوس العلوية والسفلية من القسمة. وقوله: الروحانيات نورانية علوية لطيفة وصفها بأوصاف الأجسام، فإن النوراني والظلماني والعلوي والسفلي واللطيف والكثيف لا يكون إلا جسماً إلا أن يريد بهذه الصفات غير ما هي دالة عليها. وقوله في تفصيل علم الروحانيات بإحاطتهم بالأمور الغائبة عنها مستدرك لأن الغيبة والحضور لا يكون إلا في غير الأجسام.)
As for the Muslims, they have argued for preference by His saying: 'Your Lord did not forbid you this tree except that you become angels,' and His saying: 'The Messiah would never disdain to be a servant of Allah, nor would the angels brought near [to Him],' and His saying: 'This is not a human; this is none but a noble angel.' And the answer to the philosophers' doubt is based on invalidating their principles, and that has preceded by adhering to the mentioned verses in [؟].

(I say: There is much confusion in this discourse. As for his statement that the simple is nobler than the composite, it entails that the elements are nobler than people, even than prophets, because their bodies are composed of the elements. As for his statement that spiritual beings are purified from lust and anger while corporeal beings are not free from them, it is said to him: If you mean by spiritual beings the intellects, then human souls are separate (mufariqa) and they are clothed with lust and anger, while celestial bodies and [؟] and their souls are free from them. As for his statement that spiritual beings are pure existences without the nature of passivity and corporeal beings are composed of matter and form, in this division the higher and lower souls have fallen out of the division. As for his statement that spiritual beings are luminous, high, and subtle, he has described them with attributes of bodies, for luminous and dark, high and low, subtle and dense are only bodies, unless he intends by these attributes other than what they signify. As for his statement detailing the knowledge of spiritual beings as encompassing matters absent from them, this is redundant because absence and presence only apply to non-bodies.)
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage provides the Muslim response to the philosophers' arguments for angelic superiority. First, the Muslims cite Quranic verses that imply angels are not superior: the verse about Adam's prohibition ('except that you become angels') and verses that equate angels with servants. The answer to the philosophers is said to be based on invalidating their principles, which has been previously done. The marginal note (أقول) then offers a detailed critique of several of the philosophers' arguments. It points out contradictions: if simplicity is better, then elements (simple bodies) would be better than humans. If spiritual beings are purified from lust, then human souls are also spiritual but still subject to lust. The classification of pure existence vs. composite bodies omits the category of souls (which are spiritual but composite in a sense). The description of spiritual beings as luminous and subtle uses corporeal attributes. The claim about knowledge of absent things is flawed because absence and presence are spatiotemporal concepts that only apply to bodies.

Key Terms
إبطال الأصول (ibṭāl al-uṣūl)
شبهة (shubhah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Muslim response only involves quoting Quranic verses without engaging the philosophers' logic.
Correction: The passage explicitly says the answer is 'based on invalidating their principles' (ibṭāl uṣūlihim), showing a combined scriptural and rational approach.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the Muslim answer to the philosophers' arguments according to this passage?
A) Denying the existence of angels
B) Invalidating the philosophers' principles
C) Accepting the philosophers' premises
D) Ignoring their arguments

Key Takeaway
The Muslim response to angelic superiority is rooted in Quranic verses and the invalidation of the philosophers' principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that both the Messiah and the angels are called 'servants of Allah,' do you truly believe that your own status before Allah is no less than that of the highest creation? Or do you still feel inferior, as if your humanity is a flaw? How would your daily life change if you fully internalized that you are a servant just like the angels brought near?
?
The Quran describes Joseph as a 'noble angel' though he was human. Do you live as though your humanity can be elevated to that level, or are you content with the lowest rank? What would it take for you to become someone whom others describe as 'angelic' in character?
?
Razi's answer to the philosophers was not just quoting verses but also dismantling their entire basis of thought. When you face a doubt about your faith, do you only look for proof texts, or do you also examine and challenge the assumptions behind the doubt? What is a belief you hold that you have never truly questioned?

Critique of the Philosophers' Arguments
Razi critiques five specific philosophical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical inconsistencies Razi identifies in the philosophers' arguments for angelic superiority, including their claims about simplicity, purity, and existence.

Core Idea
The philosophers' own criteria imply absurdities and contradictions.

In this detailed critique, Razi dissects several key arguments used by philosophers to claim angelic superiority. He shows how their reasoning leads to absurd conclusions, such as elements being nobler than humans, and how their classifications omit important categories like human souls.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and catch the tone
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice that Razi uses the phrase 'أقول' (I say) to introduce his own critique, and he calls the philosophers' discourse 'خبط كثير' (much confusion).
2
Identify the five critiques
List the five points Razi addresses: simplicity, purity, composition, attributes (luminous/subtle), and knowledge of the unseen.
Each starts with 'وقوله' (and his statement).
3
Understand the flaw in each
For each critique, determine why Razi considers it invalid. For example, if simplicity is better, then elements would be better than humans.
Razi uses logical consequences and missing categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المسلمون فقد احتجوا على التفضيل بقوله تعالى: «ما نهاكما ربكما عن هذه الشجرة إلا أن تكونا ملكين»، وقوله: «ولن يستنكف المسيح أن يكون عبداً لله ولا الملائكة المقربون»، وقوله: «ما هذا بشراً إن هذا إلا ملك كريم». والجواب عن شبهة الفلاسفة مبني على إبطال أصولهم وقد تقدم ذلك من التمسك بالآيات المذكورة في [؟].

(أقول: في هذا الكلام خبط كثير. أما قوله البسيط أشرف من المركب فيقتضي أن تكون العناصر أشرف من الناس حتى من الأنبياء فإن أجسادهم مركبة من العناصر. وقوله الروحانيات مطهرة عن الشهوة والغضب والجسمانيات غير خالية عنها فيقال له إن أردت بالروحانيات المعارف فالنفيس البشرية مفارقة وهي ملابسة بالشهوة والغضب والأجسام الفلكية والمنصرية ونفوسها خالية عنها. وقوله: الروحانيات وجودات محضة ليس فيها طبيعة الانفعال والجسمانيات مركبة من مادة وصورة ففي هذه القسمة سقطت النفوس العلوية والسفلية من القسمة. وقوله: الروحانيات نورانية علوية لطيفة وصفها بأوصاف الأجسام، فإن النوراني والظلماني والعلوي والسفلي واللطيف والكثيف لا يكون إلا جسماً إلا أن يريد بهذه الصفات غير ما هي دالة عليها. وقوله في تفصيل علم الروحانيات بإحاطتهم بالأمور الغائبة عنها مستدرك لأن الغيبة والحضور لا يكون إلا في غير الأجسام.)
As for the Muslims, they have argued for preference by His saying: 'Your Lord did not forbid you this tree except that you become angels,' and His saying: 'The Messiah would never disdain to be a servant of Allah, nor would the angels brought near [to Him],' and His saying: 'This is not a human; this is none but a noble angel.' And the answer to the philosophers' doubt is based on invalidating their principles, and that has preceded by adhering to the mentioned verses in [؟].

(I say: There is much confusion in this discourse. As for his statement that the simple is nobler than the composite, it entails that the elements are nobler than people, even than prophets, because their bodies are composed of the elements. As for his statement that spiritual beings are purified from lust and anger while corporeal beings are not free from them, it is said to him: If you mean by spiritual beings the intellects, then human souls are separate (mufariqa) and they are clothed with lust and anger, while celestial bodies and [؟] and their souls are free from them. As for his statement that spiritual beings are pure existences without the nature of passivity and corporeal beings are composed of matter and form, in this division the higher and lower souls have fallen out of the division. As for his statement that spiritual beings are luminous, high, and subtle, he has described them with attributes of bodies, for luminous and dark, high and low, subtle and dense are only bodies, unless he intends by these attributes other than what they signify. As for his statement detailing the knowledge of spiritual beings as encompassing matters absent from them, this is redundant because absence and presence only apply to non-bodies.)

Commentary
This passage provides the Muslim response to the philosophers' arguments for angelic superiority. First, the Muslims cite Quranic verses that imply angels are not superior: the verse about Adam's prohibition ('except that you become angels') and verses that equate angels with servants. The answer to the philosophers is said to be based on invalidating their principles, which has been previously done. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You may have defended your beliefs with logical arguments. When have you, like the philosophers in this lesson, used criteria that you later realized were inconsistent? What will you change about how you examine your own reasoning?
?
Razi shows that simple things are not always nobler—human prophets are composite yet nobler than elements. Think of a time you judged something as 'better' based on a superficial criterion. How will you train yourself to look deeper?
?
The philosophers described spiritual beings with physical terms. Where in your own life do you reduce spiritual realities to material terms? What step will you take this week to treat the unseen as truly beyond physical description?

Muslim Proofs and Author's Initial Critique
Muslims cite verses; author dismisses as confused.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Muslim position on angelic superiority based on Quranic verses and the author's immediate dismissal of their reasoning as confused.

Core Idea
The author calls the entire Muslim philosophical discourse 'much confusion.'

The passage begins by presenting the Muslim response to the philosophers' claim of angelic superiority. Muslims cite three Quranic verses to support their view. However, the author immediately interjects with a sharp critique, labeling the entire argumentation as 'much confusion.' This lesson examines that initial exchange and sets the stage for the detailed refutation that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the Muslim argument
Read the opening sentence where Muslims cite three Quranic verses as proof for angels being superior to humans. Note which verses are referenced.
The verses are from Surah Al-A'raf (7:20), Surah An-Nisa (4:172), and Surah Yusuf (12:31).
2
Read the author's one-line critique
After the Muslim argument, the author says 'I say: In this speech there is much confusion.' This is a strong dismissal. Consider why the author would reject the entire approach so decisively.
The author's critique will be unpacked in later lessons.
3
Reflect on the disconnect
Think about the difference between citing scripture and using rational argument. The Muslims used Quranic verses, but the philosophers' arguments were rational. The author suggests that such a direct appeal to scripture does not properly engage with the philosophical framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المسلمون فقد احتجا على التفضيل بقوله تعالى « مانها كما ربكما عن هذه الشجرة إلا أن تكونا ملكين ، وقوله ولن يستنكف المسيح أن يكون عبداً الله ولا الملائكة المقربون ، وقوله ما هذا بشوا إن هذا إلا ملك كريم.. والجواب عن شبهة الفلاسفة مبنى على إبطال أصولهم وقد تقدم ذلك من التمسك بالآيات المذكورة في
(1) أقول : في هذا الكلام خبط كثير .
أما قوله البسيط أشرف من المركب فيقتضى أن تكون العناصر أشرف من الناس حتى من الأنبياء فإن أجسادهم مركبة من العناصر .
وقوله الروحانيات مطاهرة عن الشهوة والغضب والجسمانيات غير خالية عنها فيقال له إن أردت بالروحانيات المعارف فالنفيس البشرية مفارقة وهى ملابسة بالشهرة والغضب والأجسام الفلكية والمنصرية ونفوسها خالية عنها .
وقوله : الروحانيات وجودات محضة ليس فيها طبيعة الانفعال والجسمانيات مركبة من مادة وصورة ففى هذه القسمة سقطت النفوس العلوية والسفلية من القسمة .
وقوله: الروحانيات نورانية علوية لطيفة وصفها بأوصاف الأجسام ، فإن النوراني والظلمانى والعلوي والسفلى واللطيف والكثيف لا يكون إلا جسما إلا أن يريد بهذه الصفات غير ما هى دالة عليها وقوله في تفصيل علم الروحانيات بإحاطتهم بالأمور الغائبة عنها مستدرك لأن الغيبة والحضور لا يكون في غير الأجسام ، وقوله
اطلاعهم على مستقبل أحوالنا يناقض قوله ، لان علومهم كلية وقوله وعلومهم فعلية يقتضى أنها لا تعلم إلا له لأنها ليست بفاعلة إياه ولا يعلم السافل منها ما هو أعلى درجة منه ، وأما عكوفهم على العبادة فمن شأن النفوس السمارية عندهم التي تحرك أجسامها تقربا إلى مباديها .
وقوله: الروحانيات تقوى على تصريف السحاب والزلازل فهذا أخرج العقول عن الروحانيات لأنها لا نباشر الأجسام والرياح والأبخرة التى تصرف الرياح وتعمل الزلازل ليست بعقول ولا نفرس .
وفى قوله الجسمانيات اختياراتها غير جازمة أخرج النفوس البشرية عن الروحانيات وفي قوله الروحانيات مختصة بالهياكل العلوية والجسمانيات بالهياثل الفاسدة أخرج المقول من الروحانيات وجعل النفوس البشرية جسمانية ، وقوله الأرواح الفلكية هي المدرات أمرا خاص بالفرس السماوية وخرج العقول من الروحانيات .
وقوله : هى المبدأ والمعاد لا يقول به أحد فإن الفلاسفة يقولون إن المبدأ من الله والمداد إليه لا من النفوس وإليها ، أما الأول فظهر ، وأما الثانى الآن كمال النفوس الإنسانيه وغاية سعيها معرفة الله والتوجه بالكلية إليه وهو المراد من العود إليه .
(1) أقول : لو دات الآية الأولى على تفضيل الملك على آدم وقت مخاطبة إبليس لكنها ما دلت على تفضيله عليهما بعد الاجتباء، وفى الآية الثانية فى الاستنكاف عن الملائكه لا يدل على تفه يلهم على المسيح بل إنما ذكرهم بعد المسيح الذى قتل النصارى إنه ابن الله لدول الم. مركين انهم بنات الرحمن ، والآية الثالثة تدل على تخيل النساء أن جمال الملك يكون أكثر من جمال البشر لا على تفضيل الملك على البشر
As for the Muslims, they argued for [the angels'] superiority by His saying, exalted: 'What your Lord forbade you from this tree is only that you become angels' [Quran 7:20?], and His saying: 'The Messiah would never disdain to be a servant of Allah, nor the angels brought near' [Quran 4:172], and His saying: 'This is not a human; this is nothing but a noble angel' [Quran 12:31?]. And the answer to the philosophers' doubt is based on invalidating their principles, and that has preceded in adhering to the verses mentioned in the [first section?].
(1) I say: In this speech there is much confusion.
As for his saying 'the simple is nobler than the composite,' it requires that the elements be nobler than people, even than the prophets, for their bodies are composed of elements.
And his saying 'the spiritual beings are pure from lust and anger, while the bodily beings are not free from them,' so it is said to him: If you mean by the spiritual beings the intellects, then the human soul is separate and is clothed with lust and anger, while the celestial and [Christian?] bodies and their souls are free from them.
And his saying 'the spiritual beings are pure existences with no nature of passivity, and the bodily beings are composed of matter and form,' so in this division the higher and lower souls fall out of the division.
And his saying 'the spiritual beings are luminous, high, subtle' describes them with attributes of bodies, for the luminous and dark, high and low, subtle and dense are not except bodies, unless he intends by these attributes other than what they indicate. And his saying in detailing the knowledge of spiritual beings by their encompassing matters absent from them is objectionable, because absence and presence do not occur except in bodies. And his saying:
'their knowledge of our future events contradicts his saying, because their sciences are universal, and his saying that their sciences are active requires that they know only what they cause, for they are not the doers of it, and the lower of them does not know what is higher in degree than it. And as for their devotion to worship, it is from the nature of the celestial souls according to them, which move their bodies in proximity to their principles.
And his saying: 'the spiritual beings have power to dispose of clouds and earthquakes' – this excludes the intellects from the spiritual beings, because they do not directly handle bodies, and the winds and vapors that produce winds and cause earthquakes are not intellects nor souls.
And in his saying 'the choices of bodily beings are not decisive,' he excludes human souls from the spiritual beings, and in his saying 'the spiritual beings are specific to the high structures and the bodily beings to the corrupt structures,' he excludes the intellects from the spiritual beings and makes human souls bodily. And his saying 'the celestial souls are the administrators of affairs' is specific to the heavenly souls, and excludes the intellects from the spiritual beings.
And his saying 'they are the beginning and the return' – no one says that, for the philosophers say that the beginning is from God and the return is to Him, not from the souls and to them. As for the first, it is clear; as for the second, it is the perfection of human souls and the utmost of their striving is knowledge of God and turning entirely to Him, and that is the intended meaning of the return to Him.
(1) I say: If the first verse indicated the superiority of the angel over Adam at the time of Iblis's address, it does not indicate his superiority over them [Adam and Eve?] after election. And in the second verse, concerning the disdain of the angels, it does not indicate their superiority over the Messiah, but rather He mentioned them after the Messiah whom the Christians said is the son of God, [just as] the polytheists [؟] said they are daughters of the Most Merciful. And the third verse indicates the imagination of women that the beauty of an angel is greater than the beauty of a human, not the superiority of the angel over the human.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents the Muslim response and the author's detailed critique of the philosophers' ten arguments for the superiority of spiritual beings. First, Muslims cite Quranic verses (7:20? 4:172, 12:31?) to support angels' superiority. Then the author begins his critique with 'I say: In this speech there is much confusion.' He systematically refutes each point: (1) The argument 'simple is nobler than composite' would make elements nobler than humans. (2) If spiritual beings are pure from lust/anger, human souls are separate yet have those; celestial bodies lack them. (3) The division into pure existences vs. matter-form leaves out higher and lower souls. (4) Describing spiritual beings with physical attributes (luminous, high) is invalid because those attributes only apply to bodies. (5) Their knowledge of the unseen is problematic because absence/presence only apply to bodies. (6) Their knowledge of future contradicts their universal/active nature. (7) Their power over clouds/earthquakes excludes intellects. (8) Their decisive choices exclude human souls from spiritual beings. (9) Their association with high structures excludes intellects. (10) Their role as beginning and return contradicts philosophical principles. Finally, a second 'I say' critiques the Quranic citations, arguing the verses do not prove general superiority.

Key Terms
تفضيل
ملك
اجتباء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quranic verses cited by Muslims definitively prove that angels are superior to all humans in every respect.
Correction: The author argues that these verses are misinterpreted or taken out of context. For example, the first verse addresses a specific situation with Iblis, and the third verse reflects a human perception, not an objective reality. The author's later critique will show that the verses do not establish absolute superiority.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the Muslims' citation of Quranic verses?
A) He accepts them and elaborates on their meaning.
B) He dismisses the entire discourse as 'much confusion'.
C) He provides additional verses to support the argument.
D) He argues that the verses apply only to prophets.

Key Takeaway
The Muslims used Quranic texts to argue for angelic superiority, but the author rejects the entire discourse as confused, setting up his own rational critique.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quranic verses, do you tend to accept them as straightforward proofs, or do you pause to consider how they function in a broader debate? This passage shows that even verses can be part of 'much confusion'—how does that challenge your confidence in using isolated texts to settle complex theological questions?
?
Have you ever defended a religious position by citing scripture without fully grasping the opposing arguments or the deeper principles involved? The author's critique suggests that such an approach may be insufficient. Is there an area of your faith where you need to re-examine your assumptions rather than simply quoting familiar verses?
?
The author dismisses an entire line of reasoning as 'much confusion.' Are you willing to accept that some of your own cherished arguments might be flawed? How would you react if a scholar pointed out 'much confusion' in your understanding? Would you defend yourself or seek clarity?

Refutation of Ten Philosophers' Arguments
Detailed refutation of ten philosophers' arguments and critique of Quranic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's detailed refutation of the philosophers' ten arguments for the superiority of spiritual beings, and his final critique of the Muslim proof texts.

Core Idea
Each of the ten arguments fails due to internal inconsistencies or category errors.

After dismissing the Muslim proof texts as confused, the author proceeds to systematically dismantle the philosophers' ten rational arguments for the superiority of spiritual beings over bodily beings. He points out contradictions, misclassifications, and flawed premises. At the end, he also revisits the Quranic verses to show they do not prove superiority. This lesson walks through the key refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the first critique: simple vs. composite
The author argues that if 'the simple is nobler than the composite,' then the elements (simple) would be nobler than all humans, including prophets, which is absurd.
2
Follow the cascade of objections
The author lists multiple points: purity from lust, existence types, attributes of bodies, knowledge of the unseen, future knowledge, power over nature, choice, association with structures, and the role as beginning and return. Note how he exposes internal contradictions.
Some critiques use the philosophers' own definitions against them.
3
Read the final critique of the Quranic verses
In the second 'I say,' the author argues that the three cited verses do not prove general superiority: the first is about a specific event, the second is a refutation of deification, and the third reflects a human misconception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المسلمون فقد احتجا على التفضيل بقوله تعالى « مانها كما ربكما عن هذه الشجرة إلا أن تكونا ملكين ، وقوله ولن يستنكف المسيح أن يكون عبداً الله ولا الملائكة المقربون ، وقوله ما هذا بشوا إن هذا إلا ملك كريم.. والجواب عن شبهة الفلاسفة مبنى على إبطال أصولهم وقد تقدم ذلك من التمسك بالآيات المذكورة في
(1) أقول : في هذا الكلام خبط كثير .
أما قوله البسيط أشرف من المركب فيقتضى أن تكون العناصر أشرف من الناس حتى من الأنبياء فإن أجسادهم مركبة من العناصر .
وقوله الروحانيات مطاهرة عن الشهوة والغضب والجسمانيات غير خالية عنها فيقال له إن أردت بالروحانيات المعارف فالنفيس البشرية مفارقة وهى ملابسة بالشهرة والغضب والأجسام الفلكية والمنصرية ونفوسها خالية عنها .
وقوله : الروحانيات وجودات محضة ليس فيها طبيعة الانفعال والجسمانيات مركبة من مادة وصورة ففى هذه القسمة سقطت النفوس العلوية والسفلية من القسمة .
وقوله: الروحانيات نورانية علوية لطيفة وصفها بأوصاف الأجسام ، فإن النوراني والظلمانى والعلوي والسفلى واللطيف والكثيف لا يكون إلا جسما إلا أن يريد بهذه الصفات غير ما هى دالة عليها وقوله في تفصيل علم الروحانيات بإحاطتهم بالأمور الغائبة عنها مستدرك لأن الغيبة والحضور لا يكون في غير الأجسام ، وقوله
اطلاعهم على مستقبل أحوالنا يناقض قوله ، لان علومهم كلية وقوله وعلومهم فعلية يقتضى أنها لا تعلم إلا له لأنها ليست بفاعلة إياه ولا يعلم السافل منها ما هو أعلى درجة منه ، وأما عكوفهم على العبادة فمن شأن النفوس السمارية عندهم التي تحرك أجسامها تقربا إلى مباديها .
وقوله: الروحانيات تقوى على تصريف السحاب والزلازل فهذا أخرج العقول عن الروحانيات لأنها لا نباشر الأجسام والرياح والأبخرة التى تصرف الرياح وتعمل الزلازل ليست بعقول ولا نفرس .
وفى قوله الجسمانيات اختياراتها غير جازمة أخرج النفوس البشرية عن الروحانيات وفي قوله الروحانيات مختصة بالهياكل العلوية والجسمانيات بالهياثل الفاسدة أخرج المقول من الروحانيات وجعل النفوس البشرية جسمانية ، وقوله الأرواح الفلكية هي المدرات أمرا خاص بالفرس السماوية وخرج العقول من الروحانيات .
وقوله : هى المبدأ والمعاد لا يقول به أحد فإن الفلاسفة يقولون إن المبدأ من الله والمداد إليه لا من النفوس وإليها ، أما الأول فظهر ، وأما الثانى الآن كمال النفوس الإنسانيه وغاية سعيها معرفة الله والتوجه بالكلية إليه وهو المراد من العود إليه .
(1) أقول : لو دات الآية الأولى على تفضيل الملك على آدم وقت مخاطبة إبليس لكنها ما دلت على تفضيله عليهما بعد الاجتباء، وفى الآية الثانية فى الاستنكاف عن الملائكه لا يدل على تفه يلهم على المسيح بل إنما ذكرهم بعد المسيح الذى قتل النصارى إنه ابن الله لدول الم. مركين انهم بنات الرحمن ، والآية الثالثة تدل على تخيل النساء أن جمال الملك يكون أكثر من جمال البشر لا على تفضيل الملك على البشر
As for the Muslims, they argued for [the angels'] superiority by His saying, exalted: 'What your Lord forbade you from this tree is only that you become angels' [Quran 7:20?], and His saying: 'The Messiah would never disdain to be a servant of Allah, nor the angels brought near' [Quran 4:172], and His saying: 'This is not a human; this is nothing but a noble angel' [Quran 12:31?]. And the answer to the philosophers' doubt is based on invalidating their principles, and that has preceded in adhering to the verses mentioned in the [first section?].
(1) I say: In this speech there is much confusion.
As for his saying 'the simple is nobler than the composite,' it requires that the elements be nobler than people, even than the prophets, for their bodies are composed of elements.
And his saying 'the spiritual beings are pure from lust and anger, while the bodily beings are not free from them,' so it is said to him: If you mean by the spiritual beings the intellects, then the human soul is separate and is clothed with lust and anger, while the celestial and [Christian?] bodies and their souls are free from them.
And his saying 'the spiritual beings are pure existences with no nature of passivity, and the bodily beings are composed of matter and form,' so in this division the higher and lower souls fall out of the division.
And his saying 'the spiritual beings are luminous, high, subtle' describes them with attributes of bodies, for the luminous and dark, high and low, subtle and dense are not except bodies, unless he intends by these attributes other than what they indicate. And his saying in detailing the knowledge of spiritual beings by their encompassing matters absent from them is objectionable, because absence and presence do not occur except in bodies. And his saying:
'their knowledge of our future events contradicts his saying, because their sciences are universal, and his saying that their sciences are active requires that they know only what they cause, for they are not the doers of it, and the lower of them does not know what is higher in degree than it. And as for their devotion to worship, it is from the nature of the celestial souls according to them, which move their bodies in proximity to their principles.
And his saying: 'the spiritual beings have power to dispose of clouds and earthquakes' – this excludes the intellects from the spiritual beings, because they do not directly handle bodies, and the winds and vapors that produce winds and cause earthquakes are not intellects nor souls.
And in his saying 'the choices of bodily beings are not decisive,' he excludes human souls from the spiritual beings, and in his saying 'the spiritual beings are specific to the high structures and the bodily beings to the corrupt structures,' he excludes the intellects from the spiritual beings and makes human souls bodily. And his saying 'the celestial souls are the administrators of affairs' is specific to the heavenly souls, and excludes the intellects from the spiritual beings.
And his saying 'they are the beginning and the return' – no one says that, for the philosophers say that the beginning is from God and the return is to Him, not from the souls and to them. As for the first, it is clear; as for the second, it is the perfection of human souls and the utmost of their striving is knowledge of God and turning entirely to Him, and that is the intended meaning of the return to Him.
(1) I say: If the first verse indicated the superiority of the angel over Adam at the time of Iblis's address, it does not indicate his superiority over them [Adam and Eve?] after election. And in the second verse, concerning the disdain of the angels, it does not indicate their superiority over the Messiah, but rather He mentioned them after the Messiah whom the Christians said is the son of God, [just as] the polytheists [؟] said they are daughters of the Most Merciful. And the third verse indicates the imagination of women that the beauty of an angel is greater than the beauty of a human, not the superiority of the angel over the human.
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage presents the Muslim response and the author's detailed critique of the philosophers' ten arguments for the superiority of spiritual beings. First, Muslims cite Quranic verses (7:20? 4:172, 12:31?) to support angels' superiority. Then the author begins his critique with 'I say: In this speech there is much confusion.' He systematically refutes each point: (1) The argument 'simple is nobler than composite' would make elements nobler than humans. (2) If spiritual beings are pure from lust/anger, human souls are separate yet have those; celestial bodies lack them. (3) The division into pure existences vs. matter-form leaves out higher and lower souls. (4) Describing spiritual beings with physical attributes (luminous, high) is invalid because those attributes only apply to bodies. (5) Their knowledge of the unseen is problematic because absence/presence only apply to bodies. (6) Their knowledge of future contradicts their universal/active nature. (7) Their power over clouds/earthquakes excludes intellects. (8) Their decisive choices exclude human souls from spiritual beings. (9) Their association with high structures excludes intellects. (10) Their role as beginning and return contradicts philosophical principles. Finally, a second 'I say' critiques the Quranic citations, arguing the verses do not prove general superiority.

According to the author, why is the philosophers' argument that 'spiritual beings are pure from lust and anger' problematic?
A) Because human souls are also pure from lust and anger.
B) Because if 'spiritual' means intellects, then human souls (which have lust/anger) would not be spiritual, but celestial souls (which lack them) would be, creating inconsistency.
C) Because angels have no bodies so they cannot experience lust.
D) Because the argument only applies to prophets.

Key Takeaway
The author systematically refutes each of the philosophers' ten arguments and then shows that the Quranic verses do not prove superiority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you assume something is true because it sounds logical, do you ever test it by applying it to concrete examples? Think of a belief you held that crumbled when you examined its consequences. Are you willing to scrutinize your current beliefs with the same rigor the author applied to the philosophers?
?
Have you ever trusted an elaborate system of thought because it seemed sophisticated, only to find it contradicted itself? How does this lesson challenge you to be more critical of arguments that appear convincing at first glance?
?
The philosophers claimed spiritual beings are pure and free from bodily attributes, yet they described them in physical terms. Are there areas in your faith where you use physical language for spiritual realities without realizing the inconsistency? How can you reconcile this without falling into error?

The Aristotelian Thesis on the Unity of Human Souls
Razi presents and critiques the Aristotelian proof for unity of souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Aristotelian argument that all human souls are one in species and the key objection that being a human soul may be an accidental attribute.

Core Idea
The identity of human souls hinges on whether 'human' is essential or accidental.

This passage opens with the Aristotelian claim that all human souls are identical in species, supported by a proof based on composition. However, an important objection arises: what if 'being a human soul' is merely an accidental attribute, not part of the essence? This lesson walks through the argument, the objection, and the responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic and English to get an overview of the argument and objection.
Note the footnote markers (2) and (3) indicating editorial comments.
2
Identify the Aristotelian proof
Locate the proof that souls must be one in species: differing in essence would make them composite, and every composite is a body, which is absurd.
Razi clarifies that the real proof is the single definition, not the composition claim.
3
Understand the objection
The objector suggests that being a human soul could be an accident (managing bodies), allowing souls to differ in essence while sharing this accident.
The objector also challenges that every composite is a body, using the example of logical composition.
4
Reflect on the response
Note how Razī and the objector dismantle the premise that all composites are bodies, distinguishing different types of composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنذكر الآن بعض أحوال النفس ) ) مسألة : مذهب أرسطاطاليس وأتباعه أن النفوس البشرية متحدة بالنوع ) واحتجوا بأنها لو اختلفت بالماهية بعد اشتراكها في كونها نفوسا بشرية كانت مركبة ، لأن ما به الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز ، وكل مركب جسم فهو جسم . (۲) أقول : حجتهم على أن النفوس البشرية متحدة بالنوع أن الحد الواحد يشتملهما وهذا كاف وأما أن كل مركب جسم فإن أرادوا به التركيب العقلى فليس كذلك وإن المركب من الجنس والفصل لا يكون جسما كما ذكره وإن أرادوا به التركيب من الجوهر الحق لأن المركب من الجواهر لا يكون جسما بسيطا كالعناصر أو مركبا كالمعادن والنبات والحيوان ، والمركب من الأعراض كالحلية المركبة من اللون والشكل أيضا لا تكون جسما . الاعتراض: لم لا يجوز أن يقال كونها نفوسا بشرية معناه أنها مديرة الأبدان البشرية ، وكونها مديرة من عوارضها فلم لا يجوز أن يقال إنها مختلفة بتمام الماهية مشتركة في العوارض وذلك غير ممتنع كمان الدين فإنهما مع اختلافهما يشتركان في الاختلاف والتضاد ، سلمنا . لكن لم قلت إن كل مركب جسم بل مذهبكم أن الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة لكن الموجبة الكلية لا تنعكس كنفسها، وكيف وعندهم الجوهر جنس النفوس والعقول ، وكل ما دخل تحت الجنس كانت ماهيته مركبة من الجنس والفصل ومنهم من زعم أنها مختلفة بالماهيات واحتجوا بأنها مختلفة بالعفة والفجور والذكاء والبلادة ، وليس ذلك من توابع المزاج، لأن الإنسان قد يكون بارد المزاج وفى غاية الذكاء وقد يكون بالعكس ، وقد يتبدل المزاج والصفة النفسانية باقية ، ولا من الأسباب الخارجية لانها قد تكون بحيث يقتضى خلقا والحاصل ضده، فعلنا أنه من لوازم النفس ، واختلاف اللوازم يدل على اختلاف الملزومات ، وهذه الحجة إقناعية (۳) أقول : هذه الحجة مما أورده أبو البركات وغيره من المتقدمين أيضا من ذهب إليه وهى ضعيفة لأن الملزمات وإن اختلفت ليست هي النفس وحدها بل النفس والعوارض المختلفة ، ولما كانت النفوس مشمولة على حد واحد كانت متحدة بالنوع ومختلفة بالعوارض التي ذكرت ، والتي لم تذكر ، ومجموع النفس مع العوارض إذا كان مختلفا لا يلزم منه أن يكون كل جزء أيضا مختلفا فهذه الحجة مخالطية لا إقناعية .
Let us now mention some states of the soul. ( Question: The doctrine of Aristotle and his followers is that human souls are one in species ). They argued that if they differed in essence after sharing in being human souls, they would be composite, because that by which they share is other than that by which they are distinct, and every composite is a body. (2) I say: Their proof that human souls are one in species is that a single definition encompasses them, and that is sufficient. As for the statement that every composite is a body: if they mean logical composition, it is not [so]; and the composite of genus and differentia is not a body as he mentioned; and if they mean composition from the true substance, the composite of substances is not a body, whether simple like elements or composite like minerals, plants, and animals; and the composite of accidents, like a form composed of color and shape, is also not a body. The objection: Why may it not be said that their being human souls means they are managers of human bodies, and being managers is among their accidents? So why may it not be said that they differ completely in essence while sharing in accidents? That is not impossible, like two things that differ while sharing in difference and opposition. Granted, but why did you say that every composite is a body? Rather, according to you, the body is composed of matter and form, but the universal affirmative is not convertible as such. How, when according to them substance is the genus of souls and intellects, and everything that falls under a genus has its quiddity composed of genus and differentia? And some of them claimed that they differ in quiddities, arguing that they differ in chastity, lewdness, intelligence, and stupidity. And that is not from the consequences of temperament, because a man may be cold-tempered yet extremely intelligent, or the opposite; and temperament may change while the psychological quality remains; nor from external causes, because these may be such as to require a characteristic but the opposite occurs. So we know it is from the necessary concomitants of the soul, and difference in necessary concomitants indicates difference in that which they are necessary concomitants of. This argument is persuasive. (3) I say: This argument is one that Abu al-Barakat and other predecessors who held this view adduced, and it is weak because the necessary concomitants, even if they differ, are not the soul alone but the soul together with different accidents. And since souls are included under a single definition, they are one in species and differ by the accidents mentioned and those not mentioned. And if the combination of soul and accidents is different, it does not follow that each part is also different. So this argument is confusing, not persuasive.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage opens a new investigation into the soul. It states the Aristotelian position that all human souls are identical in species (united in type). The proof given is that if souls differed in essence while sharing the property of being human souls, they would be composite—having a shared element and a distinctive element—and every composite is a body; therefore souls would be bodies, which is absurd. Hence they must be one in species. An editorial footnote (2) immediately clarifies: the real proof is that souls fall under a single definition, which is sufficient; the claim that every composite is a body is not necessarily true—logical composition, composition from genus and differentia, or composition from substances or accidents does not make something a body in the relevant sense. The main objection then follows: why not say that being a human soul is merely an accidental attribute (being a manager of the body), and that souls could differ in essence while sharing this accident? This is likened to two things that share the accident of difference or opposition. The objector then challenges the premise that every composite is a body, pointing out that body is composed of matter and form, and that the universal affirmative does not convert. They also note that according to the Aristotelians, substance is the genus of souls, and anything falling under a genus has a quiddity composed of genus and differentia, implying composition. Then a different opinion is reported: some claim souls differ in quiddities, adducing differences in moral and intellectual traits. They argue these traits are not due to temperament or external causes but are necessary concomitants of the soul itself, and difference in concomitants indicates difference in the essence. This argument is labeled 'persuasive' (iqnāʿiyya). A final editorial footnote (3) dismisses this argument as weak: it conflates the soul itself with its accidents; souls are one in species and differ by accidents, so difference in the composite (soul+accidents) does not imply difference in the soul alone. The argument is deemed confusing, not even persuasive.

متحدة بالنوع
المركب
الجسم
الهيولى والصورة
الجنس والفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Aristotelian argument proves that souls are bodies.
Correction: No, it is a reductio ad absurdum: if souls differed, they would be bodies, which is impossible, so they must be one species. The objection shows that composition does not entail being a body.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection to the Aristotelian argument that souls must be one in species because difference in essence would make them bodies?
A) Souls are immaterial and therefore not subject to composition.
B) Being a human soul is an accidental property, so souls can differ in essence while sharing that accident.
C) The soul is a body, so composition is not an issue.
D) The argument is only persuasive, not demonstrative.

Key Takeaway
The Aristotelian argument for unity of souls is challenged by the possibility that 'human soul' is an accidental attribute rather than an essential property.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi suggests that the soul's management of the body might be an accidental attribute, not its essence. If that is true, what aspect of your soul remains constant regardless of your physical state? How does this challenge you to see yourself beyond your body and its desires?
?
The Aristotelian proof assumes that a composite must be a body. Razi shows that composition can be logical or accidental. Consider your own intellectual and spiritual composition: Are you merely a collection of parts, or is there a unity that cannot be broken down? How does this affect the way you prioritize your spiritual growth over your physical routines?

The Argument from Moral Differences and Its Refutation
Razi presents and refutes an argument for soul diversity based on moral and intellectual differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that differences in moral and intellectual traits prove souls differ in essence, and Razī's refutation that these traits are merely accidental.

Core Idea
Moral differences reflect accidents, not essences; souls remain one in species despite apparent diversity.

After discussing the Aristotelian proof, the passage presents an alternative argument: some philosophers claim that human souls differ in quiddity because they exhibit varied moral and intellectual traits. They argue these traits are necessary concomitants of the soul, so differences in them indicate differences in essence. Razī, however, dismisses this argument as weak and confusing, explaining that these traits are accidental, not essential. Let's explore this debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the alternative argument
Focus on the claim that souls differ in quiddity based on differences in chastity, lewdness, intelligence, and stupidity.
2
Examine the reasoning
Understand why the proponents rule out temperament and external causes as explanations.
They argue that temperament and external factors are inconsistent with observed traits.
3
Note the conclusion about concomitants
They conclude that the traits are necessary concomitants of the soul, and difference in concomitants implies difference in the underlying essence.
4
Study Razī's refutation
Razī points out that the concomitants are not the soul alone but the soul with accidents; souls are one in species and differ by accidents.
The argument is labeled 'confusing' (mukhalita), not even persuasive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنذكر الآن بعض أحوال النفس ) ) مسألة : مذهب أرسطاطاليس وأتباعه أن النفوس البشرية متحدة بالنوع ) واحتجوا بأنها لو اختلفت بالماهية بعد اشتراكها في كونها نفوسا بشرية كانت مركبة ، لأن ما به الاشتراك غير ما به الامتياز ، وكل مركب جسم فهو جسم . (۲) أقول : حجتهم على أن النفوس البشرية متحدة بالنوع أن الحد الواحد يشتملهما وهذا كاف وأما أن كل مركب جسم فإن أرادوا به التركيب العقلى فليس كذلك وإن المركب من الجنس والفصل لا يكون جسما كما ذكره وإن أرادوا به التركيب من الجوهر الحق لأن المركب من الجواهر لا يكون جسما بسيطا كالعناصر أو مركبا كالمعادن والنبات والحيوان ، والمركب من الأعراض كالحلية المركبة من اللون والشكل أيضا لا تكون جسما . الاعتراض: لم لا يجوز أن يقال كونها نفوسا بشرية معناه أنها مديرة الأبدان البشرية ، وكونها مديرة من عوارضها فلم لا يجوز أن يقال إنها مختلفة بتمام الماهية مشتركة في العوارض وذلك غير ممتنع كمان الدين فإنهما مع اختلافهما يشتركان في الاختلاف والتضاد ، سلمنا . لكن لم قلت إن كل مركب جسم بل مذهبكم أن الجسم مركب من الهيولى والصورة لكن الموجبة الكلية لا تنعكس كنفسها، وكيف وعندهم الجوهر جنس النفوس والعقول ، وكل ما دخل تحت الجنس كانت ماهيته مركبة من الجنس والفصل ومنهم من زعم أنها مختلفة بالماهيات واحتجوا بأنها مختلفة بالعفة والفجور والذكاء والبلادة ، وليس ذلك من توابع المزاج، لأن الإنسان قد يكون بارد المزاج وفى غاية الذكاء وقد يكون بالعكس ، وقد يتبدل المزاج والصفة النفسانية باقية ، ولا من الأسباب الخارجية لانها قد تكون بحيث يقتضى خلقا والحاصل ضده، فعلنا أنه من لوازم النفس ، واختلاف اللوازم يدل على اختلاف الملزومات ، وهذه الحجة إقناعية (۳) أقول : هذه الحجة مما أورده أبو البركات وغيره من المتقدمين أيضا من ذهب إليه وهى ضعيفة لأن الملزمات وإن اختلفت ليست هي النفس وحدها بل النفس والعوارض المختلفة ، ولما كانت النفوس مشمولة على حد واحد كانت متحدة بالنوع ومختلفة بالعوارض التي ذكرت ، والتي لم تذكر ، ومجموع النفس مع العوارض إذا كان مختلفا لا يلزم منه أن يكون كل جزء أيضا مختلفا فهذه الحجة مخالطية لا إقناعية .
Let us now mention some states of the soul. ( Question: The doctrine of Aristotle and his followers is that human souls are one in species ). They argued that if they differed in essence after sharing in being human souls, they would be composite, because that by which they share is other than that by which they are distinct, and every composite is a body. (2) I say: Their proof that human souls are one in species is that a single definition encompasses them, and that is sufficient. As for the statement that every composite is a body: if they mean logical composition, it is not [so]; and the composite of genus and differentia is not a body as he mentioned; and if they mean composition from the true substance, the composite of substances is not a body, whether simple like elements or composite like minerals, plants, and animals; and the composite of accidents, like a form composed of color and shape, is also not a body. The objection: Why may it not be said that their being human souls means they are managers of human bodies, and being managers is among their accidents? So why may it not be said that they differ completely in essence while sharing in accidents? That is not impossible, like two things that differ while sharing in difference and opposition. Granted, but why did you say that every composite is a body? Rather, according to you, the body is composed of matter and form, but the universal affirmative is not convertible as such. How, when according to them substance is the genus of souls and intellects, and everything that falls under a genus has its quiddity composed of genus and differentia? And some of them claimed that they differ in quiddities, arguing that they differ in chastity, lewdness, intelligence, and stupidity. And that is not from the consequences of temperament, because a man may be cold-tempered yet extremely intelligent, or the opposite; and temperament may change while the psychological quality remains; nor from external causes, because these may be such as to require a characteristic but the opposite occurs. So we know it is from the necessary concomitants of the soul, and difference in necessary concomitants indicates difference in that which they are necessary concomitants of. This argument is persuasive. (3) I say: This argument is one that Abu al-Barakat and other predecessors who held this view adduced, and it is weak because the necessary concomitants, even if they differ, are not the soul alone but the soul together with different accidents. And since souls are included under a single definition, they are one in species and differ by the accidents mentioned and those not mentioned. And if the combination of soul and accidents is different, it does not follow that each part is also different. So this argument is confusing, not persuasive.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage opens a new investigation into the soul. It states the Aristotelian position that all human souls are identical in species (united in type). The proof given is that if souls differed in essence while sharing the property of being human souls, they would be composite—having a shared element and a distinctive element—and every composite is a body; therefore souls would be bodies, which is absurd. Hence they must be one in species. An editorial footnote (2) immediately clarifies: the real proof is that souls fall under a single definition, which is sufficient; the claim that every composite is a body is not necessarily true—logical composition, composition from genus and differentia, or composition from substances or accidents does not make something a body in the relevant sense. The main objection then follows: why not say that being a human soul is merely an accidental attribute (being a manager of the body), and that souls could differ in essence while sharing this accident? This is likened to two things that share the accident of difference or opposition. The objector then challenges the premise that every composite is a body, pointing out that body is composed of matter and form, and that the universal affirmative does not convert. They also note that according to the Aristotelians, substance is the genus of souls, and anything falling under a genus has a quiddity composed of genus and differentia, implying composition. Then a different opinion is reported: some claim souls differ in quiddities, adducing differences in moral and intellectual traits. They argue these traits are not due to temperament or external causes but are necessary concomitants of the soul itself, and difference in concomitants indicates difference in the essence. This argument is labeled 'persuasive' (iqnāʿiyya). A final editorial footnote (3) dismisses this argument as weak: it conflates the soul itself with its accidents; souls are one in species and differ by accidents, so difference in the composite (soul+accidents) does not imply difference in the soul alone. The argument is deemed confusing, not even persuasive.

Key Terms
اللوازم
الملزومات
إقناعية
مخالطية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Differences in moral traits prove souls have different essences.
Correction: Razī argues that these differences are due to accidents, not the essence; souls share a single essence while differing in accidents.

Checkpoint
What does Razī say about the argument from differences in moral traits?
A) It is persuasive and should be accepted.
B) It is demonstrative and conclusive.
C) It is weak because it conflates the soul with its accidents.
D) It proves that souls differ in quiddities.

Key Takeaway
Differences in moral traits do not prove differences in the essence of souls; they are merely accidental variations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter someone whose moral character seems vastly different from your own, do you truly believe their soul is no different in essence from yours? Can you treat them with the same spiritual dignity you afford yourself, knowing that those differences are merely accidental?
?
Razi argues that your virtues and vices are accidents, not the core of who you are. How much of your self-image is tied to your own moral accomplishments? Could you let go of that pride if you saw them as merely temporary accidents rather than essential to your soul?
?
If all human souls are one in species, then your connection to every other person is deeper than culture, race, or creed. What specific step can you take today to act on that unity—perhaps by offering help or forgiveness to someone you have judged based on their outward moral character?
