Three Objections to Metempsychosis
Presents three arguments against the theory of metempsychosis (tanasukh).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational objections to the theory of metempsychosis (tanasukh) and how each is countered or evaluated.

Core Idea
Metempsychosis fails because it cannot account for memory, necessity, or soul-body attachment.

The theory of metempsychosis, the belief that souls migrate between bodies, has been debated by theologians. The author presents three objections against this view, each targeting a different logical flaw. You will examine these arguments and their replies, including the author's own critique of the third objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: three numbered objections with replies.
The author uses 'ثانيها' and 'ثالثها' to mark the second and third.
2
Identify the three objections
List each objection: 1) Why can't a soul attach to a body? 2) If identity existed before, we would remember. 3) If necessary, numbers must equal, leaving souls idle.
3
Understand the replies to the first two
The reply to the first is that self-perception only shows the soul as one, not that it cannot attach. The reply to the second is that memory might depend on attachment to that particular body.
Notice the logical move: each reply shifts the burden or redefines conditions.
4
Evaluate the weakness of the third objection
The author himself states that the weakness of the third argument is not hidden. Consider why it might be weak: perhaps because it assumes necessity when metempsychosis might be contingent.
This teaches you to critically assess even arguments presented as refutations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا حصول المساواة فلم لا يجوز تعلق النفس بالبدن . قوله : لأن كل واحد يجد نفسه شيئاً واحدا قلنا الذي يدرك من نفسى هو نفسى وكل نفس بحد نفسها نفسا واحدة لا غير فلم يلزم محذور . وثانيها لو كانت هويتنا موجودة قبل بدتنا في بدن آخر لتذكرنا تلك الحالة والاعتراض لم لا يجوز أن يكون تذكر أحوال كل بدن موقوفا على التعلق بذلك البدن . وثالثها : أنه لو التناسخ لكان إما أن يكون واجبا فيلزم أن يكون عدد الهالكين مثل عدد المحدثين ، محال ، لأنه يلزم بقاء النفس معطلة فيما بين التعليقين، وضعف هذه الحجة لا يخفى .
We grant the occurrence of equality, so why is it not permissible for the soul to be attached to the body? He said: because each one finds himself to be one thing. We said: that which I perceive of myself is my self, and every soul by its own limit is a single soul, no other, so no absurdity follows. And the second: If our identity existed before our body in another body, we would remember that state. And the objection: why is it not permissible that remembering the states of each body is dependent on attachment to that body? And the third: That if metempsychosis were to occur, it would either be necessary, then it would entail that the number of the perishing equals the number of the coming-to-be, which is impossible, because it would entail the soul remaining idle between attachments, and the weakness of this argument is not hidden.
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage presents three objections to the theory of metempsychosis (tanasukh), which holds that a soul can inhabit multiple bodies sequentially. The first objection: even if we grant that all souls are equal in some respect, why can't a soul attach to a body? The opponent claims each person experiences himself as one thing; the reply is that self-perception only shows the soul as one, not that it cannot attach to another body. The second objection: if our identity existed before in another body, we would remember it. To counter, it is argued that remembering might depend on attachment to that particular body. The third objection: if metempsychosis were necessary, the number of souls dying would equal the number being born, which is impossible because it would leave souls idle between attachments. The author notes the weakness of this third argument.

Key Terms
التناسخ (tanasukh)
النفس (al-nafs)
الهوية (al-huwiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third objection against metempsychosis is a strong, conclusive argument.
Correction: The author explicitly states that the weakness of the third argument is not hidden, so it should not be taken as a definitive refutation.

Checkpoint
What is the second objection against metempsychosis in this passage?
A) It would lead to an infinite regress of bodies.
B) If the soul existed in a previous body, we would remember that state.
C) The soul must be a substance.
D) It contradicts the oneness of the soul.

Key Takeaway
Metempsychosis is refuted by three arguments: memory failure, necessity of number, and the nature of soul attachment, but the third argument is acknowledged as weak.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The second objection says we would remember past lives if metempsychosis were true. You do not remember any past life. Does that not strengthen your conviction that your soul is only tied to this one body? How should this personal reality shape your sense of responsibility for your actions today?
?
The author included a weak third argument and admitted its weakness. When you present arguments for the truth, do you have the courage to honestly admit which of your points are weak? In what area of your faith or life do you need to be more intellectually honest?
?
If the soul attaches to a body only once, then your unique body matters eternally. Your body is not just a temporary shell. What does that imply about how you treat your body—your health, purity, and use—starting now?

Refutation of Scriptural Objections: General Forms and Contradiction
Al-Razi answers the Mu'tazilite appeal to Quranic verses threatening eternal punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi refutes the Mu'tazilite appeal to threat verses by arguing that general forms are not decisive and that threat verses are contradicted by promise verses.

Core Idea
Quranic threats are not absolute; general forms allow specification and are balanced by promises.

The Mu'tazilites cite Quranic verses threatening eternal punishment to argue that sins cannot be forgiven. Al-Razi counters with two key arguments: first, the language of general threats is not definitive and can be specified; second, these verses are balanced by verses of mercy. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how al-Razi presents the opponent's citation of two verses, then gives his two-part response.
The response is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the principle of general forms
Al-Razi states that general forms (ṣiyagh al-‘umūm) are not decisive (qāṭi‘a) but are apparent (ẓāhira) and open to specification (takhṣīṣ). This means threat verses may not apply to all sinners.
This is a key principle from Usul al-Fiqh.
3
Recognize the role of contradiction
Notice that al-Razi points out a contradiction (mu‘āraḍa) between threat verses and promise verses. He asserts that harmonization requires interpreting threats as conditional, not absolute.
This shows a method of reconciling apparently conflicting texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج الخصم بقوله تعالى ﴿ومن يقتل مؤمنا متعمدا فجزاؤه جهنم خالدا فيها﴾ وبقوله ﴿وإن الفجار لفي جحيم﴾. والجواب سنبين في كتاب أصول الفقه أن صيغ العموم ليست قاطعة في الاستغراق بل ظاهرة محتملة للخصوص، وإذا كان كذلك لم يمكن التمسك بها والقطع على الوعيد، وأيضا فهو معارض بآيات الوعد ولا طريق إلى التوفيق إلا ما ذكرنا.
The opponent argued with His statement, exalted be He, 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell, abiding therein forever' (Quran 4:93) and with His statement, 'Indeed, the wicked will be in Hellfire' (Quran 82:14). The answer: We will explain in the book of Usul al-Fiqh that general forms (ṣiyagh al-‘umūm) are not decisive (qāṭi‘a) regarding universal coverage, but are apparent (ẓāhira) and susceptible to specification (ikhtiṣāṣ). If that is so, it is not possible to rely on them and categorically affirm the threat. Also, they are contradicted (mu‘āriḍ) by verses of promise (īʿād), and there is no way to harmonize except what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 239

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite objection using Quranic verses that seem to threaten eternal punishment for murderers and the wicked, implying that such punishment cannot be lifted. Al-Razi's answer is twofold: First, the general forms (ṣiyagh al-‘umūm) in these verses are not definitive (qāṭi‘a) in indicating absolute universality; they are merely apparent (ẓāhira) and open to specification (takhṣīṣ), meaning they may apply to specific cases (e.g., unrepentant disbelievers). Second, these threat verses are contradicted by other verses promising forgiveness (e.g., 'Verily, your Lord is full of forgiveness for mankind despite their wrongdoing'). The only way to harmonize them is through the interpretation that threats are not absolute but conditional, allowing forgiveness before repentance.

Key Terms
صيغ العموم (general forms)
ظاهر (apparent)
تخصيص (specification)
مطلق (definitive)
معارضة (contradiction)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Threat verses in the Quran are absolute and apply to all sinners without exception.
Correction: Al-Razi explains that general forms are not decisive and are subject to specification, so threat verses do not necessarily apply to everyone; they are also balanced by promise verses.

Checkpoint
What is al-Razi's main argument against using threat-verses to prove eternal punishment for all sinners?
A) Those verses are abrogated.
B) General forms are not decisive and are subject to specification.
C) The verses only apply to disbelievers.
D) The verses are metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi refutes scriptural objections by showing that threat verses are not absolute and are countered by mercy verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of warning like 'Whoever kills a believer intentionally, his recompense is Hell', do you instinctively assume that every such person is damned forever? How does al-Razi's point that general forms are not decisive change the way you should approach God's threats? Will you now pause before judging yourself or others with absolute condemnation?
?
Have you ever felt trapped by a Quranic verse that seems to promise punishment for something you've done? How does knowing that threat verses are contradicted by mercy verses—and that the only harmonization is through repentance—affect your hope in God's forgiveness? What step will you take today to act on that hope?
?
If you rely on a single verse to support a harsh view of God, you may be ignoring other verses. Which verse of promise (e.g., 'Your Lord is full of forgiveness') will you commit to reciting after every threat verse you read this week, to keep God's mercy in view?

The Khawarij and the Grave Sinner
The Khawarij position and their view of Ali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Khawarij position on the grave sinner and their specific criticism of Ali (may Allah honor his face).

Core Idea
The Khawarij declared Ali a disbeliever for inconsistency in his treatment of sinners.

This lesson examines the earliest sectarian position on the grave sinner, that of the Khawarij. Their extreme stance led them to declare even Ali a disbeliever due to his seemingly contradictory actions of killing some Muslims but praying over them.

Guided Path
1
Read the Khawarij passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. The Khawarij held that the grave sinner is a disbeliever.
Notice the reasoning: they saw Ali killing and praying over the same group as contradiction.
2
Understand the reasoning
The Khawarij considered Ali's actions inconsistent, so they concluded he must be a sinner and disassociated from him.
This shows how a theological principle led to a personal judgment.
3
Reflect on the historical context
Recognize that this disagreement occurred after the Prophet's time, showing early intra-Muslim conflicts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) أقول : هذا الخلاف وقع بعد رسول الله ، والخوارج لما قالوا بتكفير الفاسق ورأوا عليا كرم وجهه يقتل جمعا من أهل القبلة ويصلى عليهم قالوا هذه مناقضة فهو فاق بسبب واحد من هذين الفعلين قطعا وتبرءوا عنه ، وفى زمن الحسن البصرى وقع هذا البحث بين أهل عصره فتمسك جماعة بأن الإيمان هـ التصديق والمكتب لا يخلو من أن يكون مصدقا بالله ورسوله أو لا يكون ، والثانى بالاتفق كافر ، والأول مؤمن ، ، والمصدق الفاسق يدخل تحت الأول فهو مؤمن وذنبه إما يغفر له أو يشفع فيه الذي لا أو يعذب هذا با منقطعا وهؤلاء هم المرجئة والفضيلية، وذهب واصل بن عطاء وعمرو من عبيد إلى أن صاحب الكبيرة يخلد في النار للآيات الدالة على تخليد عقوبة أهل الكبائر ، والمؤمن لا يخلد في النار فهو ليس بمؤمن ولا كافر ، وهذا هو القول بالمنزلة بين المنزلتين ، واعتزلوا عن خلقة الحسن ولدلك سموا بالمعتزلة وهم الوعيدية ، أما القائل بأنه مشرك فيقول ذلك لانه يعمل عملا الله وعملا لغيره فصاروا مشركين بمخلعتهم لقوله تعالى ولا يشرك بعبادة ربه أحدا ، والحسن حكم بنفاقهم للخبر المذكور .
(1) I say: This disagreement occurred after the Messenger of Allah. The Khawarij, when they declared the grave sinner a disbeliever and saw Ali (may Allah honor his face) killing a group of the people of the qibla and praying over them, said: 'This is contradictory; so he [Ali] is a sinner due to one of these two actions, definitely,' and they dissociated from him. In the time of al-Hasan al-Basri, this research occurred among the people of his era. Some held that faith (iman) is affirmation (tasdiq), and the responsible person [؟] must either be an affirmer of Allah and His Messenger or not; the second is by consensus a disbeliever, the first is a believer. The sinful affirmer falls under the first, so he is a believer, and his sin is either forgiven or interceded for, or he is punished temporarily. These are the Murji'a and the Fudayliyya. Wasil ibn 'Ata and 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd held that the grave sinner will abide eternally in the Fire, due to verses indicating eternal punishment for the people of major sins, and the believer does not abide eternally in the Fire, so he is neither a believer nor a disbeliever. This is the doctrine of the intermediate position (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn). They seceded from al-Hasan's circle, hence they were called Mu'tazila, and they are the Wa'idiyya. As for the one who says he is an idolater (mushrik), he says that because he performs some deeds for Allah and some for other than Him, so they become idolaters by their innovation, due to His saying, exalted: 'And does not associate anyone in the worship of his Lord' (Quran 18:110). Al-Hasan judged them as hypocrites due to the mentioned report.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This footnote provides the historical context for the sectarian positions on the grave sinner. It begins by stating that the disagreement occurred after the time of the Prophet. It recounts that the Khawarij, who declared the grave sinner a disbeliever, saw Ali killing many Muslims but praying over them, which they considered contradictory, so they disassociated from him. Then it discusses the time of al-Hasan al-Basri, where some held that faith is tasdiq (affirmation), and the responsible person (mukallaf) must either be a believer or a disbeliever; the sinner who affirms is a believer, and his punishment is either forgiveness or intercession or temporary punishment. These were the Murji'a and the Fudayliyya. Then it mentions that Wasil ibn 'Ata and 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd adopted the position that the grave sinner will be eternally in Hell, and since a believer is not eternally in Hell, he is neither believer nor disbeliever - the intermediate position (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn). They seceded from al-Hasan's circle, hence the name Mu'tazila. Then it explains that those who call him an idolater (mushrik) do so because he performs acts for both Allah and other than Allah, thus associating partners. Al-Hasan al-Basri judged them as hypocrites (munafiq) based on the hadith.

Key Terms
الخوارج
الفاسق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did the Khawarij consider Ali a sinner?
A) Because he did not pray
B) Because they found his actions contradictory
C) Because he fought the Prophet
D) Because he refused to judge

Key Takeaway
The Khawarij declared the grave sinner a disbeliever and applied this to Ali, leading to their dissociation from him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed the worst about someone's faith based on an action that seemed inconsistent to you? What does the Khawarij's mistake teach you about your own judgments? Will you commit to seeking a charitable interpretation before concluding?
?
The Khawarij held a single premise so strongly that it led them to condemn a companion of the Prophet. Is there a belief you hold so tightly that it might blind you to the truth about others? How can you protect yourself from such rigidity?
?
Seeing the Khawarij's error, can you identify a time when you prioritized consistency over mercy? How would you handle that situation differently now, knowing how easily a 'logical' conclusion can become a grave sin?

The Murji'a and Fudayliyya: Faith as Affirmation
The Murji'a/Fudayliyya definition of faith and treatment of the grave sinner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Murji'a and Fudayliyya position that the grave sinner is a believer because faith is only affirmation, and punishment is temporary.

Core Idea
For the Murji'a, faith is only affirmation, so even a sinful believer remains a believer.

During the time of al-Hasan al-Basri, a group argued that faith is simply affirmation (tasdiq). This led to the view that the grave sinner is still a believer, and his punishment can be temporary, forgiven, or interceded for. These groups became known as the Murji'a and Fudayliyya.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about al-Hasan's era
Read the Arabic and translation. This passage presents the view that faith is tasdiq (affirmation).
Note the logic: if you affirm, you are a believer; if not, you are a disbeliever.
2
Identify the three options for the sinner
The passage says the sinful believer's sin may be forgiven, interceded for, or punished temporarily.
This contrasts with eternal punishment.
3
Learn the names of these groups
They are called Murji'a (those who postpone judgment) and Fudayliyya.
Fudayliyya is a subgroup named after someone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) أقول : هذا الخلاف وقع بعد رسول الله ، والخوارج لما قالوا بتكفير الفاسق ورأوا عليا كرم وجهه يقتل جمعا من أهل القبلة ويصلى عليهم قالوا هذه مناقضة فهو فاق بسبب واحد من هذين الفعلين قطعا وتبرءوا عنه ، وفى زمن الحسن البصرى وقع هذا البحث بين أهل عصره فتمسك جماعة بأن الإيمان هـ التصديق والمكتب لا يخلو من أن يكون مصدقا بالله ورسوله أو لا يكون ، والثانى بالاتفق كافر ، والأول مؤمن ، ، والمصدق الفاسق يدخل تحت الأول فهو مؤمن وذنبه إما يغفر له أو يشفع فيه الذي لا أو يعذب هذا با منقطعا وهؤلاء هم المرجئة والفضيلية، وذهب واصل بن عطاء وعمرو من عبيد إلى أن صاحب الكبيرة يخلد في النار للآيات الدالة على تخليد عقوبة أهل الكبائر ، والمؤمن لا يخلد في النار فهو ليس بمؤمن ولا كافر ، وهذا هو القول بالمنزلة بين المنزلتين ، واعتزلوا عن خلقة الحسن ولدلك سموا بالمعتزلة وهم الوعيدية ، أما القائل بأنه مشرك فيقول ذلك لانه يعمل عملا الله وعملا لغيره فصاروا مشركين بمخلعتهم لقوله تعالى ولا يشرك بعبادة ربه أحدا ، والحسن حكم بنفاقهم للخبر المذكور .
(1) I say: This disagreement occurred after the Messenger of Allah. The Khawarij, when they declared the grave sinner a disbeliever and saw Ali (may Allah honor his face) killing a group of the people of the qibla and praying over them, said: 'This is contradictory; so he [Ali] is a sinner due to one of these two actions, definitely,' and they dissociated from him. In the time of al-Hasan al-Basri, this research occurred among the people of his era. Some held that faith (iman) is affirmation (tasdiq), and the responsible person [؟] must either be an affirmer of Allah and His Messenger or not; the second is by consensus a disbeliever, the first is a believer. The sinful affirmer falls under the first, so he is a believer, and his sin is either forgiven or interceded for, or he is punished temporarily. These are the Murji'a and the Fudayliyya. Wasil ibn 'Ata and 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd held that the grave sinner will abide eternally in the Fire, due to verses indicating eternal punishment for the people of major sins, and the believer does not abide eternally in the Fire, so he is neither a believer nor a disbeliever. This is the doctrine of the intermediate position (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn). They seceded from al-Hasan's circle, hence they were called Mu'tazila, and they are the Wa'idiyya. As for the one who says he is an idolater (mushrik), he says that because he performs some deeds for Allah and some for other than Him, so they become idolaters by their innovation, due to His saying, exalted: 'And does not associate anyone in the worship of his Lord' (Quran 18:110). Al-Hasan judged them as hypocrites due to the mentioned report.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This footnote provides the historical context for the sectarian positions on the grave sinner. It begins by stating that the disagreement occurred after the time of the Prophet. It recounts that the Khawarij, who declared the grave sinner a disbeliever, saw Ali killing many Muslims but praying over them, which they considered contradictory, so they disassociated from him. Then it discusses the time of al-Hasan al-Basri, where some held that faith is tasdiq (affirmation), and the responsible person (mukallaf) must either be a believer or a disbeliever; the sinner who affirms is a believer, and his punishment is either forgiveness or intercession or temporary punishment. These were the Murji'a and the Fudayliyya. Then it mentions that Wasil ibn 'Ata and 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd adopted the position that the grave sinner will be eternally in Hell, and since a believer is not eternally in Hell, he is neither believer nor disbeliever - the intermediate position (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn). They seceded from al-Hasan's circle, hence the name Mu'tazila. Then it explains that those who call him an idolater (mushrik) do so because he performs acts for both Allah and other than Allah, thus associating partners. Al-Hasan al-Basri judged them as hypocrites (munafiq) based on the hadith.

Key Terms

التصديق

Key Takeaway
The Murji'a and Fudayliyya considered faith as affirmation, so the grave sinner remains a believer with hope of forgiveness or temporary punishment.

?

The Origin of the Mu'tazila: The Intermediate Position
The Mu'tazila intermediate position and subsequent views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila originated from the intermediate position on the grave sinner and why they seceded from al-Hasan al-Basri.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila began when Wasil ibn 'Ata declared the grave sinner in an intermediate state between belief and disbelief.

Wasil ibn 'Ata and 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd proposed that the grave sinner is neither believer nor disbeliever but in an intermediate station. Their secession from al-Hasan's circle gave them the name Mu'tazila (those who withdraw). This lesson also covers the mushrik and munafiq positions that followed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Wasil and 'Amr
Read the Arabic and translation. They argue the grave sinner will be eternally punished but is not a disbeliever.
They use the premise that believers do not abide eternally in Hell.
2
Understand the intermediate position
Since the grave sinner will be eternally punished but a believer would not, he cannot be a believer; but he is not a disbeliever because he has faith. Hence, he is in between.
This is called al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn.
3
Note the secession and name
They seceded from al-Hasan al-Basri's circle, giving rise to the name Mu'tazila.
Also called Wa'idiyya because they emphasize threat (wa'id).
4
Read the additional positions
Then the passage mentions that some considered the grave sinner a mushrik (idolater) and al-Hasan judged them as hypocrites.
These are further refinements of the early debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) أقول : هذا الخلاف وقع بعد رسول الله ، والخوارج لما قالوا بتكفير الفاسق ورأوا عليا كرم وجهه يقتل جمعا من أهل القبلة ويصلى عليهم قالوا هذه مناقضة فهو فاق بسبب واحد من هذين الفعلين قطعا وتبرءوا عنه ، وفى زمن الحسن البصرى وقع هذا البحث بين أهل عصره فتمسك جماعة بأن الإيمان هـ التصديق والمكتب لا يخلو من أن يكون مصدقا بالله ورسوله أو لا يكون ، والثانى بالاتفق كافر ، والأول مؤمن ، ، والمصدق الفاسق يدخل تحت الأول فهو مؤمن وذنبه إما يغفر له أو يشفع فيه الذي لا أو يعذب هذا با منقطعا وهؤلاء هم المرجئة والفضيلية، وذهب واصل بن عطاء وعمرو من عبيد إلى أن صاحب الكبيرة يخلد في النار للآيات الدالة على تخليد عقوبة أهل الكبائر ، والمؤمن لا يخلد في النار فهو ليس بمؤمن ولا كافر ، وهذا هو القول بالمنزلة بين المنزلتين ، واعتزلوا عن خلقة الحسن ولدلك سموا بالمعتزلة وهم الوعيدية ، أما القائل بأنه مشرك فيقول ذلك لانه يعمل عملا الله وعملا لغيره فصاروا مشركين بمخلعتهم لقوله تعالى ولا يشرك بعبادة ربه أحدا ، والحسن حكم بنفاقهم للخبر المذكور .
(1) I say: This disagreement occurred after the Messenger of Allah. The Khawarij, when they declared the grave sinner a disbeliever and saw Ali (may Allah honor his face) killing a group of the people of the qibla and praying over them, said: 'This is contradictory; so he [Ali] is a sinner due to one of these two actions, definitely,' and they dissociated from him. In the time of al-Hasan al-Basri, this research occurred among the people of his era. Some held that faith (iman) is affirmation (tasdiq), and the responsible person [؟] must either be an affirmer of Allah and His Messenger or not; the second is by consensus a disbeliever, the first is a believer. The sinful affirmer falls under the first, so he is a believer, and his sin is either forgiven or interceded for, or he is punished temporarily. These are the Murji'a and the Fudayliyya. Wasil ibn 'Ata and 'Amr ibn 'Ubayd held that the grave sinner will abide eternally in the Fire, due to verses indicating eternal punishment for the people of major sins, and the believer does not abide eternally in the Fire, so he is neither a believer nor a disbeliever. This is the doctrine of the intermediate position (al-manzila bayn al-manzilatayn). They seceded from al-Hasan's circle, hence they were called Mu'tazila, and they are the Wa'idiyya. As for the one who says he is an idolater (mushrik), he says that because he performs some deeds for Allah and some for other than Him, so they become idolaters by their innovation, due to His saying, exalted: 'And does not associate anyone in the worship of his Lord' (Quran 18:110). Al-Hasan judged them as hypocrites due to the mentioned report.

This footnote provides the historical context for the sectarian positions on the grave sinner. It begins by stating that the disagreement occurred after the time of the Prophet. Then it explains that those who call him an idolater (mushrik) do so because he performs acts for both Allah and other than Allah, thus associating partners. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own sins, do you lean towards minimizing them (like the Murji'a) or despairing of Allah's mercy (like the Khawarij)? The Mu'tazila tried to find a middle path—but their solution led to division. Where in your life are you avoiding the full weight of your accountability before Allah? What one sin will you sincerely repent from today, trusting His mercy without excusing yourself?
?
The Mu'tazila separated from the community over a theological point. Is there a belief or practice where you have isolated yourself from others or judged them harshly? What would it cost you to seek unity with fellow Muslims while still holding firmly to the truth? Can you identify one step to bridge that gap this week?
?
Wasil and 'Amr used Quranic verses to justify their position, but they ended up with a conclusion that led to a new sect. Are you ever guilty of using the Quran selectively to support your own opinions rather than submitting to its full message? Ask Allah to purify your intention from following your desires, and commit to reading the Quran with a heart ready to obey even what challenges you.

Divisions on the Obligation of the Imamate
Al-Razi classifies sects on imamate obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the major Islamic sectarian positions on whether the imamate is obligatory and the basis (reason vs. revelation) for that obligation.

Core Idea
The obligation of imamate is debated across reason, revelation, and divine duty.

This passage from al-Razi presents a comprehensive classification of Islamic groups regarding the necessity of the imamate (leadership). It not only divides groups into those who affirm or deny obligation but also further subdivides based on whether the obligation is grounded in reason or revelation, and whether it falls on God or on the community. This classification sets the stage for theological arguments about the nature and necessity of leadership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main division
Read the first few lines to find the primary split: those who say the imamate is obligatory versus those who do not.
This is the overarching classification.
2
Map the subgroups under 'obligatory'
Notice the two branches: obligation by reason and obligation by revelation. Then further subdivisions: within reason, obligation upon God vs. upon creation.
Use the commentary to clarify each group's view.
3
Understand the Imamiyya arguments
The text lists three reasons given by the Imamiyya (Shi'a) for why the imamate is obligatory upon God: as a grace, as a teacher, and as a guide.
These correspond to Twelvers, Seveners, and a third view.
4
Note the groups that deny obligation
Identify the Khawarij and al-Asamm as those who do not consider the imamate obligatory.
Contrast with the majority view.
5
Consider the beginning of the proof
The passage starts an argument for obligation based on repelling harm, but it cuts off. Reflect on how this foundational argument might proceed.
The proof is incomplete, but the classification is the focus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الرابع في الإمامة ) فصل : منهم من قال بوجوبها ومنهم من لم يقل . أما القائلون بوجوبها، منهم من أوجبها عقلا ومنهم من أوجبها سمعا ، أما الموجبون عقلا فمنهم من أوجبها على الله تعالى ومنهم من أوجبها على الخلق . والذين أوجبوها على الله تعالى هم الإمامية ثم ذكروا في وجومها وجوها . أحدها: أن يكون لطفا فى الزجر عن المقبحات العقلية وهو قول الاثنى عشرية . والثاني : أن يكون معلما بمعرفة الله تعالى وهو قول السبعية . وثالثها : أن يعلمنا اللغات وأن يرشدنا إلى الأغذية ويميزها عن السموم . وأما الذين أوجبوها على غير الله تعالى فهو قول الجاحظ والكعى وأبى الحسين البصرى . وأما الذين أوجبوها سمعا فقط وهم جمهور أصحابنا وأكثر المعتزلة . وأما الذين لم يقولوا بوجوبها فهم الخوارج والأصم . لنا أن صب الإمام يتضمن دفع الضرر عن النفس فيسكون واجبا أما الأول فلانا نعلم إذا كان الخلق لهم
The Fourth Section: On the Imamate. Chapter: Among them are those who said it is obligatory (wajib) and among them are those who did not say so. As for those who said it is obligatory: among them are those who made it obligatory by reason ('aql) and among them are those who made it obligatory by revelation (sam'). As for those who made it obligatory by reason: among them are those who made it obligatory upon Allah (ta'ala) and among them are those who made it obligatory upon the creation (al-khalq). Those who made it obligatory upon Allah are the Imamiyya. Then they mentioned for its obligation (wujub) some aspects: One: that it is a grace (lutf) in deterring from rationally evil acts (al-muqabbihat al-'aqliyya) – this is the view of the Ithna 'Ashariyya (Twelvers). The second: that the imam is a teacher of the knowledge of Allah – this is the view of the Sab'iyya (Seveners). The third: that he teaches us languages, guides us to foods and distinguishes them from poisons. As for those who made it obligatory upon other than Allah, it is the view of al-Jahiz, al-Ka'bi, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri. As for those who made it obligatory by revelation only – they are the majority of our companions (Ashab) and most of the Mu'tazila. As for those who did not say it is obligatory, they are the Khawarij and al-Asamm. Our argument: that the appointment of the imam (nasb al-imam) includes repelling harm from the self, so it is obligatory. As for the first (argument) – for we know that if the creation...
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of the imamate (leadership). It first notes the division: some hold it obligatory, others do not. Among those who hold it obligatory, some say it is obligatory by reason, others by revelation. Those who say by reason further divide: some say it is obligatory upon Allah, others upon the creation. The Imamiyya (Shi'a) say it is obligatory upon Allah, and they give three reasons: as a grace to deter from evil (Twelvers), as a teacher of divine knowledge (Seveners), and as a guide in practical matters. Then it mentions those who say it is obligatory upon other than Allah (like Jahiz, Ka'bi, Abu al-Husayn al-Basri), and those who say it is obligatory only by revelation (majority of Ashab and Mu'tazila). Those who deny obligation are Khawarij and al-Asamm. The passage then begins a proof for obligation: the appointment of an imam repels harm, thus is obligatory. The argument is cut off.

5 min
The Spectrum of Views on the Obligation of the Imamate
Razi enumerates the various sectarian positions on the obligation of the imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the major Islamic sects' positions on whether the imamate is obligatory and the basis (reason vs. revelation) they use.

Core Idea
The obligation of the imamate is debated across multiple lines: rational vs. revelatory, and upon God vs. upon the community.

Imam al-Razi begins his discussion of the imamate by mapping out the landscape of opinions. Before evaluating arguments, he presents a taxonomy that shows where each major group stands. This passage introduces the key divisions: those who affirm obligation, those who deny it, and among the affirmers, the basis of obligation—reason or revelation—and for rational obligators, the subject of obligation—God or the people.

Guided Path
1
Identify the major dichotomy
The first split is between those who say the imamate is obligatory (الواجبون) and those who do not. Note the two groups: the Khawarij and al-Asamm.
2
Trace the rational obligators
Within the obligators, note the division between rational ('aqlan) and revelatory (sam'an) obligation. Then within rational obligation, some make it obligatory upon God (Imamiyyah) and some upon the people (al-Jahiz et al.).
3
Observe the Imamiyyah's three arguments
The Imamiyyah present three distinct rational proofs: as a deterrent grace (lutf), as a teacher of divine knowledge (Sab'iyyah), and as a teacher of worldly matters (extremists).
4
Note the majority view
Most Ash'aris and Mu'tazila hold that the imamate is obligatory by revelation only, not by reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( القسم الرابع في الإمامة ) فصل : منهم من قال بوجوبها ومنهم من لم يقل . أما القائلون بوجوبها، منهم من أوجبها عقلا ومنهم من أوجبها سمعا ، أما الموجبون عقلا فمنهم من أوجبها على الله تعالى ومنهم من أوجبها على الخلق . والذين أوجبوها على الله تعالى هم الإمامية ثم ذكروا في وجومها وجوها . أحدها: أن يكون لطفا فى الزجر عن المقبحات العقلية وهو قول الاثنى عشرية . والثاني : أن يكون معلما بمعرفة الله تعالى وهو قول السبعية · وثالثها : أن يعلمنا اللغات وأن يرشدنا إلى الأغذية ويميزها عن السموم . وأما الذين أوجبوها على غير الله تعالى فهو قول الجاحظ والكعى وأبى الحسين البصرى . وأما الذين أوجبوها سمعا فقط وهم جمهور أصحابنا وأكثر المعتزلة . وأما الذين لم يقولوا بوجوبها فهم الخوارج والأصم . (1) أقول: الإمامية يقولون نصب الإمام لطف، لأنه مقرب من الطاعة ومبعد عن المعصية واللطف واجب على الله تعالى، أما السبعية فلا يقولون بوجوب شيء على الله تعالى ولا بالحسن و القبح العقليين ولا يعدون في الإمامية إنما هم يقولون بأن التعليم واجب ومعرفة الله لا تحصل إلا بمجموع المظر والتعليم، ثم الشخص المتعين الإمامة تكون معرفة الله تعالى موقوفة على معرفته وكل ما يأمر به هو فهو واجب، وطاعة وكل ما ينهى عنه معصية وقبح أو محرم، وسموهم بالسبعية لأن متقدميهم قالوا الأئمة سبعة، وعند السابع وهو محمد ابن إسماعيل توقف بعضهم عليه، وجاوزه بعضهم وقالو الأئمة يزيدون على سبعة، سبعة كأيام الأسبوع، والذين قالوا الإمام يعلمنا اللغات والأغذية فهم من الغلاة وليس هذان الصنفان من الإمامية.
(The fourth section on the Imamate. A chapter: Among them are those who said it is obligatory and among them are those who did not say so. As for those who said it is obligatory: among them are those who made it obligatory by reason ('aqlan) and among them are those who made it obligatory by revelation (sam'an). As for those who made it obligatory by reason: among them are those who made it obligatory upon Allah the Exalted and among them are those who made it obligatory upon the creation. And those who made it obligatory upon Allah the Exalted are the Imamiyyah; then they mentioned for its obligation aspects: One: that it is a grace (lutf) in deterrence from rational abominations, and that is the statement of the Twelvers. Second: that he (the imam) is a teacher of the knowledge of Allah, and that is the statement of the Sab'iyyah. Third: that he teaches us languages and guides us to foods and distinguishes them from poisons. As for those who made it obligatory upon other than Allah, that is the statement of al-Jahiz, al-Ka'bi, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri. As for those who made it obligatory by revelation only, they are the majority of our companions and most of the Mu'tazila. As for those who did not say it is obligatory, they are the Khawarij and al-Asamm. (1) I say: The Imamiyyah say that the appointment of the imam is a grace (lutf), because it brings near to obedience and distances from disobedience, and grace is obligatory upon Allah. As for the Sab'iyyah, they do not say that anything is obligatory upon Allah, nor do they affirm rational good and evil, and they are not counted among the Imamiyyah. Rather, they say that teaching is obligatory and knowledge of Allah is not attained except through the combination of reflection and teaching. Then the person specified for the imamate, knowledge of Allah the Exalted is dependent on knowledge of him, and everything he commands is obligatory and obedience, and everything he forbids is disobedience and evil or forbidden. They are called Sab'iyyah because their predecessors said the imams are seven, and upon the seventh, Muhammad ibn Isma'il, some of them stopped, and some went beyond them and said the imams exceed seven, seven like the days of the week. And those who said the imam teaches us languages and foods are among the extremists (ghulat), and these two groups are not from the Imamiyyah.)
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a classification (taqsim) of the various Islamic sects' positions on the obligation of the imamate. It first divides the community into those who affirm obligation and those who deny it. Among the affirmers, there are those who base it on reason ('aql) and those who base it on revelation (sam'). The rational obligators are further split: some hold that the imam is obligatory upon God (i.e., God must appoint one), which is the view of the Imamiyyah; others hold it is obligatory upon the people, a view held by al-Jahiz and others. The Imamiyyah themselves give three rational arguments: as a deterrent grace (lutf), as a teacher of divine knowledge (Sab'iyyah), or as a teacher of practical matters (extremists). The passage then lists those who base it on revelation only (majority of Ash'aris and Mu'tazila) and those who deny obligation entirely (Khawarij and al-Asamm). The note (1) expands on the Imamiyyah and Sab'iyyah views, explaining the concept of lutf and that the Sab'iyyah reject the notion of anything being obligatory upon God and do not affirm rational good and evil. It clarifies that the Sab'iyyah are not part of the Imamiyyah and that the extreme view of teaching languages is from the ghulat.

Key Terms
إمامة
وجوب
عقل
سمع
إمامية
سبعية
غلاة
اثنى عشرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Imamiyyah hold the same reason for the imamate's obligation.
Correction: The passage shows three distinct rational arguments within the Imamiyyah: lutf (Twelvers), teaching divine knowledge (Sab'iyyah), and teaching practical matters (extremists).

Checkpoint
According to the classification, which group holds that the imam is obligatory by revelation only?
A) Imamiyyah
B) Khawarij
C) Ash'aris and most Mu'tazila
D) Sab'iyyah

Key Takeaway
The debate on the imamate's obligation involves multiple axes: rational vs. revelatory foundation, and whether the obligation falls on God or on the community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see the diversity of views on the imamate, which position resonates most with your own understanding of religious leadership? Why? Are you willing to honestly examine the assumptions behind your preference?
?
The Imamiyyah argued that God must appoint an imam as a grace (lutf). Do you believe that God owes anything to creation? How does your answer affect your trust in God's wisdom and justice?
?
The Khawarij denied the obligation of the imamate altogether. Could this be motivated by a desire to avoid religious authority? Search your heart: do you ever resent or resist religious obligations because they restrict your freedom? What would it take for you to embrace them wholeheartedly?

The Three Rational Arguments of the Imamiyyah
Razi enumerates the three rational proofs of the Imamiyyah for the obligation of the imamate upon God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three distinct rational proofs the Imamiyyah use to argue that the imamate is obligatory upon God.

Core Idea
The Imamiyyah split into three camps on why God must appoint an imam: as a grace, as a teacher, or as a guide for worldly needs.

In the previous lesson we saw the broad taxonomy. Here we zoom in on the Imamiyyah, who believe the imamate is obligatory upon God. Imam al-Razi lists three distinct rational arguments they put forward, each associated with a different subgroup: the Twelvers, the Sab'iyyah, and the extremists. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for assessing the internal diversity of Imami thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the three arguments
The passage presents three 'وجوه' (aspects) for the Imamiyyah's position. Identify each one and note the group associated with it.
2
Understand the first: lutf (grace)
The Twelvers argue that the imam is a 'grace' (lutf) that deters from wrongdoing. This is a key concept in Shi'i theology.
3
Understand the second: teaching divine knowledge
The Sab'iyyah hold that the imam is necessary as a teacher (mu'allim) for knowledge of God, which cannot be attained by reason alone.
4
Understand the third: teaching practical matters
The extremists (ghulat) hold that the imam teaches languages and distinguishes foods from poisons, a more mundane function.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( القسم الرابع في الإمامة ) فصل : منهم من قال بوجوبها ومنهم من لم يقل . أما القائلون بوجوبها، منهم من أوجبها عقلا ومنهم من أوجبها سمعا ، أما الموجبون عقلا فمنهم من أوجبها على الله تعالى ومنهم من أوجبها على الخلق . والذين أوجبوها على الله تعالى هم الإمامية ثم ذكروا في وجومها وجوها . أحدها: أن يكون لطفا فى الزجر عن المقبحات العقلية وهو قول الاثنى عشرية . والثاني : أن يكون معلما بمعرفة الله تعالى وهو قول السبعية · وثالثها : أن يعلمنا اللغات وأن يرشدنا إلى الأغذية ويميزها عن السموم . وأما الذين أوجبوها على غير الله تعالى فهو قول الجاحظ والكعى وأبى الحسين البصرى . وأما الذين أوجبوها سمعا فقط وهم جمهور أصحابنا وأكثر المعتزلة . وأما الذين لم يقولوا بوجوبها فهم الخوارج والأصم . (1) أقول: الإمامية يقولون نصب الإمام لطف، لأنه مقرب من الطاعة ومبعد عن المعصية واللطف واجب على الله تعالى، أما السبعية فلا يقولون بوجوب شيء على الله تعالى ولا بالحسن و القبح العقليين ولا يعدون في الإمامية إنما هم يقولون بأن التعليم واجب ومعرفة الله لا تحصل إلا بمجموع المظر والتعليم، ثم الشخص المتعين الإمامة تكون معرفة الله تعالى موقوفة على معرفته وكل ما يأمر به هو فهو واجب، وطاعة وكل ما ينهى عنه معصية وقبح أو محرم، وسموهم بالسبعية لأن متقدميهم قالوا الأئمة سبعة، وعند السابع وهو محمد ابن إسماعيل توقف بعضهم عليه، وجاوزه بعضهم وقالو الأئمة يزيدون على سبعة، سبعة كأيام الأسبوع، والذين قالوا الإمام يعلمنا اللغات والأغذية فهم من الغلاة وليس هذان الصنفان من الإمامية.
(The fourth section on the Imamate. A chapter: Among them are those who said it is obligatory and among them are those who did not say so. As for those who said it is obligatory: among them are those who made it obligatory by reason ('aqlan) and among them are those who made it obligatory by revelation (sam'an). As for those who made it obligatory by reason: among them are those who made it obligatory upon Allah the Exalted and among them are those who made it obligatory upon the creation. And those who made it obligatory upon Allah the Exalted are the Imamiyyah; then they mentioned for its obligation aspects: One: that it is a grace (lutf) in deterrence from rational abominations, and that is the statement of the Twelvers. Second: that he (the imam) is a teacher of the knowledge of Allah, and that is the statement of the Sab'iyyah. Third: that he teaches us languages and guides us to foods and distinguishes them from poisons. As for those who made it obligatory upon other than Allah, that is the statement of al-Jahiz, al-Ka'bi, and Abu al-Husayn al-Basri. As for those who made it obligatory by revelation only, they are the majority of our companions and most of the Mu'tazila. As for those who did not say it is obligatory, they are the Khawarij and al-Asamm. (1) I say: The Imamiyyah say that the appointment of the imam is a grace (lutf), because it brings near to obedience and distances from disobedience, and grace is obligatory upon Allah. As for the Sab'iyyah, they do not say that anything is obligatory upon Allah, nor do they affirm rational good and evil, and they are not counted among the Imamiyyah. Rather, they say that teaching is obligatory and knowledge of Allah is not attained except through the combination of reflection and teaching. Then the person specified for the imamate, knowledge of Allah the Exalted is dependent on knowledge of him, and everything he commands is obligatory and obedience, and everything he forbids is disobedience and evil or forbidden. They are called Sab'iyyah because their predecessors said the imams are seven, and upon the seventh, Muhammad ibn Isma'il, some of them stopped, and some went beyond them and said the imams exceed seven, seven like the days of the week. And those who said the imam teaches us languages and foods are among the extremists (ghulat), and these two groups are not from the Imamiyyah.)
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a classification (taqsim) of the various Islamic sects' positions on the obligation of the imamate. It first divides the community into those who affirm obligation and those who deny it. Among the affirmers, there are those who base it on reason ('aql) and those who base it on revelation (sam'). The rational obligators are further split: some hold that the imam is obligatory upon God (i.e., God must appoint one), which is the view of the Imamiyyah; others hold it is obligatory upon the people, a view held by al-Jahiz and others. The Imamiyyah themselves give three rational arguments: as a deterrent grace (lutf), as a teacher of divine knowledge (Sab'iyyah), or as a teacher of practical matters (extremists). The passage then lists those who base it on revelation only (majority of Ash'aris and Mu'tazila) and those who deny obligation entirely (Khawarij and al-Asamm). The note (1) expands on the Imamiyyah and Sab'iyyah views, explaining the concept of lutf and that the Sab'iyyah reject the notion of anything being obligatory upon God and do not affirm rational good and evil. It clarifies that the Sab'iyyah are not part of the Imamiyyah and that the extreme view of teaching languages is from the ghulat.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sab'iyyah are considered part of the Imamiyyah.
Correction: Razi notes in the following passage that the Sab'iyyah are not counted among the Imamiyyah because they reject the obligation of things upon God and do not affirm rational good and evil.

Checkpoint
Which Imamiyyah subgroup argues that the imam is a grace (lutf) deterring from rational abominations?
A) Sab'iyyah
B) Twelvers
C) Extremists
D) Khawarij

Key Takeaway
The Imamiyyah's three arguments for the imamate's obligation upon God show internal diversity: grace (lutf), teaching divine knowledge, and teaching worldly matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Twelvers argue that the imam's grace (lutf) deters you from rational evils. In your daily life, what deters you from sin? Is your deterrence rooted in a divinely appointed guide, or something else? How certain are you that your deterrence is sufficient before Allah?
?
The Sab'iyyah claim that knowledge of Allah cannot be attained without a teacher. Have you ever truly sought knowledge of Allah as an end in itself, or do you seek it only for worldly benefit? Examine your intentions: is knowing Allah your ultimate goal, or is it a means to other ends?
?
The extremists argue that the imam teaches worldly necessities like distinguishing food from poison. Reflect on your reliance on worldly knowledge versus religious knowledge. Which do you prioritize more in your daily actions? If your schedule were a mirror, would it reflect a pursuit of Allah's guidance or merely worldly security?

Clarifying the Imamiyyah and Sab'iyyah Positions
Razi clarifies the theological positions of the Imamiyyah and Sab'iyyah on the imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise theological positions of the Imamiyyah and the Sab'iyyah on the imamate, including the concept of lutf and the Sab'iyyah's rejection of obligations upon God.

Core Idea
The Sab'iyyah are not Imamiyyah; they reject that anything is obligatory upon God and do not affirm rational good and evil.

After listing the three arguments, Imam al-Razi adds an important clarification. He explains the Imamiyyah's concept of lutf in more detail and then distinguishes the Sab'iyyah from the Imamiyyah, highlighting their different theological foundations. He also describes the origin of the name 'Sab'iyyah' and notes that the extremists who teach practical matters are not part of the Imamiyyah. This passage is essential for avoiding confusion between these groups.

Guided Path
1
Read the note carefully
The passage begins with 'أقول' (I say), indicating it is Razi's own explanation. Focus on how he defines the Imamiyyah's lutf argument.
2
Understand the Imamiyyah's lutf concept
Razi explains that the Imamiyyah say the appointment of the imam is a grace (lutf) that brings near to obedience and distances from disobedience, and lutf is obligatory upon God.
3
Contrast with the Sab'iyyah
The Sab'iyyah do not affirm that anything is obligatory upon God, nor do they affirm rational good and evil. They base their view on the need for a teacher for divine knowledge.
4
Note the origin of 'Sab'iyyah'
They are called Sab'iyyah because their early leaders said there are seven imams. Some stop at the seventh, others continue.
5
Identify the extremist view
Those who say the imam teaches languages and foods are from the extremists (ghulat), not from the Imamiyyah.

The Rational Proof for the Imamate
Razi presents a rational proof for the obligation of the imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument for the obligation of the imamate based on harm prevention.

Core Idea
Appointing an imam prevents harm, and preventing harm is obligatory.

Imam Razi begins by presenting a rational proof for the obligation of the imamate. This proof relies on the idea that having a powerful ruler deters people from corruption, thereby repelling harm. Since repelling harm is obligatory by consensus or rational necessity, the imamate becomes obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Understand the structure of the proof
The proof has a minor premise (appointing an imam prevents harm) and a major premise (preventing harm is obligatory).
2
Examine the minor premise
Razi argues that people with a powerful ruler avoid corruption more completely than without one.
3
Examine the major premise
Preventing harm is obligatory either by consensus or by the necessity of reason.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that appointing an imam is obligatory.
This is a typical kalam argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا أن صب الإمام يتضمن دفع الضرر عن النفس فيسكون واجبا أما الأول فلانا نعلم إذا كان الخلق لهم وأما رئيس قاهر يخافون بطشه ويرجون لوا به كان احتراز هم عن المفاسد أتم مما إذا لم يكن لهم هذا الرئيس إن دفع الضرر عن النفس واجب فبالإجماع عند من يقول بالوجوب المقلى وبضرورة العقل عند من يقول به (1) والدليل الذى جاء به المصنف على وجوب الإمامية سمعا فصغراء عقلى من باب الحسن والقبح وهو ليس من مذهبه ، وكبراءه التي أحالها إلى الإجماع أوضح عقلا من الصغرى، والأولى أن يعتمد على قوله تعالى : « أطيعوا الله وأطيه والرسول وأولى الأمر منكم ، وعلى قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : من مات ولم يعرف إمام زمانه مات ميتة جاهلية ، وعلى أمثال ذلك ، ومن الظاهر أن أصحاب النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وآله بعد وفاته أجمعوا على طاعة إمام بعده فذهب بعضهم إلى أنه نص صلى الله عليه وسلم على على كرم الله وجهه ، وبعضهم قالوا إنا ننصب إماما ونصبوا أبا بكر رضى الله عنه وبايعوه جميعا وبايعه على رضى الله عنه أيضاً ، ولو لم يكن نصب إمام واجبا لمخالفهم من الأمة أحد فى ذلك ، ثم اجتمعوا على عمر رضى الله عنه بنص أبي بكر رضى ثم على عثمان رضی الله عنه بسبب الشورى ، ثم على على رضى الله عنه لإجماع أكثر أهل الحل والعقد عليه ، وعرف من ذلك أن الإمام ينصب إما بنص من الذى قبله وإما باختيار أهل الحل والعقد إياه وهذا هو العمدة عند أهل السنة ، ولم يذكره المصنف رحمه الله تعالى في هذا الكتاب .
For us [the proof]: The appointment of the imam includes repelling harm from oneself, so it is obligatory. As for the first [premise], because we know that if the creation have a powerful ruler whose assault they fear and whose benevolence they hope for, their avoidance of corruptions is more complete than if they did not have this ruler. Repelling harm from oneself is obligatory by consensus among those who affirm rational obligation and by necessity of reason among those who affirm it. (1) The proof that the author brought for the obligation of the imamate by revelation – its minor premise is rational from the category of good and evil, and it is not from his school, and its major premise, which he referred to consensus, is more evidently rational than the minor. And it is better to rely on His statement: 'Obey Allah and obey the Messenger and those in authority among you' and on his statement, peace be upon him: 'Whoever dies and does not know the imam of his time dies a death of ignorance' and similar to that. And it is apparent that the Companions of the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him and his family) after his death agreed upon obeying an imam after him. Some of them held that the Prophet explicitly designated 'Ali, and some said we appoint an imam, and they appointed Abu Bakr and all gave allegiance, and 'Ali also gave allegiance. And if the appointment of an imam were not obligatory, none of the community would have opposed them in that. Then they agreed upon 'Umar by the designation of Abu Bakr, then upon 'Uthman due to the consultation (shura), then upon 'Ali due to the consensus of most of the people of loosening and binding. And it is known from this that the imam is appointed either by designation from the previous one or by the choice of the people of loosening and binding. And this is the relied-upon [view] according to Ahl al-Sunnah, and the author did not mention it in this book.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents a rational argument (proof) for the obligation of the imamate. The argument is: appointing an imam prevents harm (since a strong ruler deters corruption), and preventing harm is obligatory; therefore, the imamate is obligatory. The minor premise (that an imam prevents harm) is supported by observation: people with a powerful ruler avoid corruption more. The major premise (that repelling harm is obligatory) is established either by consensus or rational necessity. The note (1) then critiques this proof. It points out that the minor premise is based on rational good and evil (husn wa qubh) – a Mu'tazilite principle, not from the author's Ash'ari school. The major premise, which relies on consensus, is more rationally sound. The note then recommends better evidence: the Qur'anic verse (4:59), the hadith about knowing the imam of one's time, and the historical consensus of the Companions after the Prophet's death. It recounts how the Companions agreed on the need for an imam, though they differed on whether it was by designation or election. The note concludes that the Sunni view relies on either explicit designation or election by the people of loosening and binding, and regrets that the author did not mention this standard Sunni proof.

Key Terms
نصب الإمام
دفع الضرر
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the rational proof is the only evidence for the imamate.
Correction: It is one proof, but it has weaknesses as the next lesson shows.

Checkpoint
What is the minor premise in Razi's rational proof?
A) Appointing an imam is obligatory by revelation
B) Appointing an imam prevents harm
C) Preventing harm is obligatory
D) The imamate is necessary

Key Takeaway
Razi's rational proof for the imamate is based on harm prevention, using a two-premise argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine a ruler whose power you fear and whose kindness you hope for, does that image move you to avoid corruption more than thinking of Allah's knowledge alone? What does that reveal about your heart's true reliance?
?
The proof says repelling harm from oneself is obligatory. What harm in your own soul—a bad habit, a hidden sin, an ignored duty—have you been tolerating instead of repelling? Why haven't you acted?
?
If the imamate is necessary to prevent societal harm, what does your lack of concern for righteous leadership say about your responsibility toward the Muslims around you? Will you seek to be part of the solution, or remain passive?

Critique and Better Evidence for the Imamate
Razi critiques the rational proof and offers scriptural evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rational proof is considered weak by the author and what evidence is preferred.

Core Idea
Scriptural evidence (Qur'an, hadith, consensus) is stronger than rational proof based on good and evil.

After presenting the rational proof, Razi critiques it, noting that its minor premise relies on rational good and evil – a principle not accepted in his Ash'ari school. He then recommends relying on scriptural evidence: the Qur'anic verse about obedience, the hadith about knowing the imam, and the historical consensus of the Companions.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique
The note points out that the minor premise is based on rational good and evil, which is not from the author's school.
2
Identify the preferred evidence
Razi recommends the Qur'anic verse (4:59), the hadith about the imam, and the consensus of the Companions.
3
Understand the historical consensus
The Companions agreed on the need for an imam after the Prophet's death, though they differed on how to appoint.
4
Learn the Sunni view
The imam is appointed either by designation or by election of the people of loosening and binding.
This is the standard Sunni position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا أن صب الإمام يتضمن دفع الضرر عن النفس فيسكون واجبا أما الأول فلانا نعلم إذا كان الخلق لهم وأما رئيس قاهر يخافون بطشه ويرجون لوا به كان احتراز هم عن المفاسد أتم مما إذا لم يكن لهم هذا الرئيس إن دفع الضرر عن النفس واجب فبالإجماع عند من يقول بالوجوب المقلى وبضرورة العقل عند من يقول به (1) والدليل الذى جاء به المصنف على وجوب الإمامية سمعا فصغراء عقلى من باب الحسن والقبح وهو ليس من مذهبه ، وكبراءه التي أحالها إلى الإجماع أوضح عقلا من الصغرى، والأولى أن يعتمد على قوله تعالى : « أطيعوا الله وأطيه والرسول وأولى الأمر منكم ، وعلى قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : من مات ولم يعرف إمام زمانه مات ميتة جاهلية ، وعلى أمثال ذلك ، ومن الظاهر أن أصحاب النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وآله بعد وفاته أجمعوا على طاعة إمام بعده فذهب بعضهم إلى أنه نص صلى الله عليه وسلم على على كرم الله وجهه ، وبعضهم قالوا إنا ننصب إماما ونصبوا أبا بكر رضى الله عنه وبايعوه جميعا وبايعه على رضى الله عنه أيضاً ، ولو لم يكن نصب إمام واجبا لمخالفهم من الأمة أحد فى ذلك ، ثم اجتمعوا على عمر رضى الله عنه بنص أبي بكر رضى ثم على عثمان رضی الله عنه بسبب الشورى ، ثم على على رضى الله عنه لإجماع أكثر أهل الحل والعقد عليه ، وعرف من ذلك أن الإمام ينصب إما بنص من الذى قبله وإما باختيار أهل الحل والعقد إياه وهذا هو العمدة عند أهل السنة ، ولم يذكره المصنف رحمه الله تعالى في هذا الكتاب .
For us [the proof]: The appointment of the imam includes repelling harm from oneself, so it is obligatory. As for the first [premise], because we know that if the creation have a powerful ruler whose assault they fear and whose benevolence they hope for, their avoidance of corruptions is more complete than if they did not have this ruler. Repelling harm from oneself is obligatory by consensus among those who affirm rational obligation and by necessity of reason among those who affirm it. (1) The proof that the author brought for the obligation of the imamate by revelation – its minor premise is rational from the category of good and evil, and it is not from his school, and its major premise, which he referred to consensus, is more evidently rational than the minor. And it is better to rely on His statement: 'Obey Allah and obey the Messenger and those in authority among you' and on his statement, peace be upon him: 'Whoever dies and does not know the imam of his time dies a death of ignorance' and similar to that. And it is apparent that the Companions of the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him and his family) after his death agreed upon obeying an imam after him. Some of them held that the Prophet explicitly designated 'Ali, and some said we appoint an imam, and they appointed Abu Bakr and all gave allegiance, and 'Ali also gave allegiance. And if the appointment of an imam were not obligatory, none of the community would have opposed them in that. Then they agreed upon 'Umar by the designation of Abu Bakr, then upon 'Uthman due to the consultation (shura), then upon 'Ali due to the consensus of most of the people of loosening and binding. And it is known from this that the imam is appointed either by designation from the previous one or by the choice of the people of loosening and binding. And this is the relied-upon [view] according to Ahl al-Sunnah, and the author did not mention it in this book.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents a rational argument (proof) for the obligation of the imamate. The argument is: appointing an imam prevents harm (since a strong ruler deters corruption), and preventing harm is obligatory; therefore, the imamate is obligatory. The minor premise (that an imam prevents harm) is supported by observation: people with a powerful ruler avoid corruption more. The major premise (that repelling harm is obligatory) is established either by consensus or rational necessity. The note (1) then critiques this proof. It points out that the minor premise is based on rational good and evil (husn wa qubh) – a Mu'tazilite principle, not from the author's Ash'ari school. The major premise, which relies on consensus, is more rationally sound. The note then recommends better evidence: the Qur'anic verse (4:59), the hadith about knowing the imam of one's time, and the historical consensus of the Companions after the Prophet's death. It recounts how the Companions agreed on the need for an imam, though they differed on whether it was by designation or election. The note concludes that the Sunni view relies on either explicit designation or election by the people of loosening and binding, and regrets that the author did not mention this standard Sunni proof.

Key Terms

إجماع
نص
أهل الحل والعقد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the rational proof is the strongest evidence for the imamate.
Correction: Razi shows it is weak because it relies on Mu'tazilite principles; scriptural evidence is stronger.

Checkpoint
What does Razi recommend as better evidence for the obligation of the imamate?
A) Rational good and evil
B) The consensus of the Mu'tazilites
C) The Qur'anic verse, hadith, and historical consensus
D) The argument from lutf

Key Takeaway
Scriptural evidence and historical consensus provide stronger support for the imamate than rational arguments based on good and evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a religious obligation, do you rely more on rational arguments or on clear scriptural commands? Which has more weight in your heart right now?
?
The Companions differed on the method of appointing an imam, yet they united in obedience. Is there a similar area in your life where you are more focused on the method than on the unity and obedience required by Allah?
?
If you were to die today, would you truly know the Imam of your time? And what would that knowledge obligate you to change in your daily actions?

Classification of Shia Sects and the Imamiyyah Imam List
Razi classifies Shia into four sub-sects and lists the twelve Imams of the Imamiyyah with a difference on nass.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi classifies Shia into four sub-sects and the Imamiyyah's list of twelve Imams along with their internal difference on the nature of 'Ali's authority.

Core Idea
The Shia are not monolithic; they have four distinct groups with different imam lists and doctrines.

Razi begins by categorizing the Shia into four main types, clarifying that they are not a single sect. He then focuses on the Imamiyyah, presenting their twelve-imam lineage and noting a key disagreement among them about whether 'Ali was explicitly designated or had discretionary authority. This passage gives us a snapshot of early sectarian divisions within Shiism.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Identify the four types of Shia mentioned in the first sentence.
The four types are: Imamiyyah, Kaysaniyyah, Zaydiyyah, and Ghulat.
2
Study the Imam list
Note the names of the twelve Imams in order as listed by the Imamiyyah.
The list ends with Muhammad al-Mahdi, the awaited Qa'im.
3
Understand the difference
Compare the two views on nass jali (explicit designation) versus delegated authority concerning 'Ali.
One group says 'Ali was explicitly designated; another says he could delegate the imamate.

( مسألة : الشيعة جنس تحته أربعة أنواع الإمامية والكيسانية والزيدية والغلاة الإمامية ) فالذين استقر رأيهم على أن الإمام بعد الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام على بن أبي طالب ثم ولده الحسن ثم أخوه الحسين ثم ابنه على ثم ابنه محمد الباقر ثم ابنه جعفر الصادق ثم ابنه موسى الكاظم ثم ابنه على الرضا ثم ابنه محمد المتقى ثم ابنه على التقى ثم ابنه الحسن الزكى ثم ابنه محمد وهو القائم المنتظر رضوان الله عليهم أجمعين . وقد كان لهم في هذه المراتب اختلافات فيقول القائلون بالنص الجلى على على بن أبى طالب رضى الله عنه اتفقوا على أنه متمين للإمامة . وعن فرقة الإمامية أنهم قالوا الأمر بعد الذي عليه الصلاة والسلام إلى على بن أبي طالب يفعل في الإمامة ما أحب ، إن شاء جعلها لنفسه وإن شاء ولاها غيره . ( ) انظر في تفصيل ذلك, اعتقادات فرق المسلمين والمشركين ، بتحقيقها . الله عنه عليه . ( ۳۱ - مدل )
(Issue: The Shia are a genus under which are four types: the Imamiyyah, the Kaysaniyyah, the Zaydiyyah, and the Ghulat (extremists) of the Imamiyyah.) Those whose opinion settled that the imam after the Messenger (peace and blessings upon him) is 'Ali ibn Abi Talib, then his son al-Hasan, then his brother al-Husayn, then his son 'Ali, then his son Muhammad al-Baqir, then his son Ja'far al-Sadiq, then his son Musa al-Kadhim, then his son 'Ali al-Rida, then his son Muhammad al-Taqi, then his son 'Ali al-Taqi, then his son al-Hasan al-Zaki, then his son Muhammad who is the awaited Qa'im, may Allah be pleased with them all. And they had differences regarding these ranks. Those who affirm explicit designation (nass jali) concerning 'Ali ibn Abi Talib (may Allah be pleased with him) agreed that he is the one who completes the imamate. And from a faction of the Imamiyyah, they said that the authority after the Messenger (upon him peace) belongs to 'Ali ibn Abi Talib; he may do in the imamate as he wishes: if he wishes, he may take it for himself, and if he wishes, he may delegate it to another. ( ) See the details of that in 'I'tiqadat firaq al-muslimin wa al-mushrikin' with its verification. Allah (be pleased) with him upon him. (31 - madl?)
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage is a classification of Shia sects and a presentation of the Imamiyyah doctrine. It states that the Shia are divided into four types: Imamiyyah (those who believe in a series of imams), Kaysaniyyah (followers of al-Mukhtar and Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyyah), Zaydiyyah (followers of Zayd ibn 'Ali), and the Ghulat (extremists who exaggerate the status of the imams). It then gives the specific list of the twelve Imams as held by the Imamiyyah: 'Ali, Hasan, Husayn, 'Ali Zayn al-Abidin, Muhammad al-Baqir, Ja'far al-Sadiq, Musa al-Kadhim, 'Ali al-Rida, Muhammad al-Taqi, 'Ali al-Taqi, Hasan al-Askari, and Muhammad al-Mahdi. It notes differences among them: some hold that 'Ali was explicitly designated (nass jali) and that he is the one who completes the imamate, while another faction holds that after the Prophet, the authority was given to 'Ali to do as he wished—he could take the imamate himself or delegate it to another. The passage ends with a reference to another work for details.

Classification of Shia Sects and the Twelve Imams
Classifies Shia into four types and lists the twelve Imamiyya imams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four main Shia sects and the Imamiyya's line of twelve imams, including their internal differences on how the imam is appointed.

Core Idea
The Shia are not monolithic; they divide into four main groups with distinct views on the imamate.

This passage from Imam al-Razi's Muhassal provides a concise classification of Shia sects. It lists the four major types and then details the Imamiyya's succession of twelve imams, highlighting a key disagreement on whether the imam is explicitly designated by text or has authority to appoint his successor.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification
Start by reading the first sentence where al-Razi states that the Shi'a are a genus with four types. Note the names: Imamiyya, Kaysaniyya, Zaydiyya, and Ghulat.
This sets the stage for the rest of the passage.
2
List the twelve Imams
Read the list of imams from Ali ibn Abi Talib to Muhammad al-Mahdi. Observe the father-son succession and the end with the Qa'im al-Muntazar.
This is the Imamiyya's line.
3
Identify the internal difference
Notice that the Imamiyya had differences. The passage mentions two views: those who assert explicit designation (nass jali) and those who say the imam after the Prophet has authority to appoint his successor.
This shows diversity within the Imamiyya.
4
Reflect on the classification
Consider how this classification helps in understanding the broader Shia landscape and the variety of opinions on the imamate.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الشيعة جنس تحته أربعة أنواع الإمامية والكيسانية والزيدية والغلاة الإمامية ) فالذين استقر رأيهم على أن الإمام بعد الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام على بن أبي طالب ثم ولده الحسن ثم أخوه الحسين ثم ابنه على ثم ابنه محمد الباقر ثم ابنه جعفر الصادق ثم ابنه موسى الكاظم ثم ابنه على الرضا ثم ابنه محمد المتقى ثم ابنه على التقى ثم ابنه الحسن الزكى ثم ابنه محمد وهو القائم المنتظر رضوان الله عليهم أجمعين . وقد كان لهم في هذه المراتب اختلافات فيقول القائلون بالنص الجلى على على بن أبى طالب رضى الله عنه اتفقوا على أنه متمين للإمامة . وعن فرقة الإمامية أنهم قالوا الأمر بعد الذي عليه الصلاة والسلام إلى على بن أبي طالب يفعل في الإمامة ما أحب ، إن شاء جعلها لنفسه وإن شاء ولاها غيره . (1) أقول الإمامية يقولون نصب الإمام لطف ، لأنه مقرب من الطاعة ومبعد عن المعصية واللطف واجب على الله تعالى ، أما السبعية فلا يقولون بوجوب شيء على الله تعالى ولا بالحسن و القبح العقليين ولا يعدون في الإمامية إنما هم يقولون بأن التعليم واجب ومعرفة الله لا تحصل إلا بمجموع المظر والتعليم، ثم الشخص المتعين الإمامة تكون معرفة الله تعالى موقوفة على معرفته وكل ما يأمر به هو فهو واجب ، وطاعة وكل ما ينهى عنه معصية وقبح أو محرم، وسموهم بالسبعية لأن متقدميهم قالوا الأئمة سبعة ، وعند السابع وهو محمد ابن إسماعيل توقف بعضهم عليه ، وجاوزه بعضهم وقالو الأئمة يزيدون على سبعة ، سبعة كأيام الأسبوع ، والذين قالوا الإمام يعلمنا اللغات والأغذية فهم من الغلاة وليس هذان الصنفان من الإمامية .
Issue: The Shi'a are a genus under which there are four types: the Imamiyya, the Kaysaniyya, the Zaydiyya, and the Ghulat. The Imamiyya are those whose view settled that the Imam after the Messenger, peace be upon him, is Ali ibn Abi Talib, then his son al-Hasan, then his brother al-Husayn, then his son Ali, then his son Muhammad al-Baqir, then his son Ja'far al-Sadiq, then his son Musa al-Kazim, then his son Ali al-Rida, then his son Muhammad al-Muttaqi, then his son Ali al-Taqi, then his son al-Hasan al-Zaki, then his son Muhammad who is the Qa'im al-Muntazar, may God be pleased with them all. They had differences regarding these ranks. Those who assert explicit designation (nass jali) concerning Ali ibn Abi Talib, may God be pleased with him, agreed that he is determined for the imamate. And from a group of the Imamiyya, they said: The matter after the one upon whom be peace and blessings is up to Ali ibn Abi Talib; he may do in the imamate whatever he wishes: if he wishes, he takes it for himself, and if he wishes, he appoints another. (1) I say: The Imamiyya say that installing the imam is a grace (lutf), because it brings closer to obedience and distances from disobedience, and grace is obligatory upon God Almighty. As for the Sab'iyya, they do not affirm the obligation of anything upon God Almighty, nor do they affirm rational good and evil, and they are not counted among the Imamiyya. Rather, they say that instruction (ta'lim) is obligatory, and knowledge of God is not attained except through a combination of reflection and instruction. Then the person determined for the imamate: knowledge of God Almighty depends on knowledge of him, and everything he commands is obligatory and obedience, and everything he forbids is disobedience and evil or forbidden. They were named Sab'iyya because their predecessors said the imams are seven, and at the seventh, who is Muhammad ibn Isma'il, some stopped with him, and others went beyond him and said the imams exceed seven, seven like the days of the week. Those who said that the imam teaches us languages and foods are from the Ghulat, and these two groups are not from the Imamiyya.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage classifies the Shi'a into four main types and elaborates on the Imamiyya. It first states that the Shi'a are a genus with four species: Imamiyya, Kaysaniyya, Zaydiyya, and Ghulat. Then it explains the view of the Imamiyya: they believe in a specific line of twelve imams starting from Ali and ending with Muhammad al-Mahdi. However, they differed on how the imam is determined: some said the imam is explicitly designated by text (nass jali) from the previous imam, while others held that the imam after the Prophet has authority to appoint his successor or take the imamate himself. The marginal note (1) provides additional detail: The Imamiyya base the necessity of the imamate on the principle of lutf (grace), which is obligatory upon God. In contrast, the Sab'iyya (Seveners) reject the obligation of anything upon God and reject rational good and evil; they are not considered true Imamiyya. The Sab'iyya believe that instruction is obligatory and that knowledge of God requires both reflection and instruction from the imam. They are called Sab'iyya because they limit the imams to seven, some stopping at Muhammad ibn Isma'il, others extending beyond seven. The note also distinguishes the Ghulat, who held extreme views such as the imam teaching languages and foods, concluding that these two groups are not part of the Imamiyya.

Correction: The passage shows that even within the Imamiyya, there are differences on whether the imam is explicitly designated or has authority to appoint his successor.

?

?

?
The Ghulat are those who 'exaggerate' in their beliefs about the imams. Is there any belief or practice you hold that might be an exaggeration of true teaching? What would it cost you to honestly reevaluate it?
