The Imam as Grace (Lutf) and the Sab'iyya Rejection
Explains lutf as basis for imamate and distinguishes Sab'iyya and Ghulat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Imamiyya's argument that installing an imam is a grace (lutf) obligatory upon God, and how the Sab'iyya and Ghulat diverge from this view.

Core Idea
For the Imamiyya, the imam is a necessary grace from God; others deny this obligation.

This passage continues with a marginal note explaining the theological basis for the Imamiyya's belief in the necessity of the imam. It introduces the concept of lutf (grace) as an obligatory act of God, and contrasts this with the Sab'iyya (Seveners) who reject both the obligation of anything upon God and rational good and evil. It also briefly mentions the extreme views of the Ghulat.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of lutf
Read the first part of the note where the Imamiyya argue that installing an imam is a grace (lutf) because it brings closer to obedience and distances from disobedience. They consider this grace obligatory upon God.
This is a key kalam principle.
2
Compare with the Sab'iyya view
Next, read about the Sab'iyya. They reject that anything is obligatory upon God and also reject rational good and evil. They believe knowledge of God requires instruction from an imam.
The Sab'iyya are considered separate from the Imamiyya.
3
Identify the Ghulat and final distinction
Finally, note that the Ghulat hold extreme views such as the imam teaching languages and foods. The passage concludes that these two groups (Sab'iyya and Ghulat) are not considered part of the Imamiyya.
This clearly separates the main Imamiyya from these groups.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الشيعة جنس تحته أربعة أنواع الإمامية والكيسانية والزيدية والغلاة الإمامية ) فالذين استقر رأيهم على أن الإمام بعد الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام على بن أبي طالب ثم ولده الحسن ثم أخوه الحسين ثم ابنه على ثم ابنه محمد الباقر ثم ابنه جعفر الصادق ثم ابنه موسى الكاظم ثم ابنه على الرضا ثم ابنه محمد المتقى ثم ابنه على التقى ثم ابنه الحسن الزكى ثم ابنه محمد وهو القائم المنتظر رضوان الله عليهم أجمعين . وقد كان لهم في هذه المراتب اختلافات فيقول القائلون بالنص الجلى على على بن أبى طالب رضى الله عنه اتفقوا على أنه متمين للإمامة . وعن فرقة الإمامية أنهم قالوا الأمر بعد الذي عليه الصلاة والسلام إلى على بن أبي طالب يفعل في الإمامة ما أحب ، إن شاء جعلها لنفسه وإن شاء ولاها غيره . (1) أقول الإمامية يقولون نصب الإمام لطف ، لأنه مقرب من الطاعة ومبعد عن المعصية واللطف واجب على الله تعالى ، أما السبعية فلا يقولون بوجوب شيء على الله تعالى ولا بالحسن و القبح العقليين ولا يعدون في الإمامية إنما هم يقولون بأن التعليم واجب ومعرفة الله لا تحصل إلا بمجموع المظر والتعليم، ثم الشخص المتعين الإمامة تكون معرفة الله تعالى موقوفة على معرفته وكل ما يأمر به هو فهو واجب ، وطاعة وكل ما ينهى عنه معصية وقبح أو محرم، وسموهم بالسبعية لأن متقدميهم قالوا الأئمة سبعة ، وعند السابع وهو محمد ابن إسماعيل توقف بعضهم عليه ، وجاوزه بعضهم وقالو الأئمة يزيدون على سبعة ، سبعة كأيام الأسبوع ، والذين قالوا الإمام يعلمنا اللغات والأغذية فهم من الغلاة وليس هذان الصنفان من الإمامية .
Issue: The Shi'a are a genus under which there are four types: the Imamiyya, the Kaysaniyya, the Zaydiyya, and the Ghulat. The Imamiyya are those whose view settled that the Imam after the Messenger, peace be upon him, is Ali ibn Abi Talib, then his son al-Hasan, then his brother al-Husayn, then his son Ali, then his son Muhammad al-Baqir, then his son Ja'far al-Sadiq, then his son Musa al-Kazim, then his son Ali al-Rida, then his son Muhammad al-Muttaqi, then his son Ali al-Taqi, then his son al-Hasan al-Zaki, then his son Muhammad who is the Qa'im al-Muntazar, may God be pleased with them all. They had differences regarding these ranks. Those who assert explicit designation (nass jali) concerning Ali ibn Abi Talib, may God be pleased with him, agreed that he is determined for the imamate. And from a group of the Imamiyya, they said: The matter after the one upon whom be peace and blessings is up to Ali ibn Abi Talib; he may do in the imamate whatever he wishes: if he wishes, he takes it for himself, and if he wishes, he appoints another. (1) I say: The Imamiyya say that installing the imam is a grace (lutf), because it brings closer to obedience and distances from disobedience, and grace is obligatory upon God Almighty. As for the Sab'iyya, they do not affirm the obligation of anything upon God Almighty, nor do they affirm rational good and evil, and they are not counted among the Imamiyya. Rather, they say that instruction (ta'lim) is obligatory, and knowledge of God is not attained except through a combination of reflection and instruction. Then the person determined for the imamate: knowledge of God Almighty depends on knowledge of him, and everything he commands is obligatory and obedience, and everything he forbids is disobedience and evil or forbidden. They were named Sab'iyya because their predecessors said the imams are seven, and at the seventh, who is Muhammad ibn Isma'il, some stopped with him, and others went beyond him and said the imams exceed seven, seven like the days of the week. Those who said that the imam teaches us languages and foods are from the Ghulat, and these two groups are not from the Imamiyya.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage classifies the Shi'a into four main types and elaborates on the Imamiyya. It first states that the Shi'a are a genus with four species: Imamiyya, Kaysaniyya, Zaydiyya, and Ghulat. Then it explains the view of the Imamiyya: they believe in a specific line of twelve imams starting from Ali and ending with Muhammad al-Mahdi. However, they differed on how the imam is determined: some said the imam is explicitly designated by text (nass jali) from the previous imam, while others held that the imam after the Prophet has authority to appoint his successor or take the imamate himself. The marginal note (1) provides additional detail: The Imamiyya base the necessity of the imamate on the principle of lutf (grace), which is obligatory upon God. In contrast, the Sab'iyya (Seveners) reject the obligation of anything upon God and reject rational good and evil; they are not considered true Imamiyya. The Sab'iyya believe that instruction is obligatory and that knowledge of God requires both reflection and instruction from the imam. They are called Sab'iyya because they limit the imams to seven, some stopping at Muhammad ibn Isma'il, others extending beyond seven. The note also distinguishes the Ghulat, who held extreme views such as the imam teaching languages and foods, concluding that these two groups are not part of the Imamiyya.

Key Terms
اللطف
السبعية
الحسن والقبح
الغلاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Shia believe that God is obligated to do things and that good and evil are rational categories.
Correction: The Sab'iyya reject the obligation of anything upon God and reject rational good and evil, showing diversity among Shia.

Checkpoint
What do the Sab'iyya reject according to the passage?
A) The obligation of anything upon God and rational good/evil
B) The necessity of an imam
C) The twelve imams
D) The concept of lutf

Key Takeaway
The Imamiyya's imamate is based on the principle of lutf (grace) obligatory upon God, while the Sab'iyya and Ghulat hold different views and are not considered true Imamiyya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that God has obligations toward you—like providing guidance—or do you see everything as sheer favor? How does your answer shape your gratitude and your demands on God?
?
The Sab'iyya reject rational good and evil, making morality depend entirely on the imam's instruction. Are there any moral convictions you hold only because an authority told you, not because you have reasoned them? What would it cost you to examine them?
?
The Ghulat expected the imam to teach languages and foods—things beyond his role. In your life, have you placed any person in an almost divine position, expecting them to solve problems only God can? What would it look like to return them to their proper place?

Sects and Disagreements

The Kamilia and Sabaiyya Sects: Extreme Views on ʿAlī
Two early Shia sects and their extreme beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extreme beliefs of two early Shia sects—the Kamilia and Sabaiyya—regarding the Companions, ʿAlī, and his return.

Core Idea
Extreme sects deified ʿAlī and accused the Companions of disbelief.

This passage from Imam al-Rāzī’s Muḥaṣṣal introduces two fringe groups from early Islamic history: the Kāmiliyya and the Sabāiyya. These sects held extreme views about ʿAlī ibn Abī Ṭālib, ranging from condemning the Companions to deifying ʿAlī himself. Understanding these beliefs helps us see the boundaries of orthodox Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the factual claims.
The passage contains two distinct sect descriptions.
2
Understand the Kāmiliyya position
Identify what the Kāmiliyya believed about the Companions and ʿAlī. Note their view on taqiyya and the majority response.
3
Understand the Sabāiyya position
Identify how the Sabāiyya deified ʿAlī, including their claims about his non-death and natural phenomena.
4
Reflect on doctrinal extremes
Consider how these beliefs contrast with mainstream Islamic theology and why they were rejected.
The passage shows the majority Shia view for the Kāmiliyya but not for the Sabāiyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم الكاملية وهم أصحاب أبى كامل معاذ بن الحصين التيهاني أن الصحابة كفرت بمخالفتهم النص الجلى، وأن دليا كفر تترك القتال معهم . أما الأكثرون اتفقوا على أنه كان متعينا في الإمامة وإن كان محقا في ترك القتال للتقية ، ثم اختلفوا بعد موته. وزعمت السبائية أصحاب ابن سبأ أنه لم يمت وأنه في السموات وأن الرعد صوته والبرق سوطه وأنه ينزل إلى الأرض بعد حين فيقتل أعداءه فإذا سمع هؤلاء صوت الرعد يقولون عليك السلام يا أمير المؤمنين .
The Kamilia, who are the followers of Abu Kamil Mu'adh ibn al-Husayn al-Tihani, claimed that the Companions committed disbelief (kufr) by violating the explicit designation (nass jali), and that 'Ali disbelieved by abandoning fighting with them. As for the majority, they agreed that he was determined for the imamate, though he was correct in abandoning fighting due to dissimulation (taqiyya). Then they differed after his death. The Sabaiyya, the followers of Ibn Saba', claimed that he did not die, that he is in the heavens, that thunder is his voice, lightning his whip, and that he will descend to the earth after a time and kill his enemies. When these people hear thunder, they say: 'Peace be upon you, O Commander of the Faithful.'
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents two extreme Shia sects: the Kamilia and the Sabaiyya. The Kamilia, followers of Abu Kamil, held that all the Companions who opposed the explicit designation of 'Ali became disbelievers, and even 'Ali himself disbelieved by not fighting them. However, the majority of Shia disagreed, affirming that 'Ali was the rightful imam but was justified in not fighting due to taqiyya. After 'Ali's death, they further divided. The Sabaiyya, followers of 'Abdullah ibn Saba', held extreme deification of 'Ali, claiming he did not die but resides in the heavens, with natural phenomena like thunder and lightning being manifestations of his power. They believe he will return to earth to take revenge. When they hear thunder, they greet him as the Commander of the Faithful.

Key Terms
الكاملية
السبائية
النص الجلى
التقية
الغلو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Shia held extreme views about ʿAlī, such as deification or condemning all Companions.
Correction: The majority of Shia (Imamiyya) do not deify ʿAlī or claim he didn't die; these were fringe sects like the Kāmiliyya and Sabāiyya.

Checkpoint
What did the Kamilia believe about ʿAlī's action of not fighting the Companions?
A) He was correct and it was an act of taqiyya
B) He committed disbelief by not fighting
C) He was following the explicit designation
D) He was waiting for a divine command

Key Takeaway
The Kāmiliyya and Sabāiyya represent early theological extremes that orthodox Muslim thought rejected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear thunder, do you ever think of Ali? Why might this lesson challenge any unconscious tendency to assign divine attributes to righteous figures?
?
The Kamilia declared even Ali a disbeliever for his actions. Have you ever judged someone as a disbeliever based on a single action without considering their full context and faith? What would it take to change that pattern?
?
Both sects relied on claims about Ali that lacked clear scriptural evidence. In your own life, are there any beliefs you hold about a person or group that you assume are true without checking the primary sources? How can you commit to verifying from the Quran and Sunnah before adopting a position?

Shia Divisions After the Early Imams
Traces the succession disputes among Shia after the deaths of Ali, Hasan, and Husayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the major splits among the Shia regarding the imamate after the deaths of Ali, Hasan, and Husayn.

Core Idea
Succession disputes fractured the early Shia community into multiple branches.

Imam Razi traces the succession disputes among the Shia after the deaths of the first three imams. This passage shows how the Shia community was not monolithic but split over who should lead after each imam's death. We'll see the emergence of groups like the Kaysaniyya and the lines that later developed into Zaydi and Imami traditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the flow. Note how the disputes are enumerated after each imam's death.
The passage is dense with names and groups.
2
Identify the groups and lines
Look for the different factions: the Kaysaniyya, the majority, and the minority lines through Hasan's descendants.
Pay attention to the terms 'الكيانية' (Kaysaniyya) and 'النفس الزكية' (the Pure Soul).
3
Trace the succession after each imam
Follow the sequence: after Ali, after Hasan, after Husayn. Note who each group supported.
4
Connect to later Shia sects
Think about how these early splits relate to known Shia sects like the Zaydiyya (from Zayd, a descendant of Husayn) and the Imamiyya.
The passage doesn't mention later sects, but this background is foundational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الباقون فقطعوا بموته . هم اختلفوا، منهم من قال الإمام بعده محمد بن الحنفية فهو قول الكيانية على ما سيأتى قولهم في فصل مفرد والأكثرون قالوا بعده الحسن . ثم اختلفوا بعد موت الحسن فمنهم من ساق الإمامة إلى ولد الحسن وهو الملقب بالرضا من آل محمد ، ومنه إلى ولده عبد الله ثم إلى ولده محمد وهو النفس الزكية ، ثم إلى أخيه إبراهيم، والأكثرون ساقوها من الحسن إلى الحسين ثم اختلفوا بعد قتله، فمنهم من ساقها إلى أخيه محمد بن الحنفية وهو قول أكثر الكيسانية والأكثرون ساقوها إلى ولده على زين العابدين .
As for the rest, they affirmed his death. They differed: some said the imam after him is Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya, and that is the saying of the Kayaniyya, as their saying will come in a separate section. The majority said after him is al-Hasan. Then they differed after the death of al-Hasan: some traced the imamate to the son of al-Hasan, who is called al-Rida from the family of Muhammad, and from him to his son 'Abdullah, then to his son Muhammad who is the Pure Soul (al-Nafs al-Zakiyya), then to his brother Ibrahim. The majority traced it from al-Hasan to al-Husayn. Then they differed after his killing: some traced it to his brother Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya, which is the saying of most of the Kaysaniyya, while the majority traced it to his son 'Ali Zayn al-'Abidin.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage records the disputes among the Shia after the deaths of the early imams. After 'Ali's death, some (the Kaysaniyya) held that the imam was Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya, but the majority said it was al-Hasan. Then after al-Hasan's death, some traced the imamate through al-Hasan's son (called al-Rida) and his descendants, including Muhammad al-Nafs al-Zakiyya and his brother Ibrahim. The majority, however, traced it from al-Hasan to al-Husayn. After al-Husayn's death at Karbala, some (the Kaysaniyya) said it passed to his brother Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya, while the majority said it passed to his son 'Ali Zayn al-'Abidin. This demonstrates the fragmentation of the Shia community based on different criteria for succession.

Key Terms
الكيسانية
محمد بن الحنفية
النفس الزكية
على زين العابدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that after the death of an imam, all Shia agreed on the next imam.
Correction: In reality, each succession was disputed, with different groups supporting different candidates, leading to multiple lines and sects.

Checkpoint
After the death of al-Husayn, which group traced the imamate to Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya?
A) The majority
B) The Kaysaniyya
C) The Zaydiyya
D) The Imamiyya

Key Takeaway
The early Shia community was divided over the rightful imam after each death, with the Kaysaniyya supporting Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya and the majority tracing the line through al-Hasan and al-Husayn.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were a companion of 'Ali or al-Hasan, would you have followed the majority or a splinter group? What criteria would you use to decide, and are you willing to apply that same standard to your own loyalties today?
?
This lesson shows that even among those who loved the Prophet's family, there was disagreement on who should lead. When you face uncertainty about religious authority, do you seek clarity from the sources or follow the crowd?
?
Consider the Kaysaniyya: they supported Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya out of genuine belief. Are there beliefs you hold that might be based on incomplete information? How would you respond if you discovered your group was historically on the minority side?

Initial Shia Divisions After Muhammad al-Baqir
Describes the initial schism between Zaydiyya and Imamiyya after Muhammad al-Baqir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial split between Zaydiyya and Imamiyya after Muhammad al-Baqir's death and the differing views on whether he actually died.

Core Idea
The death of an imam triggered both a succession dispute and a theological dispute over his very death.

This passage from Imam al-Razi's Muhassal details the fragmentation of Shia groups after the death of Muhammad al-Baqir. Some traced the imamate to his son Zayd, forming the Zaydiyya, while others followed his son Muhammad al-Baqir, later known as Imamiyya. But even among the Imamiyya, there was disagreement about whether al-Baqir actually died.

Guided Path
1
Read about the Zaydiyya vs Imamiyya
Read the first part of the passage which states that after al-Baqir's death, the Zaydiyya traced imamate to his son Zayd, and the Imamiyya traced it to al-Baqir himself.
Note that the Zaydiyya are named after Zayd ibn Ali.
2
Identify the dispute over al-Baqir's death
Notice that after mentioning the split, the passage adds that some said he did not die and are awaiting him, while the majority affirmed his death.
The phrase 'ينظرونه' likely means 'they await him' (from انتظار).
3
Reflect on sectarian implications
Consider how a seemingly factual question (did he die?) became a basis for sectarian division, with implications for succession and occultation beliefs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلفوا بعد موته فالزيدية ساقوها إلى ولده زيد بن على كما سيأتى شرح أحوال الزبدية في فصل مفرد ، والإمامية ساقوها إلى محمد الباقر . واختلفوا بعد موته ، فمنهم من قال إنه لم يمت ينظرونه ، ومنهم من قطع بموته وهم الأكثرون . ثم اختلفوا فمنهم من ساقها إلى غير ولده هم فريقان . أحدهما الذين ساقوها إلى محمد بن عبد الله بن الحسن بن الحسين وهو قول أصحاب المغيرة بن سعيد المجلى . وثانيهما الذين ساقوها إلى أبي منصور البلخى على ما سيأتى شرح هاتين الفرقتين في فصل الفلاة أما الذين ساقوها إلى ولده جعفر الصادق فقد اختلفوا بعد موته على قولين أحدهما الذين قطعوا بأنه لم يمت ولن يموت حتى يظهر أمره وهو القائم المهدى ، ورووا عنه أنه قال لو رأيتم رأسى مدهدها عليكم من الجبل فلا تصدقوا وإنى صاحبكم صاحب السيف . ثم اختلفوا فقالت الناووسية فيته وقال آخرون إنه لم يمت وإن أوليا.. يرونه في بعض الأوقات وإنه يعدهم ويانيهم ولكنه ما عين له وقتا للخروج . وثانيها : الذين آمنوا أن جعفر مات ولا إمام بعده وسيرجع إلى الدنيا فيملا الدنيا عدلا كما ملئث جوار وهم الاووسية . وثالثها : الذين ساقوا الإمامة إلى ولده ، والذين سافو الامامة إلى غير ولده . ورابعها : التيمية أصحاب عبد الله بن سعيد التيمي . وخامسها : الجمدية أصحاب أبى جمدة من الكوفة
Then they differed after his death: the Zaydiyya traced it to his son Zayd ibn Ali, as the explanation of the affairs of the Zaydiyya will come in a separate section. And the Imamiyya traced it to Muhammad al-Baqir. They differed after his death: some said he did not die and are waiting for him [؟], and others affirmed his death, and they are the majority. Then they differed: some traced it to other than his son, and they are two groups. One of them traced it to Muhammad ibn Abdullah ibn al-Hasan ibn al-Husayn, which is the saying of the followers of al-Mughira ibn Sa'id al-Mujalli. The second traced it to Abu Mansur al-Balkhi, as the explanation of these two groups will come in the chapter on the Ghulat. As for those who traced it to his son Ja'far al-Sadiq, they differed after his death on two opinions: one of them affirmed that he did not die and will never die until his affair appears, and he is the Qa'im al-Mahdi, and they narrated from him that he said: 'If you see my head rolled down [؟] to you from the mountain, do not believe it, for I am your master, the master of the sword.' Then they differed: the Nawusiyya said concerning him [؟], and others said he did not die, and that his friends [؟] see him at times, and he promises them [؟] but did not set a time for his emergence. The second opinion: those who believed that Ja'far died and there is no imam after him, and he will return to the world and fill it with justice as it was filled [؟] with oppression, and they are the Awusiyya. The third: those who traced the imamate to his son, and those who traced the imamate to other than his son. The fourth: the Taymiyya, followers of Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Taymi. The fifth: the Jamadiyya, followers of Abu Jamda from Kufa.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage continues the enumeration of Shia sub-sects after the death of Muhammad al-Baqir. It mentions that the Zaydiyya followed his son Zayd, while the Imamiyya followed his son Muhammad al-Baqir. After al-Baqir's death, some believed he did not die and await his return, but the majority affirmed his death. Then there were those who traced the imamate not through al-Baqir's son, but through other lines: one group followed Muhammad ibn Abdullah (al-Nafs al-Zakiyya), and another followed Abu Mansur al-Balkhi (both considered Ghulat). Then it discusses the aftermath of Ja'far al-Sadiq's death: some (the Nawusiyya) held that Ja'far did not die and is the Mahdi, with a reported saying that even if they see his head come down a mountain, they should not believe it. Others said he is alive but hidden, seen by his followers on occasion, with no set time for reappearance. Another opinion held that Ja'far died and there is no imam after him until his return (raj'a) to fill the earth with justice (the Awusiyya). Others traced the imamate to his son or to other families. The passage ends with additional sects: Taymiyya and Jamadiyya.

Key Terms
الزيدية
الإمامية
النظر [الانتظار]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all Shia after al-Baqir believed he died.
Correction: Some Shia believed he did not die and was in occultation, awaiting his appearance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what was the majority view among the Imamiyya regarding Muhammad al-Baqir's death?
A) They believed he died.
B) They believed he did not die.
C) They followed Zayd instead.
D) They rejected imamate altogether.

Key Takeaway
The immediate aftermath of al-Baqir's death saw both a dynastic split (Zaydiyya vs Imamiyya) and a theological dispute over his physical death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that some early followers refused to accept their imam's death. Is there a person, idea, or attachment in your life that you are refusing to let go of, even though it may be 'dead' or no longer valid? What would it cost you to release it?
?
When you disagree with the majority of your community, do you have the courage to stand on what you believe, as those who said 'he did not die' did? Or do you conform without examining the evidence? Reflect on a specific situation where you chose convenience over conviction.
?
The division after al-Baqir's death was not just about succession but about the very reality of death. What does your own relationship with death reveal about your trust in Allah's decree? Are you living each day with the awareness that death is certain, or do you avoid thinking about it?

The Ghulat: Non-Son Lines After al-Baqir
Identifies two Ghulat groups that traced imamate to non-sons of al-Baqir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two Ghulat groups that emerged after Muhammad al-Baqir's death—the Mughiriyya and the Mansuriyya—who traced the imamate to figures other than his son.

Core Idea
Some Shia groups bypassed al-Baqir's sons and traced the imamate to other descendants or even to non-descendants.

After mentioning the Zaydiyya and Imamiyya, al-Razi notes that some Shia traced the imamate to non-sons of Muhammad al-Baqir. Two specific groups are named: the followers of al-Mughira ibn Sa'id al-Mujalli and the followers of Abu Mansur al-Balkhi. These groups are categorized as Ghulat (exaggerators) and will be discussed later.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about non-son groups
Read the second part of the passage, which states that some traced imamate to other than his son, and they are two groups.
This follows the previous split.
2
Identify the leaders and groups
The first group is the Mughiriyya (followers of al-Mughira ibn Sa'id), tracing imamate to Muhammad ibn Abdullah. The second is the Mansuriyya (followers of Abu Mansur al-Balkhi).
Both are considered Ghulat (extremists).
3
Understand the Ghulat classification
Note that the author says their explanation will come in the chapter on the Ghulat, indicating these are considered extreme sects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلفوا بعد موته فالزيدية ساقوها إلى ولده زيد بن على كما سيأتى شرح أحوال الزبدية في فصل مفرد ، والإمامية ساقوها إلى محمد الباقر . واختلفوا بعد موته ، فمنهم من قال إنه لم يمت ينظرونه ، ومنهم من قطع بموته وهم الأكثرون . ثم اختلفوا فمنهم من ساقها إلى غير ولده هم فريقان . أحدهما الذين ساقوها إلى محمد بن عبد الله بن الحسن بن الحسين وهو قول أصحاب المغيرة بن سعيد المجلى . وثانيهما الذين ساقوها إلى أبي منصور البلخى على ما سيأتى شرح هاتين الفرقتين في فصل الفلاة أما الذين ساقوها إلى ولده جعفر الصادق فقد اختلفوا بعد موته على قولين أحدهما الذين قطعوا بأنه لم يمت ولن يموت حتى يظهر أمره وهو القائم المهدى ، ورووا عنه أنه قال لو رأيتم رأسى مدهدها عليكم من الجبل فلا تصدقوا وإنى صاحبكم صاحب السيف . ثم اختلفوا فقالت الناووسية فيته وقال آخرون إنه لم يمت وإن أوليا.. يرونه في بعض الأوقات وإنه يعدهم ويانيهم ولكنه ما عين له وقتا للخروج . وثانيها : الذين آمنوا أن جعفر مات ولا إمام بعده وسيرجع إلى الدنيا فيملا الدنيا عدلا كما ملئث جوار وهم الاووسية . وثالثها : الذين ساقوا الإمامة إلى ولده ، والذين سافو الامامة إلى غير ولده . ورابعها : التيمية أصحاب عبد الله بن سعيد التيمي . وخامسها : الجمدية أصحاب أبى جمدة من الكوفة
Then they differed after his death: the Zaydiyya traced it to his son Zayd ibn Ali, as the explanation of the affairs of the Zaydiyya will come in a separate section. And the Imamiyya traced it to Muhammad al-Baqir. They differed after his death: some said he did not die and are waiting for him [؟], and others affirmed his death, and they are the majority. Then they differed: some traced it to other than his son, and they are two groups. One of them traced it to Muhammad ibn Abdullah ibn al-Hasan ibn al-Husayn, which is the saying of the followers of al-Mughira ibn Sa'id al-Mujalli. The second traced it to Abu Mansur al-Balkhi, as the explanation of these two groups will come in the chapter on the Ghulat. As for those who traced it to his son Ja'far al-Sadiq, they differed after his death on two opinions: one of them affirmed that he did not die and will never die until his affair appears, and he is the Qa'im al-Mahdi, and they narrated from him that he said: 'If you see my head rolled down [؟] to you from the mountain, do not believe it, for I am your master, the master of the sword.' Then they differed: the Nawusiyya said concerning him [؟], and others said he did not die, and that his friends [؟] see him at times, and he promises them [؟] but did not set a time for his emergence. The second opinion: those who believed that Ja'far died and there is no imam after him, and he will return to the world and fill it with justice as it was filled [؟] with oppression, and they are the Awusiyya. The third: those who traced the imamate to his son, and those who traced the imamate to other than his son. The fourth: the Taymiyya, followers of Abdullah ibn Sa'id al-Taymi. The fifth: the Jamadiyya, followers of Abu Jamda from Kufa.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage continues the enumeration of Shia sub-sects after the death of Muhammad al-Baqir. It mentions that the Zaydiyya followed his son Zayd, while the Imamiyya followed his son Muhammad al-Baqir. After al-Baqir's death, some believed he did not die and await his return, but the majority affirmed his death. Then there were those who traced the imamate not through al-Baqir's son, but through other lines: one group followed Muhammad ibn Abdullah (al-Nafs al-Zakiyya), and another followed Abu Mansur al-Balkhi (both considered Ghulat). Then it discusses the aftermath of Ja'far al-Sadiq's death: some (the Nawusiyya) held that Ja'far did not die and is the Mahdi, with a reported saying that even if they see his head come down a mountain, they should not believe it. Others said he is alive but hidden, seen by his followers on occasion, with no set time for reappearance. Another opinion held that Ja'far died and there is no imam after him until his return (raj'a) to fill the earth with justice (the Awusiyya). Others traced the imamate to his son or to other families. The passage ends with additional sects: Taymiyya and Jamadiyya.

Key Terms
الغلاة
المغيرة بن سعيد
أبو منصور البلخى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of these groups is mentioned as a Ghulat group in the passage?
A) Zaydiyya
B) Mughiriyya
C) Imamiyya
D) Awusiyya

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi identifies two Ghulat lineages after al-Baqir: the Mughiriyya tracing to Muhammad ibn Abdullah and the Mansuriyya tracing to Abu Mansur al-Balkhi.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Ghulat attached religious authority to figures outside the established lineage. In your own heart, do you ever attach spiritual authority to someone based on their personality, lineage, or charisma rather than their adherence to the Quran and Sunnah? Where might you be vulnerable to such misguidance, and what will you do to correct it?
?
Al-Razi classifies these groups as Ghulat (extremists) for their extreme views. Have you ever considered that some beliefs or practices you hold might be excessive when measured against the balanced standards of Ahl al-Sunnah? What concrete step will you take today to check your own faith against the authentic sources?
?
These groups deviated by prioritizing human figures over divine guidance. How confident are you that your own sectarian affiliation does not lead you to follow human opinion over the clear commands of Allah? What one action will you commit to this week to ensure your ultimate allegiance is to Allah alone?

Sects After Ja'far al-Sadiq and Other Groups
Enumerates the sects after Ja'far al-Sadiq's death and two other sects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple sects that emerged after Ja'far al-Sadiq's death: those who believed he was alive and the Mahdi, those who awaited his return, and other smaller sects like the Taymiyya and Jamadiyya.

Core Idea
Ja'far al-Sadiq's death produced conflicting beliefs: some said he never died, others said he died and will return, and still others looked to other imams.

After discussing the Ghulat, al-Razi turns to those who traced the imamate to Ja'far al-Sadiq, son of Muhammad al-Baqir. After Ja'far's death, opinions split widely: some (Nawusiyya) asserted he never died and is the Mahdi; others said he is alive but hidden, occasionally seen; another group (Awusiyya) said he died and will return to fill the world with justice. The passage also mentions the Taymiyya and Jamadiyya as separate sects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on Ja'far al-Sadiq
Read the long passage about those who traced imamate to Ja'far and their differing opinions after his death.
The text contains multiple enumerated groups.
2
Identify the three main views on Ja'far
Look for (1) Nawusiyya: Ja'far never died and is Mahdi, (2) hidden alive but seen sometimes, (3) Awusiyya: Ja'far died and will return.
3
Note the additional sects
The passage ends by naming the Taymiyya and Jamadiyya as separate sects.
These are distinct from the groups around Ja'far.

Source Text

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all Shia after Ja'far accepted his death and the imamate passed to his son directly.
Correction: Many sects held diverse views: some believed he never died, some believed he will return, and others rejected the imamate of his son.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did the Nawusiyya believe about Ja'far al-Sadiq?
A) He died and there is no imam after him.
B) He did not die and is the Mahdi.
C) He will return after death.
D) He appointed his son as imam.

Key Takeaway
After Ja'far al-Sadiq's death, Shia sects split dramatically: some declared him the undying Mahdi, others awaited his return, and smaller groups like the Taymiyya and Jamadiyya also emerged.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read about the many sects that formed after Ja'far al-Sadiq, each claiming to follow the truth, does it challenge you to examine your own attachments? Are there any teachers, scholars, or figures you follow so closely that you might deny clear facts just to defend them? What would it take for you to abandon a beloved figure if the truth contradicted your devotion?
?
These sects split over beliefs about whether an imam died or will return. Do you base your faith on events and personalities, or on the unchanging Book and Sunnah? In your daily life, are there beliefs you hold simply because 'my group says so' without verifying them directly from the Quran and authentic hadith? Write down one belief you will verify this week.
?
The Awusiyya believed Ja'far died and there is no imam after him, yet still hoped for his return. How often do you hold onto hopes that contradict reality? Is there a situation in your life where you are waiting for something that will never happen? How can you align your hopes with what Allah has actually promised, rather than wishful thinking?

Kamilia and Sabaiyya: Extreme Claims about Ali and the Companions
Razi reports the beliefs of Kamilia and Sabaiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extreme beliefs of two early Shia sects—Kamilia and Sabaiyya—as reported by Razi, and how they contrast with the mainstream Sunni view.

Core Idea
Extreme sects claimed that even Ali disbelieved by not fighting, or that he never died.

In this passage, Razi reports two fringe sects that took extreme positions regarding the Companions and the fate of Ali. The Kamilia condemned both the Companions and Ali himself, while the Sabaiyya deified Ali and awaited his return. This lesson examines these claims and distinguishes them from the mainstream.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of Razi's report.
2
Identify the two sects and their claims
Find the Kamilia and Sabaiyya in the passage. For each, note: who they follow, what they claim about the Companions, and what they claim about 'Ali.
The Kamilia say 'Ali disbelieved; the Sabaiyya say he never died.
3
Contrast with the mainstream view
The passage mentions 'the majority' (al-aktharun) and 'the rest' (al-baqun). Identify their positions and see how they differ from the extreme sects.
This highlights the marginal nature of these sects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم الكاملية وهم أصحاب أبي كامل معاذ بن الحصين التيهاني أن الصحابة كفرت بمخالفتهم النص الجلي، وأن عليا كفر بترك القتال معهم. أما الأكثرون اتفقوا على أنه كان متعينا في الإمامة وإن كان محقا في ترك القتال للتقية، ثم اختلفوا بعد موته. وزعمت السبائية أصحاب ابن سبأ أنه لم يمت وأنه في السموات وأن الرعد صوته والبرق سوطه وأنه ينزل إلى الأرض بعد حين فيقتل أعداءه فإذا سمع هؤلاء صوت الرعد يقولون عليك السلام يا أمير المؤمنين. وأما الباقون فقطعوا بموته.
And the Kamilia—they are the followers of Abu Kamil Mu'adh ibn al-Husayn al-Tayhani—claimed that the Companions disbelieved by opposing the clear text, and that 'Ali disbelieved by abandoning fighting with them. As for the majority, they agreed that he was designated for the imamate even though he was correct in abandoning fighting for dissimulation (taqiyya), then they differed after his death. And the Sabaiyya—the followers of Ibn Saba—claimed that he did not die and that he is in the heavens, and that thunder is his voice and lightning his whip, and that he will descend to the earth after a while and kill his enemies, so when these hear the sound of thunder they say: 'Peace be upon you, O Commander of the Faithful.' And the rest were certain of his death.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage is a report of two extreme Shia sects. First, Razi describes the Kamilia, followers of Abu Kamil, who claimed that the Companions disbelieved by opposing the clear text (nass jali) regarding 'Ali, and that 'Ali himself disbelieved by not fighting them. Then he notes that the majority (al-aktharun) agreed that 'Ali was indeed the designated imam, but he was right to avoid fighting for taqiyya. Second, Razi reports the Sabaiyya, followers of 'Abdullah ibn Saba, who claimed that 'Ali did not die but is in the heavens, and they attribute natural phenomena to him—thunder as his voice, lightning as his whip—and await his return to kill his enemies. They greet the thunder as if greeting 'Ali. In contrast, the rest (al-baqun) were certain of his death.

Key Terms
الكاملية
السبائية
النص الجلي
التقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all Shia hold extreme views about the Companions and Ali's death.
Correction: Razi shows that these are only two small sects. The majority of Muslims (including mainstream Shia) affirmed Ali's imamate and death.

Checkpoint
What did the Kamilia claim about 'Ali?
A) He was not the rightful imam
B) He disbelieved by not fighting the Companions
C) He is alive in the heavens
D) He will return at the end of time

Key Takeaway
The passage shows that extreme Shia sects existed that either condemned Ali or deified him, but the majority of Muslims affirmed his imamate and death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever completely cut off or condemned someone because they didn't act exactly as you thought they should—like the Kamilia condemned 'Ali? What would it take for you to consider that they might have a valid reason, like taqiyya, that you don't see?
?
The Sabaiyya elevated 'Ali to a divine level. In your own heart, have you ever placed a religious figure—a scholar, a leader, or a saint—on a pedestal so high that you almost attribute infallibility or supernatural powers to them? How do you guard against that subtle shirk?
?
This passage presents two extremes: one that condemns 'Ali, one that deifies him. Where do you personally stand in your love and respect for the Companions and the Ahl al-Bayt? Are you confident that you are on the middle path, or are you leaning toward one of these extremes?

Succession After Ali: Kisaniyya and Others
Razi enumerates successive sectarian splits after Ali's death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various disagreements among Shia groups over who succeeded Imam Ali and how the imamate was traced after al-Hasan and al-Husayn.

Core Idea
The succession after Ali produced multiple splits, each with a majority and minority view.

This passage from Razi's work details the early Shia disagreements over the line of imams after Ali. Razi records both the majority and minority positions, including the Kisaniyya's claim for Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya. As you read, notice how the majority consistently shifted from one figure to another, while smaller groups held onto alternative lines.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the repeated pattern of 'ikhtilaf' (disagreement).
2
Identify the first disagreement after Ali
Who did the Kisaniyya support? Who did the majority support? Mark these in the text.
3
Trace the disagreements after al-Hasan's death
Note the smaller group's line (al-Rida and his descendants) and the majority's shift to al-Husayn.
4
Examine the disagreement after al-Husayn's killing
See how the Kisaniyya reappear with the claim for Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya, while the majority traces to Ali Zayn al-Abidin.
5
Summarize the pattern
Each time, a minority group clung to an alternative line, while the majority followed a different path. Write a short summary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلفوا: منهم من قال الإمام بعده محمد بن الحنفية فهو قول الكيسانية على ما سيأتي قولهم في فصل مفرد، والأكثرون قالوا بعده الحسن. ثم اختلفوا بعد موت الحسن: فمنهم من ساق الإمامة إلى ولد الحسن وهو الملقب بالرضا من آل محمد، ومنه إلى ولده عبد الله ثم إلى ولده محمد وهو النفس الزكية، ثم إلى أخيه إبراهيم، والأكثرون ساقوها من الحسن إلى الحسين ثم اختلفوا بعد قتله، فمنهم من ساقها إلى أخيه محمد بن الحنفية وهو قول أكثر الكيسانية، والأكثرون ساقوها إلى ولده علي زين العابدين.
Then they differed: among them were those who said the imam after him was Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya—that is the saying of the Kisaniyya, as their saying will come in a separate chapter—and the majority said after him al-Hasan. Then they differed after the death of al-Hasan: among them were those who traced the imamate to the son of al-Hasan, who is titled al-Rida from the family of Muhammad, and from him to his son 'Abd Allah, then to his son Muhammad—he is al-Nafs al-Zakiyya—then to his brother Ibrahim; and the majority traced it from al-Hasan to al-Husayn, then they differed after his killing: among them were those who traced it to his brother Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya—that is the saying of most of the Kisaniyya—and the majority traced it to his son 'Ali Zayn al-'Abidin.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage continues the historical report of Shia sects. After the death of 'Ali, the first split occurred: some (the Kisaniyya) held that Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya was the imam, while the majority (al-aktharun) held that al-Hasan succeeded. After al-Hasan's death, there were further splits: a minority traced the imamate to al-Hasan's sons (al-Rida, 'Abd Allah, Muhammad al-Nafs al-Zakiyya, Ibrahim), but the majority shifted it from al-Hasan to his brother al-Husayn. After al-Husayn's martyrdom, some again revived the claim of Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya (most Kisaniyya), but the majority held that the imamate passed to al-Husayn's son 'Ali Zayn al-'Abidin.

Key Terms
الكيسانية
النفس الزكية
علي زين العابدين
الرضا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Kisaniyya are often conflated with later Shia groups, but here they are a distinct sect that favored Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya.
Correction: The Kisaniyya were a specific group that held Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya as the rightful imam after Ali, separate from the majority line.

Checkpoint
After al-Hasan's death, where did the majority trace the imamate?
A) To al-Husayn
B) To Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya
C) To al-Hasan's son al-Rida
D) To Ibrahim

Key Takeaway
The early Shia imamate succession was marked by multiple disagreements, with the majority eventually tracing the line from Ali to al-Hasan to al-Husayn to Ali Zayn al-Abidin, while smaller groups like the Kisaniyya persisted with alternative lines.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imagine you were a Shi'a living in the first century after the Prophet. Based on the passage, after which of these successions would you have found yourself in the minority? Are you comfortable being in a minority even when the evidence points elsewhere?
?
The passage shows that after each imam's death, the community split again and again. Where in your own life have you seen or contributed to divisions over leadership or authority? Are you quick to side with the majority or do you cling to personal loyalty even when it contradicts the majority of knowledge?
?
The Kisaniyya insisted on Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya as imam even after his death. Is there a person, scholar, or group you have elevated above clear textual evidence? Are you willing to examine your attachments in light of the Qur'an and Sunnah today?

The Initial Split After 'Ali Zayn al-'Abidin
Razi states the two branches after 'Ali Zayn al-'Abidin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Shia divided into Zaidiyya and Imamiyya after the death of 'Ali Zayn al-'Abidin.

Core Idea
The first major Shia split after Zayn al-'Abidin defined the Zaidiyya and Imamiyya.

After the death of 'Ali Zayn al-'Abidin, the Shia community faced a critical decision: who would be the next imam? Two major groups emerged, each tracing the imamate to a different son. This lesson presents Razi's account of that foundational division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the two groups mentioned.
Notice the reference to a later chapter on the Zaidiyya.
2
Identify the two groups
Find the names 'Zaidiyya' and 'Imamiyya' and note which son each followed.
Zaidiyya followed Zayd; Imamiyya followed Muhammad al-Baqir.
3
Consider the significance
Think about why this split is important for understanding later Shia history.
This division set the stage for further developments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلفوا بعد موته: فالزيدية ساقوها إلى ولده زيد بن علي كما سيأتي شرح أحوال الزيدية في فصل مفرد، والإمامية ساقوها إلى محمد الباقر. واختلفوا بعد موته، فمنهم من قال إنه لم يمت ينتظرونه، ومنهم من قطع بموته وهم الأكثرون. ثم اختلفوا: فمنهم من ساقها إلى غير ولده وهم فريقان: أحدهما الذين ساقوها إلى محمد بن عبد الله بن الحسن بن الحسين وهو قول أصحاب المغيرة بن سعيد المجلى، وثانيهما الذين ساقوها إلى أبي منصور البلخي على ما سيأتي شرح هاتين الفرقتين في فصل [الفلاة؟].
Then they differed after his death: the Zaidiyya traced it to his son Zayd ibn 'Ali, as will come the explanation of the conditions of the Zaidiyya in a separate chapter; and the Imamiyya traced it to Muhammad al-Baqir. And they differed after his death: among them were those who said that he did not die, awaiting him; and among them were those who were certain of his death, and they are the majority. Then they differed: among them were those who traced it to other than his son, and they are two groups: one of them traced it to Muhammad ibn 'Abd Allah ibn al-Hasan ibn al-Husayn, and that is the saying of the followers of al-Mughira ibn Sa'id al-Mujalli; and the second of them traced it to Abu Mansur al-Balkhi, as will come the explanation of these two groups in a chapter [al-falah?].
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
After the death of 'Ali Zayn al-'Abidin, the major division occurred: the Zaidiyya followed his son Zayd, while the Imamiyya followed another son, Muhammad al-Baqir. After al-Baqir's death, there was a further split: some believed he did not die and were awaiting his return (these are likely the early 'waiters'), but the majority held that he died. Then among those who affirmed his death, some transferred the imamate to non-descendants, specifically two groups: one following al-Mughira ibn Sa'id who claimed the imamate for Muhammad ibn 'Abd Allah (al-Nafs al-Zakiyya), and another following Abu Mansur al-Balkhi. Razi promises to detail these two groups in a later chapter.

Key Terms
الزيدية
الإمامية
زيد بن علي
محمد الباقر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the Zaidiyya and Imamiyya are the only Shia groups from the start.
Correction: These are specific branches that emerged after Zayn al-'Abidin; other Shia groups existed earlier.

Key Takeaway
The death of 'Ali Zayn al-'Abidin led to the primary Shia division between Zaidiyya and Imamiyya.

After Muhammad al-Baqir, some Shia denied his death and awaited his return.

Two Groups Tracing Imamah to Non-Descendants
Razi enumerates two groups that traced imamate to non-descendants.

Sects After Ja'far al-Sadiq
Razi enumerates five sects after Ja'far al-Sadiq's death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the major sectarian divisions that arose among Shia after the death of Ja'far al-Sadiq, including those who denied his death and those who affirmed it with a future return.

Core Idea
Ja'far al-Sadiq's death fractured his followers into at least five distinct sects.

After the death of Ja'far al-Sadiq, his followers split over whether he had truly died or would return as the Mahdi. This passage from Razi catalogues these early Shia sects, some of which are obscure. The text contains some uncertain words, but the overall classification is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note that Razi enumerates five opinions, but they are not all equally distinct.
The text has OCR uncertainties; focus on the main ideas.
2
Identify the two main positions
The first major split is between those who said Ja'far did not die (he is the Qa'im) and those who said he died and will return.
These are the first two 'sayings' mentioned.
3
Note the subgroups
Within the first group, there is a further split: al-Nawusiyya and another unnamed group who see him occasionally.
The second group (those who believe he died and will return) is also called al-Nawusiyya, causing ambiguity.
4
Recognize the remaining sects
The third, fourth, and fifth opinions refer to those who traced imamate to his son, al-Taymiyya, and al-Jumdiyya.
These are less detailed in the passage.
5
Reflect on the classification
Consider why Razi might list these as separate. The ambiguity about al-Nawusiyya shows how early sectarian labels were fluid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الذين ساقوها إلى ولده جعفر الصادق فقد اختلفوا بعد موته على قولين: أحدهما الذين قطعوا بأنه لم يمت ولن يموت حتى يظهر أمره وهو القائم المهدي، ورووا عنه أنه قال: لو رأيتم رأسي مدحرجا عليكم من الجبل فلا تصدقوا وإني صاحبكم صاحب السيف. ثم اختلفوا: فقالت الناووسية [فيته؟] وقال آخرون إنه لم يمت وإن أولياءه يرونه في بعض الأوقات وإنه يعدهم و[يانيهم؟] ولكنه ما عين له وقتا للخروج. وثانيها: الذين آمنوا أن جعفر مات ولا إمام بعده وسيرجع إلى الدنيا فيملأ الدنيا عدلا كما ملئت جورا وهم [الناووسية؟]. وثالثها: الذين ساقوا الإمامة إلى ولده، والذين سافوا الامامة إلى غير ولده. ورابعها: التيمية أصحاب عبد الله بن سعيد التيمي. وخامسها: الجمدية أصحاب أبي جمدة من الكوفة.
As for those who traced the imamate to his son Ja'far al-Sadiq, they differed after his death on two sayings: one of them—those who were certain that he did not die and will never die until his affair appears, and he is the Qa'im al-Mahdi, and they narrated from him that he said: 'If you see my head rolling down the mountain to you, do not believe it, for I am your companion, the companion of the sword.' Then they differed: al-Nawusiyya said [fiyyahu?] and others said that he did not die and that his friends see him at some times and that he promises them and [comes to them?], but he has not appointed a time for his emergence. And the second: those who believed that Ja'far died and there is no imam after him, and he will return to the world and fill it with justice as it was filled with injustice—they are al-Nawusiyya. And the third: those who traced the imamate to his son, and those who traced the imamate to other than his son. And the fourth: al-Taymiyya, the followers of 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id al-Taymi. And the fifth: al-Jumdiyya, the followers of Abu Jamda from Kufa.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage details the many opinions that emerged after the death of Ja'far al-Sadiq. The first major split was between those who denied his death entirely, claiming he is the Qa'im al-Mahdi, and those who affirmed his death but believed he would return. Within the first group, there was a subgroup called al-Nawusiyya (possibly the same as those who said he died and will return, but the text is ambiguous). A third group traced the imamate to his son (later Twelver line). A fourth group, al-Taymiyya, followed 'Abd Allah ibn Sa'id al-Taymi. A fifth, al-Jumdiyya, followed Abu Jamda from Kufa. Razi is classifying these sects.

Key Terms
الناووسية
التيمية
الجمدية
القائم المهدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Nawusiyya is a single sect with a clear position.
Correction: The passage confusingly applies the name 'Nawusiyya' to both those who denied Ja'far's death and those who affirmed it, suggesting either textual corruption or that the label was used for two different groups.

Checkpoint
What did the first group among those who traced imamate to Ja'far al-Sadiq believe?
A) That Ja'far died and will return
B) That Ja'far did not die and is the Mahdi
C) That the imamate passed to his son
D) That the imamate passed to a non-descendant

Key Takeaway
Ja'far al-Sadiq's death led to at least five distinct sectarian positions, reflecting early Shia diversity and the fluidity of labels like al-Nawusiyya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read about the fragmentation after Ja'far's death, do you see any tendency in yourself to divide over personalities rather than principles? How attached are you to a specific leader or scholar, and what happens to your faith if that attachment is challenged?
?
The Nawusiyya could not agree on whether Ja'far had died or not. Are there core beliefs you hold that you have never really examined, simply because you inherited them from a group? Are you willing to test them against the sources?
?
If you were a follower of Ja'far al-Sadiq at his death, which of these five positions would you find most tempting, and why? What does that say about your own desire for certainty in the face of ambiguity?

Ya'furiyya and Sects Regarding Musa ibn Ja'far
Razi enumerates sects around Musa ibn Ja'far's imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various sectarian divisions among those who affirmed the imamate of Musa ibn Ja'far, including the Ya'furiyya, Mutawawwira, Bishriyya, and Qaramita.

Core Idea
After Musa ibn Ja'far's death, his followers split into agnostics, immortalists, and claimants.

Imam al-Razi here catalogues the factions that emerged around the succession of Musa ibn Ja'far. He begins with those who hesitated altogether (Ya'furiyya), then describes three groups: those uncertain of his death (Mutawawwira), those who asserted his continued life (Bishriyya), and those who claimed his appointment (Qaramita). This passage shows how early Shi'i circles fragmented over this key figure.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the flow of sects.
Notice the pattern: hesitation → uncertainty → certainty with splits.
2
Identify the four groups
List the four named groups: Ya'furiyya, Mutawawwira, Bishriyya, Qaramita. Note their core belief.
The Mutawawwira got their name from an insult.
3
Understand the key difference
Distinguish between those who hesitated (Ya'furiyya) and those who affirmed Musa's imamate then differed after his death.
4
Reflect on the insult
Note why Yunus ibn 'Abd al-Rahman called them 'dogs that have been turned' (mutawawwira). What does that tell you about his view?
This illustrates the polemical context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الذين توقفوا في سوق الإمامة من جعفر إلى ولده وغير ولده وهم اليعفورية أصحاب أبي يعفور فإنهم جوزوا كلا الأمرين. ثم اختلف القائلون بإمامة موسى بن جعفر بعد موته: فمنهم من توقف في موته وقال لا أدرى مات أو لم يمت ويقال لهم المطورية، لأن يونس بن عبد الرحمن وهو من علماء السبعية قال لهم ما أنتم إلا كلاب مطورة. ومنهم من قطع أنه لم يمت، وأنه حي! ثم اختلفوا: فزعمت البشرية أصحاب محمد بن بشر أن موسى حي لم يمت ولا يموت إلى الوقت المعلوم، وأنه أوصى بالإمامة إليه. وزعمت القرامطة أن موسى أوصى بها إليه.
And as for those who hesitated in transferring the imamate from Ja'far to his son or to other than his son—they are al-Ya'furiyya, the followers of Abu Ya'fur—they permitted both matters. Then those who asserted the imamate of Musa ibn Ja'far differed after his death: among them were those who hesitated about his death and said: 'I do not know whether he died or did not die'—they are called al-Mutawawwira, because Yunus ibn 'Abd al-Rahman, who was one of the scholars of al-Sab'iyya, said to them: 'You are nothing but dogs that have been turned (mutawawwira).' And among them were those who were certain that he did not die, and that he is alive! Then they differed: al-Bishriyya, the followers of Muhammad ibn Bishr, claimed that Musa is alive, has not died, and will not die until the known time, and that he appointed the imamate to him. And al-Qaramita claimed that Musa appointed it to him.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage begins with the Ya'furiyya, who did not take a firm position on who succeeded Ja'far—they allowed both possibilities. Then it turns to those who asserted the imamate of Musa ibn Ja'far (the seventh imam for Twelvers). After his death, there were those who were agnostic about his death (al-Mutawawwira) — they said 'I do not know' — and were insulted by Yunus ibn 'Abd al-Rahman. Others were certain he did not die. Among those certain of his life, two subgroups emerged: al-Bishriyya (followers of Muhammad ibn Bishr) who held that Musa is alive and will not die until an appointed time, and that he appointed the imamate to his follower; and al-Qaramita, who also claimed that Musa appointed the imamate to their founder.

Key Terms
اليعفورية
المطورية
البشرية
القرامطة
يونس بن عبد الرحمن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qaramita (Qarmatians) are often associated with later Ismaili movements that denied the imamate of Musa ibn Ja'far altogether.
Correction: This passage shows that the early Qaramita actually claimed that Musa appointed the imamate to their founder, meaning they affirmed his imamate but claimed it was passed on.

Checkpoint
Why were al-Mutawawwira called that name?
A) They hesitated in the imamate
B) Yunus ibn 'Abd al-Rahman called them dogs that have been turned
C) They claimed Musa was alive
D) They followed a man named Mutawawwir

Key Takeaway
The imamate of Musa ibn Ja'far spawned multiple sectarian responses, ranging from agnosticism to belief in his occultation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When faced with conflicting claims about religious leadership, do you tend to suspend judgment like the Ya'furiyya, or commit to a position like the Bishriyya? Which approach is more honest before Allah?
?
The Mutawawwira were insulted for their indecision. Have you ever remained agnostic on a matter of creed to avoid commitment? What would it cost you to seek certainty?
?
Both the Bishriyya and Qaramita claimed that Musa appointed their own leader. How do you test the truth of a claim when multiple parties assert divine appointment? Are you willing to examine your own biases?

The Child Imam Controversy: Muhammad al-Taqi
Razi recounts the Imami split over al-Taqi's imamate and the justification for a child imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the internal Imami debate about the imamate of a young child and the arguments used to justify it.

Core Idea
Can a child be an imam? The Imamiyya debated, citing Jesus as precedent.

After the death of Imam Ali al-Rida, his young son Muhammad al-Taqi was a controversial choice for imam. Some rejected his imamate due to his age, while the majority affirmed it. This passage from Razi records the different positions and the theological reasoning used to justify a child imam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to grasp the historical context and the arguments.
Pay attention to the two splits: first over who succeeded Musa, then over al-Taqi.
2
Identify the factions
Note the minority and majority on Musa's successor, and the different groups regarding al-Taqi.
3
Examine the child imam arguments
Focus on the two positions that affirmed al-Taqi's imamate: one based on divine knowledge grant, the other on functional limitation.
4
Compare with Jesus
Understand why the analogy to Jesus (peace be upon him) was used to justify a child imam.
Jesus is a Quranic example of a child receiving knowledge and prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القاطعون بموته: فمنهم من ساقها إلى ولده أحمد بن موسى والأكثرون ساقوها إلى ولده علي الرضا. ثم القائلون بإمامة علي اختلفوا بعد موته: فمنهم من لم يقل بإمامة ولده محمد التقي لصغره وعدم عليه في ذلك الوقت، فإنه لما مات الرضا كان سن التقي أربعة ومنهم من قال ثمانية، فأما الأكثرون قالوا بإمامة التقي. ثم اختلفوا: فقال قوم لا يبعد أن يخلق الله تعالى فيه العلوم كل الدين أصوله وفروعه، وإن كان صغيرا كما في حق عيسى عليه السلام، وقال آخرون إنه كان إماما على معنى أن الأمر له دون سائر الناس، ولكن لا يجوز أن يكون إماما في الصلوات ومفتيا في الحوادث، وأما المفتي كان بعض أصحابه إلى أن صار [بالفا؟].
As for those who were certain of his death: among them were those who traced it to his son Ahmad ibn Musa, and the majority traced it to his son 'Ali al-Rida. Then those who asserted the imamate of 'Ali differed after his death: among them were those who did not assert the imamate of his son Muhammad al-Taqi due to his young age and lack of [knowledge?] at that time, for when al-Rida died, the age of al-Taqi was four, and some said eight. As for the majority, they asserted the imamate of al-Taqi. Then they differed: a group said it is not unlikely that Allah creates in him all the sciences of the religion, its principles and branches, even if he is young, as in the case of 'Isa (peace be upon him). And others said that he was an imam in the sense that authority belongs to him over other people, but it is not permissible for him to be an imam in prayers or a mufti in events; as for the mufti, it was some of his companions until he became [of age?].
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
After those who affirmed Musa's death, there were two lines: a minority traced imamate to his son Ahmad ibn Musa, but the majority traced it to his son 'Ali al-Rida. Then after al-Rida's death, the question arose about his young son Muhammad al-Taqi (also known as al-Jawad). Some denied his imamate because of his age (four or eight years old). But the majority affirmed it. To justify a child imam, some argued that Allah can grant knowledge to a child, as He did with Jesus. Others conceded that the child is the rightful imam in principle, but cannot perform duties like leading prayer or issuing fatwas until he reaches adulthood; that function was carried out by his companions until then.

Key Terms
علي الرضا
محمد التقي
الإمام الصغير
عيسى عليه السلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Imamiyya agreed that a child could be imam without issue.
Correction: This passage shows that some rejected al-Taqi's imamate due to age, and among those who affirmed, there were two different explanations for how a child could be imam.

Checkpoint
How did some justify the imamate of a child like Muhammad al-Taqi?
A) By comparing to Jesus who received knowledge in childhood
B) They did not justify it; they rejected it outright
C) By saying he would be taught by his father's spirit
D) By saying the imamate is purely hereditary regardless of age

Key Takeaway
The Imamiyya were not unanimous on the child imam; they employed theological reasoning, including an analogy to Jesus, to justify Muhammad al-Taqi's imamate despite his young age.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a young person with surprising depth of faith or knowledge, do you instinctively trust them or doubt them? What does your reaction reveal about your view of how Allah bestows wisdom?
?
Some in the passage acknowledged al-Taqi's authority but delayed his practical role until maturity. Are there areas in your life where you acknowledge Allah's authority but postpone full obedience? What is holding you back from submitting now?
?
The Imamiyya used the example of Jesus to argue that age does not limit divine gifts. How might this challenge your assumptions about who can teach or lead in religious matters? Will you seek out and learn from those you have underestimated?

Imamate Transmission After Muhammad al-Taqi
Razi tracks the imamate through the next few imams up to al-Hasan al-Askari.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the succession disputes after Muhammad al-Taqi and 'Ali al-Taqi, and how the majority line led to al-Hasan al-Askari.

Core Idea
The imamate line narrowed through disputes after each imam's death.

This passage offers a snapshot of sectarian dynamics within early Imami circles. After the death of each imam, followers split over who the next imam was, with a majority eventually converging on the line that would become the Twelver imams. As you read, notice how the 'awaited one' title appears at a key juncture.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic along with the literal translation to grasp the structure. Note the two main disagreements: after al-Taqi and after 'Ali al-Taqi.
The passage is short but dense with information.
2
Identify the two splits
First split: after Muhammad al-Taqi, some traced to Musa, majority to 'Ali al-Taqi. Second split: after 'Ali al-Taqi, some claimed he is the awaited, some traced to Ja'far, majority to al-Hasan.
Each split has multiple opinions; note the majority position.
3
Understand the 'awaited one' concept
Notice that some considered 'Ali al-Taqi himself as the awaited Mahdi. This highlights the fluidity of the concept before it settled on the twelfth imam.
This is a common misunderstanding corrected later.
4
Reflect on the narrowing pattern
Ask yourself: why does the majority opinion matter for the later Twelver doctrine? The commentary will help connect these events to the final line.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القائلون بإمامة التقي اختلفوا بعد موته: فمنهم من ساقها إلى ولده موسى والأكثرون ساقوها إلى علي التقي. ثم اختلفوا بعد موته: فزعم بعضهم أنه هو المنتظر، ومنهم من ساقها إلى ولده جعفر، والأكثرون ساقوها إلى ولده الحسن بن علي.
Then those who asserted the imamate of al-Taqi differed after his death: among them were those who traced it to his son Musa, and the majority traced it to 'Ali al-Taqi. Then they differed after his death: some claimed that he is the awaited one, and among them were those who traced it to his son Ja'far, and the majority traced it to his son al-Hasan ibn 'Ali.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage summarizes the transmission of imamate after Muhammad al-Taqi. After his death, some traced it to his son Musa (likely al-Musawi), but most traced it to another son, 'Ali al-Taqi (al-Hadi). After 'Ali al-Taqi's death, some considered him the awaited Mahdi, others traced it to his son Ja'far, but the majority traced it to his other son, al-Hasan ibn 'Ali (al-Askari). This reflects the gradual narrowing to the line that would become the Twelver imams.

Key Terms
علي التقي
الحسن بن علي
المنتظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The awaited one (al-muntazar) is always the twelfth imam.
Correction: In this passage, some considered 'Ali al-Taqi himself as the awaited, showing that the title was applied to earlier imams before settling on the twelfth.

Checkpoint
After 'Ali al-Taqi's death, where did the majority trace the imamate?
A) To his son Musa
B) To his son Ja'far
C) To his son al-Hasan
D) To himself as the awaited

Key Takeaway
After each imam's death, splits occurred, but the majority consistently maintained the line leading to al-Hasan al-Askari.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see Muslims today divided over leadership or interpretation, do you calmly seek the established majority of scholars or are you drawn to minority voices? How does the pattern of splits after each imam in this passage challenge your approach to religious authority?
?
If you were living during the time of these imams, which faction would you have likely followed, and what would you need to be certain you were on the right path? How does your answer reveal the importance of seeking knowledge and avoiding blind partisanship?

The First Three Opinions After al-Hasan al-Askari
Razi lists the first three of twelve opinions after al-Hasan al-Askari's death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first three opinions about what happened after al-Hasan al-Askari's death, including the arguments that he did not die, that he died and will return, and that he died and appointed his brother Ja'far.

Core Idea
After al-Hasan's death, some said he never died, some said he would return, others said he appointed a successor.

The death of al-Hasan al-Askari in 260 AH left the Shia community in confusion. Without a clear successor, various factions emerged with distinct explanations. The first three opinions represent foundational positions that shaped later Shia theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the first opinion
Read the first opinion carefully: it claims al-Hasan did not die because the world cannot be without an infallible imam.
This is a key argument for the imam's continued existence.
2
Read the second opinion
The second opinion accepts his death but holds that he will return (the meaning of 'qa'im').
3
Read the third opinion
The third opinion says he died without return but appointed his brother Ja'far as imam.
This is a historical claim about succession.
4
Reflect on the diversity
Consider how these three opinions already show significant disagreement among early Shia.
These are just the first three of twelve.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اختلفوا بعد موت الحسن على اثني عشر قولا. الأول: أنه لم يمت لأنه لو مات وليس له ولد ظاهر خلا الزمان عن الإمام المعصوم وأنه غير جائز. الثاني: أنه مات لكن سيجيء وهو المعنى بكونه قائما أي يقوم بعده. الثالث: أنه مات ولا يجيء ولكنه أوصى بالإمامة إلى أخيه جعفر. الرابع: بل أوصى بها إلى أخيه محمد. الخامس: أنه مات من غير عقب فعلم أنه ما كان إماما وأن الإمام جعفر. السادس: بل ظهر أن الإمام كان محمدا لأن جعفر كان مجاهرا بالفسق والحسن كان فاسقا في الخفية فتعين محمد للإمامة.
Then they differed after the death of al-Hasan on twelve sayings. The first: that he did not die, because if he died and has no apparent son, the time would be empty of the infallible imam, and that is not permissible. The second: that he died but will come, and that is the meaning of his being 'qa'im' (rising), i.e., he will rise after his death. The third: that he died and will not come, but he appointed the imamate to his brother Ja'far. The fourth: rather, he appointed it to his brother Muhammad. The fifth: that he died without offspring, so it is known that he was not an imam, and that the imam was Ja'far. The sixth: rather, it became clear that the imam was Muhammad, because Ja'far was openly sinful and al-Hasan was secretly sinful, so Muhammad was determined for the imamate.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage lists the first six of twelve opinions about al-Hasan al-Askari's death. The first opinion argues he did not die, because otherwise there would be no infallible imam on earth, which is impossible. The second says he died but will return (like a resurrection). The third says he died without return but appointed his brother Ja'far. The fourth says he appointed another brother, Muhammad. The fifth denies his imamate entirely, saying he had no children so he wasn't the imam; instead, Ja'far was the true imam. The sixth claims that Muhammad was the true imam because Ja'far was openly sinful and al-Hasan was secretly sinful, so Muhammad was the only righteous one. These opinions reflect the confusion after al-Askari's death, which eventually led to the Twelver doctrine of the Occultation.

Key Terms
الإمام المعصوم
القائم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think these twelve opinions are from the same sect.
Correction: They represent a wide spectrum of early Shia thought, including different factions.

Checkpoint
What is the first opinion about al-Hasan al-Askari's death?
A) He died and will return
B) He did not die because the world cannot be without an infallible imam
C) He died and appointed Ja'far
D) He never existed

Key Takeaway
The first three opinions show early Shia grappling with the succession crisis after al-Hasan al-Askari's death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The first group could not accept that the world could be without an infallible imam. Is there any religious guide or teacher in your life whose absence you have struggled to accept? How has that affected your reliance on God rather than on a human figure?
?
The second group hoped for the deceased leader's return. Do you ever find yourself longing for a 'golden age' of Islam, wishing a historical figure would return to fix things? How can you live in hope without neglecting your present duties?
?
The third group accepted the leader's death and looked to the appointed successor. When a spiritual mentor or beloved scholar passes away, how do you ensure that your attachment remains to the truth rather than to the person? What steps will you take to keep their teachings alive without deifying them?

The Second Three Opinions After al-Hasan al-Askari
Razi lists the next three of twelve opinions after al-Hasan al-Askari's death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three more opinions after al-Hasan al-Askari's death: that he appointed Muhammad, that he never was imam (Ja'far was), and that Muhammad was the true imam due to righteousness.

Core Idea
Some factions denied al-Hasan's imamate entirely, while others argued for his brother Muhammad's superiority.

Continuing the list of twelve opinions, the next three present more radical positions: one claims al-Hasan appointed his brother Muhammad, another denies his imamate because he had no children, and the third asserts Muhammad's imamate based on moral character.

Read the fourth opinion

2
Read the fifth opinion

This is a denial of al-Hasan's imamate.
3
Read the sixth opinion
The sixth opinion asserts Muhammad was the imam because Ja'far was openly sinful and al-Hasan was secretly sinful.
This uses moral argument.
4
Reflect on the diversity
Notice how these three opinions continue to show disagreement, including complete rejection of al-Hasan's imamate.
These are opinions 4-6 of twelve.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
