Sects on Musa ibn Ja'far's Imamate and Death
Classifies sects that disputed Musa ibn Ja'far's death and succession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various sectarian positions that emerged after the death of Musa ibn Ja'far regarding his Imamate, including whether he died or is still alive, and who succeeded him.

Core Idea
The Imamiyya fragmented over whether Musa ibn Ja'far died and who succeeded him.

This passage from Imam al-Razi's Muhassal presents a detailed classification of the sects that arose within the Imamiyya after the death of Imam Musa ibn Ja'far. It shows how different groups disagreed not only on whether he died but also on the line of succession. By studying this classification, you will see the historical diversity of early Shi'i thought and the theological debates that shaped later orthodoxy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main sects
As you read, list the sects mentioned: Ya'furiyya, Mutawwiriyya, Bishriyya, Qaramita, and the two groups that affirmed death (those who passed it to Ahmad and the majority to Ali al-Rida).
2
Understand the Ya'furiyya position
The Ya'furiyya suspended judgment on whether the Imamate passed from Ja'far to his son or to someone else. Note their unique stance of allowing both possibilities.
3
Compare groups on Musa's death
After affirming Musa's Imamate, the groups split: Mutawwiriyya hesitated about his death, Bishriyya claimed he is alive and will not die until an appointed time, and Qaramita claimed he bequeathed the Imamate to a successor while alive.
The Bishriyya and Qaramita both assert he did not die, but differ on details.
4
Trace the succession lines
Those who affirmed Musa's death passed the Imamate either to his son Ahmad ibn Musa (a minority) or to his son Ali al-Rida (the majority). This shows the eventual consensus around Ali al-Rida.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الَّذِينَ تَوَقَّفُوا فِي سَوْقِ الْإِمَامَةِ مِنْ جَعْفَرٍ إِلَى وَلَدِهِ وَغَيْرِ وَلَدِهِ وَهُمُ الْيَعْفُورِيَّةُ أَصْحَابُ أَبِي يَعْفُورٍ فَإِنَّهُمْ جَوَّزُوا كِلَا الْأَمْرَيْنِ. ثُمَّ اخْتَلَفَ الْقَائِلُونَ بِإِمَامَةِ مُوسَى بْنِ جَعْفَرٍ بَعْدَ مَوْتِهِ فَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ تَوَقَّفَ فِي مَوْتِهِ وَقَالَ لَا أَدْرِي مَاتَ أَوْ لَمْ يَمُتْ وَيُقَالُ لَهُمُ الْمُطَوِّرِيَّةُ، لِأَنَّ يُونُسَ بْنَ عَبْدِ الرَّحْمَنِ وَهُوَ مِنْ عُلَمَاءِ السَّبْعِيَّةِ قَالَ لَهُمْ مَا أَنْتُمْ إِلَّا كِلَابٌ مُطَوَّرَةٌ. وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ قَطَعَ أَنَّهُ لَمْ يَمُتْ، وَأَنَّهُ حَيٌّ! ثُمَّ اخْتَلَفُوا فَزَعَمَتِ الْبِشْرِيَّةُ أَصْحَابُ مُحَمَّدِ بْنِ بِشْرٍ أَنَّ مُوسَى حَيٌّ لَمْ يَمُتْ وَلَا يَمُوتُ إِلَى الْوَقْتِ الْمَعْلُومِ، وَأَنَّهُ أَوْصَى بِالْإِمَامَةِ إِلَيْهِ. وَزَعَمَتِ الْقَرَامِطَةُ أَنَّ مُوسَى أَوْصَى بِهَا إِلَيْهِ. وَأَمَّا الْقَاطِعُونَ بِمَوْتِهِ فَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ سَاقَهَا إِلَى وَلَدِهِ أَحْمَدَ بْنِ مُوسَى وَالْأَكْثَرُونَ سَاقُوهَا إِلَى وَلَدِهِ عَلِيٍّ الرِّضَا.
As for those who stopped in the transmission of the Imamate from Ja'far to his son and other than his son, and they are the Ya'furiyya, followers of Abu Ya'fur, they allowed both matters. Then those who affirmed the Imamate of Musa ibn Ja'far disagreed after his death: among them were those who hesitated about his death and said, 'I do not know whether he died or did not die,' and they are called al-Mutawwiriyya, because Yunus ibn Abd al-Rahman, who was from the scholars of the Sab'iyya, said to them, 'You are nothing but metamorphosed dogs.' And among them were those who asserted that he did not die and that he was alive! Then they disagreed: the Bishriyya, followers of Muhammad ibn Bishr, claimed that Musa is alive, has not died, and will not die until the appointed time, and that he bequeathed the Imamate to him. And the Qaramita claimed that Musa bequeathed it to him. As for those who asserted his death, among them were those who passed it to his son Ahmad ibn Musa, and the majority passed it to his son Ali al-Rida.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage classifies the various sects that emerged after the death of Musa ibn Ja'far regarding his Imamate and succession. It begins by mentioning the Ya'furiyya, who suspended judgment on whether the Imamate passed from Ja'far to his son or to someone else. Then it describes the groups that affirmed Musa's Imamate but disagreed after his death: some hesitated about his death (al-Mutawwiriyya), some asserted he did not die and was alive (this group includes the Bishriyya who claimed he is alive until an appointed time and that he appointed a successor, and the Qaramita who claimed Musa bequeathed the Imamate to a successor). Finally, those who affirmed his death split: some passed the Imamate to his son Ahmad ibn Musa, but the majority passed it to his son Ali al-Rida. The passage carefully delineates each position, showing the fragmentation of the Imamiyya over succession.

اليعفورية
المطورية
البشرية
القرامطة
علي الرضا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Imamiyya agreed that Musa ibn Ja'far died and was succeeded by Ali al-Rida.
Correction: The passage shows that several sects believed he was alive or hesitated about his death, and succession was disputed even among those who affirmed death.

Checkpoint
Which sect claimed that Musa ibn Ja'far was alive and would not die until an appointed time?
A) al-Mutawwiriyya
B) al-Bishriyya
C) al-Qaramita
D) al-Ya'furiyya

Key Takeaway
The Imamiyya after Musa ibn Ja'far fragmented into multiple sects with conflicting views on his death and successor, with the majority eventually settling on Ali al-Rida.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When faced with a religious disagreement where two groups both claim truth, do you rush to pick a side or can you, like the Mutawwiriyya, honestly say 'I do not know'? What would change in your life if you allowed yourself more epistemic humility?
?
The Bishriyya held firmly to their belief that Musa was alive despite the reality of his death. Is there any belief you hold that, if challenged, you would struggle to let go of? Are you willing to put that belief to the test of authentic evidence, even if it is uncomfortable?
?
The majority of the Imamiyya settled on Ali al-Rida after the death of Musa. In your own life, when you are in a situation of confusion or division, do you seek the guidance of the majority of scholars, or do you rely on your own independent reasoning? What is one step you can take today to align your approach with the prophetic methodology of following the majority (as long as it is upon truth)?

Disagreements on Imamate of Ali al-Rida and His Successors
Describes splits after Ali al-Rida's death and subsequent imams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various disagreements among the Imamiyya regarding the succession after Ali al-Rida, particularly concerning the young age of Muhammad al-Taqi and the subsequent splits.

Core Idea
After Ali al-Rida's death, the Imamiyya split over his young successor's age and the nature of his Imamate.

This passage from al-Razi's Muhassal details the internal disagreements among those who affirmed the Imamate of Ali al-Rida after his death. The central issue was whether his son Muhammad al-Taqi could be Imam at such a young age, and how his Imamate was understood. The author then traces the further splits that occurred after al-Taqi's death and later imams.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the sequence of disagreements after each imam's death.
Pay attention to the structure: 'then they disagreed' appears multiple times.
2
Identify the first disagreement
Focus on the dispute over Muhammad al-Taqi's Imamate due to his young age. Note the two responses: some rejected it, others affirmed it with different justifications.
The text mentions ages (4 or 8) and the analogy with Jesus.
3
Trace the subsequent splits
After al-Taqi's death, the community divided again. Who did they follow? Then after that imam's death, further splits.
Note the names: Musa, Ali al-Taqi, Ja'far, al-Hasan ibn Ali.
4
Reflect on the pattern
Observe how age and succession disputes repeatedly caused division. This shows the historical complexity of early Imamiyya.
Consider the role of young age and the concept of 'awaiting' (al-muntazar).
5
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you grasped the main objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ الْقَائِلُونَ بِإِمَامَةِ عَلِيٍّ الرِّضَا اخْتَلَفُوا بَعْدَ مَوْتِهِ فَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ لَمْ يَقُلْ بِإِمَامَةِ وَلَدِهِ مُحَمَّدٍ التَّقِيِّ لِصِغَرِهِ وَعُدِمَ عَلَيْهِ فِي ذَلِكَ الْوَقْتِ، فَإِنَّهُ لَمَّا مَاتَ الرِّضَا كَانَ سِنُّ التَّقِيِّ أَرْبَعَةً وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ قَالَ ثَمَانِيَةً، فَأَمَّا الْأَكْثَرُونَ قَالُوا بِإِمَامَةِ التَّقِيِّ. ثُمَّ اخْتَلَفُوا فَقَالَ قَوْمٌ لَا يَبْعُدُ أَنْ يَخْلُقَ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى فِيهِ الْعُلُومَ لِكُلِّ الدِّينِ أُصُولَهُ وَفُرُوعَهُ وَإِنْ كَانَ صَغِيرًا كَمَا فِي حَقِّ عِيسَى عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ، وَقَالَ آخَرُونَ إِنَّهُ كَانَ إِمَامًا عَلَى مَعْنَى أَنَّ الْأَمْرَ لَهُ دُونَ سَائِرِ النَّاسِ، وَلَكِنْ لَا يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَكُونَ إِمَامًا فِي الصَّلَوَاتِ وَمُفْتِيًا فِي الْحَوَادِثِ، وَأَمَّا الْمُفْتِي كَانَ بَعْضَ أَصْحَابِهِ إِلَى أَنْ صَارَ بِالْفَا. ثُمَّ الْقَائِلُونَ بِإِمَامَةِ التَّقِيِّ اخْتَلَفُوا بَعْدَ مَوْتِهِ، فَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ سَاقَهَا إِلَى وَلَدِهِ مُوسَى وَالْأَكْثَرُونَ سَاقُوهَا إِلَى عَلِيٍّ التَّقِيِّ. ثُمَّ اخْتَلَفُوا بَعْدَ مَوْتِهِ فَزَعَمَ بَعْضُهُمْ أَنَّهُ هُوَ الْمُنْتَظَرُ، وَمِنْهُمْ مَنْ سَاقَهَا إِلَى وَلَدِهِ جَعْفَرٍ، وَالْأَكْثَرُونَ سَاقُوهَا إِلَى وَلَدِهِ الْحَسَنِ بْنِ عَلِيٍّ.
Then those who affirmed the Imamate of Ali al-Rida disagreed after his death: among them were those who did not affirm the Imamate of his son Muhammad al-Taqi due to his young age and his absence at that time. For when al-Rida died, the age of al-Taqi was four, and some said eight. As for the majority, they affirmed the Imamate of al-Taqi. Then they disagreed: a group said it is not unlikely that Allah creates in him the sciences of all religion, its principles and branches, even if he is young, as in the case of Jesus, peace be upon him. Others said that he was an Imam in the sense that authority belongs to him over other people, but it is not permissible for him to be an Imam in prayers and a mufti in events; rather, the mufti was one of his companions until he attained [?]. Then those who affirmed the Imamate of al-Taqi disagreed after his death: among them were those who passed it to his son Musa, and the majority passed it to Ali al-Taqi. Then they disagreed after his death: some claimed that he is the awaited one, and among them were those who passed it to his son Ja'far, and the majority passed it to his son al-Hasan ibn Ali.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage continues the doxography by detailing the splits after the death of Ali al-Rida. The first disagreement concerns his son Muhammad al-Taqi: some rejected his Imamate because he was too young (four or eight years old), but the majority affirmed it. A further disagreement arose about the nature of his Imamate: some argued that God could grant him all religious knowledge despite his youth (analogous to Jesus), while others held that he was Imam in authority but not in leading prayers or issuing legal rulings until he matured. After al-Taqi's death, the Imamiyya split again: some passed the Imamate to his son Musa, but the majority to Ali al-Taqi (likely Ali al-Hadi). After Ali al-Taqi's death, some believed he was the awaited Mahdi, others passed it to his son Ja'far, but the majority passed it to his son al-Hasan ibn Ali (al-Askari). This shows the ongoing fragmentation and the role of age and succession disputes.

Key Terms
علي الرضا
محمد التقي
الصغر
الإمامة
المنتظر
الحسن بن علي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Imamiyya agreed on the succession after al-Rida without dispute.
Correction: The passage shows significant disagreement: some rejected al-Taqi due to age, and after each imam's death, further splits occurred.

Checkpoint
What was the main objection to the Imamate of Muhammad al-Taqi after the death of Ali al-Rida?
A) He was not a descendant of Ali
B) He was too young
C) He was not present at the time
D) He was not learned

Key Takeaway
After Ali al-Rida's death, the Imamiyya experienced multiple splits due to age and succession disputes, with the majority eventually passing the Imamate to al-Hasan ibn Ali.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the Imamiyya splits over a young Imam, what does your own reaction to youthful religious leadership reveal about your reliance on outward signs versus faith in divine enablement? Are you willing to submit to someone who appears unqualified by human standards?
?
The passage shows that some limited al-Taqi's authority to spiritual leadership but denied him practical religious functions like leading prayers. In your own life, do you ever separate someone's spiritual rank from their practical authority? How does this split reflect your own struggles with obedience and trust?
?
After al-Rida's death, the community fragmented repeatedly. Examine a recent conflict in your own community or family: Did you choose unity or division? What was the core issue, and how does this lesson's account of splits challenge you to seek clarity without breaking bonds?

First Four Opinions on al-Hasan al-Askari's Succession
Lists the first four of twelve opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first four views on whether al-Hasan al-Askari died, will return, or designated a brother as Imam.

Core Idea
The Imamate crisis after al-Hasan al-Askari generated diverse responses from denial of death to designation of brothers.

The death of al-Hasan al-Askari without an apparent son created a major dilemma for the Imamiyya. Various groups proposed solutions, from denying his death to claiming he designated a brother. This lesson presents the first four of twelve opinions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text covering the first four opinions. Note the numbering (الأول, الثاني, الثالث, الرابع).
The introductory phrase 'ثُمَّ اخْتَلَفُوا...' sets the context.
2
Identify each opinion
For each opinion, identify the core claim: denial of death, future return, bequest to Ja'far, bequest to Muhammad.
Notice how each opinion is based on a specific rationale.
3
Compare to earlier patterns
Consider how these opinions echo earlier disagreements about other Imams, such as Musa al-Kazim.
The denial of death (first opinion) is similar to claims about previous Imams.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ اخْتَلَفُوا بَعْدَ مَوْتِ الْحَسَنِ بْنِ عَلِيٍّ عَلَى اثْنَيْ عَشَرَ قَوْلًا. الْأَوَّلُ: أَنَّهُ لَمْ يَمُتْ لِأَنَّهُ لَوْ مَاتَ وَلَيْسَ لَهُ وَلَدٌ ظَاهِرٌ خَلَا الزَّمَانُ عَنِ الْإِمَامِ الْمَعْصُومِ وَأَنَّهُ غَيْرُ جَائِزٍ. الثَّانِي: أَنَّهُ مَاتَ لَكِنْ سَيَجِيءُ وَهُوَ الْمَعْنَى بِكَوْنِهِ قَائِمًا أَيْ يَقُومُ بَعْدَهُ. الثَّالِثُ: أَنَّهُ مَاتَ وَلَا يَجِيءُ وَلَكِنَّهُ أَوْحَى بِالْإِمَامَةِ إِلَى أَخِيهِ جَعْفَرٍ. الرَّابِعُ: بَلْ أَوْحَى بِهَا إِلَى أَخِيهِ مُحَمَّدٍ. الْخَامِسُ: أَنَّهُ مَاتَ مِنْ غَيْرِ عَقِبٍ فَعُلِمَ أَنَّهُ مَا كَانَ إِمَامًا وَأَنَّ الْإِمَامَ جَعْفَرٌ. السَّادِسُ: بَلْ ظَهَرَ أَنَّ الْإِمَامَ كَانَ مُحَمَّدًا لِأَنَّ جَعْفَرًا كَانَ مُجَاهِرًا بِالْفِسْقِ وَالْحَسَنُ كَانَ فَاسِقًا فِي الْخَفِيَّةِ فَتَعَيَّنَ مُحَمَّدٌ لِلْإِمَامَةِ. السَّابِعُ: أَنَّ الْحَسَنَ خَلَفَ ابْنًا وُلِدَ قَبْلَ مَوْتِهِ بِسِنِينَ اسْمُهُ مُحَمَّدٌ اسْتَتَرَ خَوْفًا مِنْ عَمِّهِ جَعْفَرٍ وَغَيْرِهِ مِنَ الْأَعْدَاءِ وَهُوَ الْمُنْتَظَرُ. الثَّامِنُ: أَنَّ لَهُ ابْنًا وُلِدَ بَعْدَ مَوْتِهِ بِثَمَانِيَةِ أَشْهُرٍ. التَّاسِعُ: لَمَّا مَاتَ الْإِمَامُ وَلَا وَلَدَ لَهُ فَلَا يَجُوزُ انْتِقَالُ الْإِمَامَةِ مِنْهُ إِلَى غَيْرِهِ فَبَقِيَ الزَّمَانُ خَالِيًا مِنَ الْإِمَامِ وَارْتَفَعَتِ التَّكَالِيفُ. الْعَاشِرُ: يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْإِمَامُ لَا مِنْ ذَلِكَ النَّسْلِ بَلْ مِنْ نَسْلٍ آخَرَ مِنَ الْعَلَوِيَّةِ. الْحَادِيَ عَشَرَ: لَمَّا لَمْ يَجُزِ انْتِقَالُ الْإِمَامَةِ مِنْ ذَلِكَ النَّسْلِ إِلَى نَسْلٍ آخَرَ، وَلَا يَجُوزُ خُلُوُّ الزَّمَانِ عَنِ الْإِمَامِ فَعُلِمَ بَقِيَ مِنْ نَسْلِهِ ابْنٌ وَإِنْ كُنَّا لَا نَعْرِفُهُ فَنَحْنُ عَلَى وَلَايَتِهِ إِلَى أَنْ يَظْهَرَ. الثَّانِيَ عَشَرَ: أَمْرُ الْإِمَامَةِ مَعْلُومٌ إِلَى عَلِيٍّ الرِّضَا وَبَعْدَهُ مُخْتَلَفٌ فَيَتَوَقَّفُ.
Then they disagreed after the death of al-Hasan ibn Ali into twelve opinions. First: that he did not die, for if he died and had no apparent son, the time would be devoid of the infallible Imam, and that is not permissible. Second: that he died but will come, and this is the meaning of his being 'qa'im' i.e., he will rise after him. Third: that he died and will not come, but he bequeathed the Imamate to his brother Ja'far. Fourth: rather, he bequeathed it to his brother Muhammad. Fifth: that he died without offspring, so it is known that he was not an Imam, and that the Imam is Ja'far. Sixth: rather, it became clear that the Imam was Muhammad, because Ja'far was openly sinful and al-Hasan was secretly sinful, so Muhammad was determined for the Imamate. Seventh: that al-Hasan left a son born before his death by years, named Muhammad, who went into hiding out of fear of his uncle Ja'far and other enemies, and he is the awaited one. Eighth: that he had a son born after his death by eight months. Ninth: when the Imam died and has no son, it is not permissible for the Imamate to transfer from him to another, so the time remains devoid of the Imam and the obligations are lifted. Tenth: it is permissible for the Imam to be not from that lineage but from another lineage of the Alawites. Eleventh: since the transfer of the Imamate from that lineage to another is not permissible, and it is not permissible for time to be devoid of the Imam, it is known that a son from his lineage remains even if we do not know him, so we are upon his allegiance until he appears. Twelfth: the matter of the Imamate is known up to Ali al-Rida, and after him it is disputed, so one suspends judgment.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of twelve distinct opinions held by various sects after the death of al-Hasan al-Askari (the eleventh Imam). The first opinion claims he did not die (similar to the earlier claim about Musa). The second says he died but will return as the Qa'im. The third and fourth say he bequeathed the Imamate to his brother Ja'far or Muhammad respectively. The fifth and sixth argue that either Ja'far or Muhammad was the true Imam because al-Hasan was not really an Imam or was sinful. The seventh and eighth claim al-Hasan had a son who went into hiding (the Twelver position), either born before his death or after eight months. The ninth says the Imamate ended and obligations are lifted. The tenth allows the Imam from another Alawite lineage. The eleventh asserts an unknown son exists. The twelfth suspends judgment after al-Rida. Each opinion reflects a theological response to the problem of succession without clear text.

Key Terms
الحسن بن علي
الإمامة
المعصوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second opinion, what does 'qa'im' mean?
A) He never died
B) He will rise after his death
C) He designated a successor
D) He is hidden

Key Takeaway
The first four opinions show a spectrum of responses: denial of death, future return, and bequest to a brother.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The first group denied al-Hasan's death because they could not bear the thought of a time without an infallible Imam. Is there a truth about your faith that you have avoided accepting because it would force you to change your understanding of Allah? What is one step you can take this week to honestly face that truth?
?
The early community split into twelve opinions over succession. In your own life, when you are faced with uncertainty about a religious matter, do you tend to latch onto the first comfortable answer, or do you pursue the truth even if it is uncomfortable? What area of your deen are you most tempted to take shortcuts in?
?
Each opinion here reflects a different way of preserving the Imamate. What does your heart cling to as your ultimate security in times of confusion? Is it a person, a group, or Allah Himself? How would your life change if you truly believed that Allah alone is sufficient regardless of human leadership?

Opinions Five to Eight: Sin, Hidden Son, and the Awaited Imam
Lists opinions five through eight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some groups considered al-Hasan or his brother sinful, and how the belief in a hidden son emerged.

Core Idea
Accusations of sin and the birth of a hidden son became key arguments in defining the true Imam.

The next four opinions delve into the character of the claimants and introduce the idea of a hidden son. This lesson examines the reasoning behind these views.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic covering opinions five through eight. Notice the shift from designation to evaluations of sinfulness and claims about offspring.
Opinions five and six argue about sinfulness, while seven and eight introduce a hidden son.
2
Analyze the logic of each opinion
For opinion five: al-Hasan's lack of offspring proves he was not Imam. For opinion six: Ja'far's open sinfulness disqualifies him, so Muhammad is Imam. For seven: al-Hasan left a son who hid. For eight: son born posthumously.
Opinion six introduces the idea that the true Imam must be free from sin.
3
Connect to later Twelver doctrine
Opinion seven is closest to the Twelver belief: a son who went into hiding. Note the emphasis on fear from enemies.
This opinion is the foundation of the occultation doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ اخْتَلَفُوا بَعْدَ مَوْتِ الْحَسَنِ بْنِ عَلِيٍّ عَلَى اثْنَيْ عَشَرَ قَوْلًا. الْأَوَّلُ: أَنَّهُ لَمْ يَمُتْ لِأَنَّهُ لَوْ مَاتَ وَلَيْسَ لَهُ وَلَدٌ ظَاهِرٌ خَلَا الزَّمَانُ عَنِ الْإِمَامِ الْمَعْصُومِ وَأَنَّهُ غَيْرُ جَائِزٍ. الثَّانِي: أَنَّهُ مَاتَ لَكِنْ سَيَجِيءُ وَهُوَ الْمَعْنَى بِكَوْنِهِ قَائِمًا أَيْ يَقُومُ بَعْدَهُ. الثَّالِثُ: أَنَّهُ مَاتَ وَلَا يَجِيءُ وَلَكِنَّهُ أَوْحَى بِالْإِمَامَةِ إِلَى أَخِيهِ جَعْفَرٍ. الرَّابِعُ: بَلْ أَوْحَى بِهَا إِلَى أَخِيهِ مُحَمَّدٍ. الْخَامِسُ: أَنَّهُ مَاتَ مِنْ غَيْرِ عَقِبٍ فَعُلِمَ أَنَّهُ مَا كَانَ إِمَامًا وَأَنَّ الْإِمَامَ جَعْفَرٌ. السَّادِسُ: بَلْ ظَهَرَ أَنَّ الْإِمَامَ كَانَ مُحَمَّدًا لِأَنَّ جَعْفَرًا كَانَ مُجَاهِرًا بِالْفِسْقِ وَالْحَسَنُ كَانَ فَاسِقًا فِي الْخَفِيَّةِ فَتَعَيَّنَ مُحَمَّدٌ لِلْإِمَامَةِ. السَّابِعُ: أَنَّ الْحَسَنَ خَلَفَ ابْنًا وُلِدَ قَبْلَ مَوْتِهِ بِسِنِينَ اسْمُهُ مُحَمَّدٌ اسْتَتَرَ خَوْفًا مِنْ عَمِّهِ جَعْفَرٍ وَغَيْرِهِ مِنَ الْأَعْدَاءِ وَهُوَ الْمُنْتَظَرُ. الثَّامِنُ: أَنَّ لَهُ ابْنًا وُلِدَ بَعْدَ مَوْتِهِ بِثَمَانِيَةِ أَشْهُرٍ. التَّاسِعُ: لَمَّا مَاتَ الْإِمَامُ وَلَا وَلَدَ لَهُ فَلَا يَجُوزُ انْتِقَالُ الْإِمَامَةِ مِنْهُ إِلَى غَيْرِهِ فَبَقِيَ الزَّمَانُ خَالِيًا مِنَ الْإِمَامِ وَارْتَفَعَتِ التَّكَالِيفُ. الْعَاشِرُ: يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَكُونَ الْإِمَامُ لَا مِنْ ذَلِكَ النَّسْلِ بَلْ مِنْ نَسْلٍ آخَرَ مِنَ الْعَلَوِيَّةِ. الْحَادِيَ عَشَرَ: لَمَّا لَمْ يَجُزِ انْتِقَالُ الْإِمَامَةِ مِنْ ذَلِكَ النَّسْلِ إِلَى نَسْلٍ آخَرَ، وَلَا يَجُوزُ خُلُوُّ الزَّمَانِ عَنِ الْإِمَامِ فَعُلِمَ بَقِيَ مِنْ نَسْلِهِ ابْنٌ وَإِنْ كُنَّا لَا نَعْرِفُهُ فَنَحْنُ عَلَى وَلَايَتِهِ إِلَى أَنْ يَظْهَرَ. الثَّانِيَ عَشَرَ: أَمْرُ الْإِمَامَةِ مَعْلُومٌ إِلَى عَلِيٍّ الرِّضَا وَبَعْدَهُ مُخْتَلَفٌ فَيَتَوَقَّفُ.
Then they disagreed after the death of al-Hasan ibn Ali into twelve opinions. First: that he did not die, for if he died and had no apparent son, the time would be devoid of the infallible Imam, and that is not permissible. Second: that he died but will come, and this is the meaning of his being 'qa'im' i.e., he will rise after him. Third: that he died and will not come, but he bequeathed the Imamate to his brother Ja'far. Fourth: rather, he bequeathed it to his brother Muhammad. Fifth: that he died without offspring, so it is known that he was not an Imam, and that the Imam is Ja'far. Sixth: rather, it became clear that the Imam was Muhammad, because Ja'far was openly sinful and al-Hasan was secretly sinful, so Muhammad was determined for the Imamate. Seventh: that al-Hasan left a son born before his death by years, named Muhammad, who went into hiding out of fear of his uncle Ja'far and other enemies, and he is the awaited one. Eighth: that he had a son born after his death by eight months. Ninth: when the Imam died and has no son, it is not permissible for the Imamate to transfer from him to another, so the time remains devoid of the Imam and the obligations are lifted. Tenth: it is permissible for the Imam to be not from that lineage but from another lineage of the Alawites. Eleventh: since the transfer of the Imamate from that lineage to another is not permissible, and it is not permissible for time to be devoid of the Imam, it is known that a son from his lineage remains even if we do not know him, so we are upon his allegiance until he appears. Twelfth: the matter of the Imamate is known up to Ali al-Rida, and after him it is disputed, so one suspends judgment.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of twelve distinct opinions held by various sects after the death of al-Hasan al-Askari (the eleventh Imam). The first opinion claims he did not die (similar to the earlier claim about Musa). The second says he died but will return as the Qa'im. The third and fourth say he bequeathed the Imamate to his brother Ja'far or Muhammad respectively. The fifth and sixth argue that either Ja'far or Muhammad was the true Imam because al-Hasan was not really an Imam or was sinful. The seventh and eighth claim al-Hasan had a son who went into hiding (the Twelver position), either born before his death or after eight months. The ninth says the Imamate ended and obligations are lifted. The tenth allows the Imam from another Alawite lineage. The eleventh asserts an unknown son exists. The twelfth suspends judgment after al-Rida. Each opinion reflects a theological response to the problem of succession without clear text.

Key Terms
المعصوم
المنتظر
الخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the seventh opinion, why did al-Hasan's son go into hiding?
A) He was sick
B) Fear of his uncle Ja'far and other enemies
C) He was not the true Imam
D) He died

Key Takeaway
Opinions five to eight show the development of arguments based on offspring and moral character, culminating in the hidden son doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear conflicting claims about who is right, do you examine the moral character of the claimants as seriously as their lineage? Are you willing to confront hidden sin in those you follow?
?
The belief in an awaited hidden imam can be a comforting hope, but are you using that hope as an excuse to avoid seeking truth now? What step will you take today to verify your own beliefs against evidence?
?
These opinions were based on evidence like offspring and sin. How honest are you when evidence contradicts what you want to believe? Will you commit to following truth even if it humbles you?

Opinions Nine to Twelve: Vacuum, Other Lineage, and Suspension
Lists opinions nine through twelve

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the final four opinions, including the view that obligations cease and the position of suspending judgment.

Core Idea
The most radical opinions claimed the end of obligations or the need to look beyond the lineage.

The final four opinions represent more extreme positions: the cessation of religious duties, the possibility of an Imam from another Alawite line, the claim of an unknown son, and complete suspension of judgment. This lesson concludes the survey.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read opinions nine through twelve. Note the shift from concrete claims to more abstract reasoning about the Imamate's status.
Opinion nine is particularly radical: it states that obligations are lifted.
2
Contrast each opinion's implications
Opinion nine: no Imam, no obligations. Opinion ten: Imam can come from another lineage. Opinion eleven: an unknown son exists. Opinion twelve: suspend judgment after Ali al-Rida.
Opinion twelve reflects an agnostic stance.
3
Reflect on the diversity
Consider how these twelve opinions show the theological creativity within the Imamiyya in response to a succession crisis.
The Twelver position (seventh/eleventh) eventually became dominant.

Source Text

Disagreement as Proof of No Clear Text
Argues that the great disagreement proves absence of clear, mutawatir text on the Twelve Imams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author considers the great disagreement among Muslims as evidence that there was no clear and widely transmitted text designating the Twelve Imams.

Core Idea
Disagreement proves absence of clear divine text.

This lesson examines a concise yet powerful argument from the author. He points to the profound disagreement among Muslims regarding the identity of the rightful Imams as itself demonstrating that no clear, universally transmitted text existed specifying the Twelve. This challenges the foundational claim of the Imami position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic sentence and its English translation to grasp the author's claim directly.
Notice the key terms: النص الجلي المتواتر.
2
Understand the logical structure
The author says the disagreement itself is 'among the strongest proofs' (من أدل الدلائل). Why? Because if there were a clear, mutawatir text, it would have prevented such fragmentation.
3
Consider the implication
Reflect on what this argument implies about the reliability of the textual evidence for the Twelve Imams. The absence of a clear, widespread text means the claim rests on weaker grounds.
This is a central point in Sunni critiques of Imami doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ هَذَا الِاخْتِلَافَ الْعَظِيمَ مِنْ أَدَلِّ الدَّلَائِلِ عَلَى عَدَمِ النَّصِّ الْجَلِيِّ الْمُتَوَاتِرِ عَلَى هَؤُلَاءِ الِاثْنَيْ عَشَرَ.
And know that this great disagreement is among the strongest proofs for the absence of a clear, mutawatir text regarding these twelve.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This is a short but potent argument. The author asserts that the immense disagreement (the twelve opinions and earlier splits) is itself the strongest evidence that there was no clear (jali) and widely transmitted (mutawatir) textual designation (nass) designating the Twelve Imams. If there had been such a clear and widespread text, such fragmentation would not have occurred. This is a classic Sunni critique of the Imami doctrine of nass.

Key Terms
النص
الجلي
المتواتر
الاثني عشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the author denies any textual basis for Imamate altogether.
Correction: The author specifically denies a clear (jali) and mutawatir text, not any text whatsoever. He leaves open the possibility of less clear or non-mutawatir texts.

Checkpoint
What does the author claim the great disagreement proves?
A) That the Imams were not infallible
B) That there was no clear, mutawatir text on the Twelve
C) That the Imamate is invalid
D) That all sects are wrong

Key Takeaway
The great disagreement among Muslims itself proves there was no clear, widely transmitted text designating the Twelve Imams.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been absolutely sure of a religious claim because it seemed obvious to you, yet others strongly disagree? How does this lesson teach you to reconsider the basis of your certainty? Are you willing to honestly examine whether your belief rests on clear, widely transmitted evidence or merely on personal conviction?
?
When you encounter heated disagreement among Muslims on a matter of doctrine, what is your immediate inner reaction—dismissal, anger, or curiosity? Can you now see disagreement itself as a signpost pointing to the need for textual evidence? What will you do the next time you face such a disagreement?
?
If you discovered that a cherished belief of yours does not have a clear, mutawatir text behind it, would you have the courage to let go of it or to hold it with less certainty? How would you handle the tension between loyalty to your community and loyalty to the truth as revealed by evidence?

The Kaysaniyya Sect: Origins and Beliefs
Defines the Kaysaniyya, their beliefs, and lists their subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand who the Kaysaniyya were, their extreme theological beliefs, and the origin of their name.

Core Idea
The Kaysaniyya combined political revolt with doctrines of incarnation and reincarnation.

This passage introduces a Shi'i sect called the Kaysaniyya, named after a companion of Ali. Their beliefs were so extreme that they eventually rejected Islamic law. The text also mentions a subgroup, the al-Hayyaniyya. Let's examine the details.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation to get an overview of the Kaysaniyya. Note the key terms and the flow of information.
The Arabic is cut off at the end, but the main points are clear.
2
Identify the key figures
The passage mentions Kaysan, Ali, Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya, and al-Mukhtar. Understand their roles.
Al-Mukhtar is said to have been named 'Kaysan' by Ali.
3
List the beliefs of the Kaysaniyya
Find the specific doctrines attributed to them: rejection of laws, denial of resurrection, belief in incarnation and metempsychosis.
These show they were not just political but held heretical theological views.
4
Understand the origin of the name
The passage explains that the sect is named after Kaysan, who was al-Mukhtar, thus called al-Kaysaniyya.
This clarifies the naming.
5
Note the subgroup al-Hayyaniyya
They believed Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya was Imam right after Ali, using the incident of the standard.
This shows internal divisions within the sect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: فِي شَرْحِ فِرَقِ الْكَيْسَانِيَّةِ. هُمْ أَصْحَابُ كَيْسَانَ مَوْلَى أَمِيرِ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ عَلِيٍّ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُ اعْتَقَدُوا فِيهِ الِاعْتِقَادَ الْعَظِيمَ وَأَنَّهُ أَخَذَ عِلْمَ التَّأْوِيلِ وَالْبَاطِنِ وَالْآفَاقِ وَالْأَنْفُسِ مِنِ ابْنِ الْحَنَفِيَّةِ رَحْمَةُ اللَّهِ عَلَيْهِ وَانْتَهَى الْأَمْرُ بِهِمْ إِلَى رَفْضِ الشَّرَائِعِ وَإِنْكَارِ الْقِيَامَةِ وَالْقَوْلِ بِالْحُلُولِ وَالتَّنَاسُخِ وَكَانَ الْمُخْتَارُ بْنُ أَبِي عُبَيْدِ اللَّهِ الثَّقَفِيُّ الْكُوفِيُّ الْقَائِمُ بِثَأْرِ الْحُسَيْنِ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُ خَارِجِيًّا أَوَّلًا وَزُبَيْرِيًّا ثَانِيًا وَشِيعِيًّا ثَالِثًا وَسُنِّيًّا رَابِعًا، وَيُقَالُ إِنَّ عَلِيًّا سَمَّى الْمُخْتَارَ بِكَيْسَانَ فَهَذِهِ الْفِرْقَةُ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُ كَانَ يُقَالُ لَهَا الْكَيْسَانِيَّةُ وَهُمُ الْمَفْتُونُ عَلَى إِمَامَةِ مُحَمَّدِ بْنِ الْحَنَفِيَّةِ. ثُمَّ اخْتَلَفُوا فَذَهَبَتِ الْحَيَّانِيَةُ أَصْحَابُ حَيَّانَ بْنِ زَيْدٍ السَّرَّاجِ إِلَى أَنَّهُ كَانَ إِمَامًا بَعْدَ عَلِيِّ بْنِ أَبِي طَالِبٍ، وَاحْتَجُّوا عَلَيْهِ بِأَنَّ عَلِيًّا دَفَعَ إِلَيْهِ الرَّايَةَ يَوْمَ الْجَمَلِ وَقَالَ: ...
Chapter: On explaining the sects of the Kaysaniyya. They are the followers of Kaysan, the mawla of Amir al-Mu'minin Ali, may Allah be pleased with him. They held a great belief concerning him, and that he took the knowledge of interpretation, the esoteric, the horizons, and the souls from Ibn al-Hanafiyya, may Allah's mercy be upon him. Their affair ended with rejecting the religious laws, denying the resurrection, and holding the doctrines of incarnation and metempsychosis. And al-Mukhtar ibn Abi Ubaydallah al-Thaqafi al-Kufi, who arose to avenge al-Husayn, may Allah be pleased with him, was first a Khariji, second a Zubayri, third a Shi'i, and fourth a Sunni. It is said that Ali named al-Mukhtar 'Kaysan'. So this sect, may Allah be pleased with him, was called al-Kaysaniyya, and they were the ones seduced/ devoted to the Imamate of Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya. Then they disagreed: al-Hayyaniyya, the followers of Hayyan ibn Zayd al-Sarraj, held that he was an Imam after Ali ibn Abi Talib, and they argued that Ali gave him the standard on the Day of the Camel and said: ...
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a chapter heading and definition of the Kaysaniyya sect. It traces them to Kaysan, a mawla of Ali, and describes their extreme beliefs: they held Kaysan in high regard, claimed he received esoteric knowledge from Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya, and eventually fell into rejecting Islamic law, denying the resurrection, and believing in incarnation and reincarnation. The passage also discusses al-Mukhtar al-Thaqafi, the leader of the revolt for al-Husayn, noting his shifting affiliations (Khariji, Zubayri, Shi'i, Sunni) and that Ali reportedly named him 'Kaysan'. Thus the sect was named after him. They were devoted to the Imamate of Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya. Then the text notes a subdivision: al-Hayyaniyya, followers of Hayyan ibn Zayd al-Sarraj, who claimed that Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya was Imam immediately after Ali, citing the incident of the standard at the Battle of the Camel. The passage is cut off but provides a clear picture of the Kaysaniyya's origins and beliefs.

الحيةانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Kaysaniyya were merely a political sect.
Correction: The passage shows they held extreme theological views like incarnation and reincarnation, going beyond political allegiance.

Checkpoint
To whom did the Kaysaniyya attribute the Imamate?
A) Ali al-Rida
B) Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya
C) Ja'far al-Sadiq
D) al-Hasan al-Askari

Key Takeaway
The Kaysaniyya were an extreme Shi'i sect named after Kaysan (al-Mukhtar), who believed in incarnation and reincarnation and were devoted to the Imamate of Muhammad ibn al-Hanafiyya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to examine your own heart, do you find any trace of exaggeration in your veneration of religious figures, leading you to compromise core Islamic beliefs like the finality of revelation or the resurrection? What would it take to uproot that?
?
The Kaysaniyya denied the resurrection and rejected the Sharia. Do you live daily with the certainty of standing before Allah on the Day of Judgment, or do your actions suggest a hidden doubt? How can you strengthen that conviction today?
?
Al-Mukhtar's affiliations shifted dramatically. Are there areas in your life where your loyalty or identity fluctuates based on convenience rather than steadfast adherence to truth? What would it mean to be unwavering in your commitment to Allah's path?

Sects and Disagreements
intermediate
6 min
Shia Disagreements and the Absence of Explicit Text on the Twelve Imams
Al-Razi argues that Shia internal divisions prove no explicit continuous text exists on the Twelve Imams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi uses internal Shia disagreements to argue against the claim of an explicit continuous text (nass jali mutawatir) designating the Twelve Imams.

Core Idea
Great disagreement disproves clear divine appointment.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi makes a striking argument: the sheer number of Shia sects and their disagreements about the Imamate is itself evidence that there cannot be a clear, mass-transmitted text (nass jali mutawatir) naming each of the Twelve Imams. He distinguishes between the explicit text for Ali and the concealed or known texts for later Imams, and he cites a treatise listing dozens of Shia sects. Let's unpack his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice how the author starts with a strong claim about disagreement as evidence.
The term 'nass jali mutawatir' is crucial—'explicit, continuously transmitted text.'
2
Identify the two types of nass discussed
The author mentions explicit text (nass jali) for Ali, but for later Imams he says texts are 'known' without consideration of explicitness. Also note the Zaydi view that the text about Ali is 'concealed' (khafiyy).
This distinction undermines any claim of uniform explicit texts for all Twelve.
3
Examine the list of Shia sects
The author enumerates sects from Zaydiyya, Kaysaniyya, Imamiyya, Ghulat, and Batiniyya with their counts. This illustrates the immense diversity within Shia itself.
The numbers (10, 12, 34, 8, 8-9) add up to more than 70, mirroring the hadith about 73 sects.
4
Reflect on the author's skeptical stance
The author notes that most of these differences are from unreliable books and that he merely transmits a treatise. This shows he is not endorsing the details but using them as evidence of lack of clarity.
The final comment about some sects being outside Islam emphasizes the extreme diversity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هذا الاختلاف العظيم من أدل الدلائل على عدم النص الجلى المتواتر على مؤلاء الأثني عشر . أقول : هذه اختلافات رويت عن الشيعة القائلين بإمامة على كرم الله وجهه وأكثرها مما لم يوجد له أثر غير المكتوب فى كتب غير معتمد عليها والنص الجملى لا يقولون به في غير على رضى الله عنه ، فإن النص من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم على على رضى الله عنه كان جليا في مثل قوله « من كنت مولاه فعلى مولاه وعند الزيدية كان خفيا لأنه محتاج إلى ضم مقدمات إليه يدل الجميع على إمامته ، والنصوص من كل إمام من الاثنى عشر على من بعده عندهم معلوم ولا يعتبر الجلاء ولا الخفاء فيها ولا كلام على ما في هذا النقل، لأنه نقل مجرد نقله من الكتب ، وقد رأيت رسالة لبعض الزنجيين من قدماء الشيعة أنه ذكر فيه أن المشهور أن الأمة تفترق سبعا وسبعين فرقة والشيعة قد افترقوا هذا القدر فضلا عن غيرهم ، فذكر من الزيدية عشر فرق ، ومن الكيسانية اثنتى عشرة فرقة ومن الإمامية أربعا وثلاثين فرقة ومن الغلاة ثمانى فرق ومن الباطنية ثمان تسع فرق لكن بعض هذه الفرق خارجون عن الاسلام كالغلاة وبعض الباطنية - والله أعلم محقيقة الحال .
Know that this great difference is among the strongest evidences for the absence of explicit, continuous report (nass jali mutawatir) concerning these Twelve [Imams]. I say: These are differences reported from the Shia who affirm the Imamate of Ali (may Allah honor his face), and most of them have no trace except what is written in unreliable books. They do not affirm explicit text (nass jil) for other than Ali (may Allah be pleased with him). For the text from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) concerning Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) was explicit in his saying 'Whoever has me as his master, Ali is his master.' Among the Zaydis it was concealed because it requires the combination of premises to indicate his Imamate. The texts from each Imam of the Twelve concerning who comes after them are known among them, and explicitness or concealment is not considered therein. There is no comment on what is in this transmission, for it is a mere transmission from books. I have seen a treatise by one of the ancients from the Shia that he mentioned therein that it is well-known that the nation will split into seventy-three sects, and the Shia have split into this many, let alone others. He mentioned from the Zaydiyya ten sects, from the Kaysaniyya twelve sects, from the Imamiyya thirty-four sects, from the Ghulat eight sects, and from the Batiniyya eight or nine sects. But some of these sects are outside Islam, like the Ghulat and some of the Batiniyya. And Allah knows the reality of the situation.
Source pages p. 246

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary by the author on the extensive differences among Shia sects regarding the Imamate. It begins by stating that the great disagreement itself is one of the clearest proofs that there is no explicit, mass-transmitted text (nass jali mutawatir) designating each of the Twelve Imams. The author then clarifies that these differences are mostly based on unreliable sources. He distinguishes the status of the text about Ali (the hadith 'Man kuntu mawlahu') which is explicit among some but concealed among Zaydis. For the subsequent Imams, according to Shia, the texts are known but do not require explicitness. The author then cites a treatise from an early Shia scholar listing the numerous sects within Shiism, demonstrating that internal divisions are immense, with some sects even outside Islam. The overall point is to undermine the claim of a clear, continuous textual tradition supporting the Twelve Imams.

النص الجلى المتواتر
النص الجملى
الزيدية
الكيسانية
الإمامية
الغلاة

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does the great disagreement among Shia sects regarding the Imamate prove?
A) The validity of the Twelve Imams
B) The absence of an explicit continuous text on the Twelve Imams
C) The superiority of the Zaydi view
D) The need for independent reasoning (ijtihad)

Key Takeaway
Internal Shia divisions on the Imamate demonstrate that there cannot be a clear, mass-transmitted text naming each of the Twelve Imams.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about sectarian divisions among Muslims, do you feel a sense of superiority over those who disagree, or does it drive you to examine the foundations of your own beliefs with the same critical eye al-Razi applies to the Shia?
?
Al-Razi uses internal disagreement as a proof of absence of clear divine text. Can you think of a belief you hold that, if it were truly from God, would not produce such division? Why do you cling to it despite disagreements?
?
If your own understanding of Islam were subjected to the same scrutiny as al-Razi’s analysis of Shia claims, would the evidence for it hold up? What would you do if you found it was based on unreliable sources, like the books al-Razi dismisses?
